WSJ Pro
VC Daily: What's an Inflection Fund?; Next-Gen Social Startups

1,549 words
20 February 2019
13:37
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Good day. You've heard of a growth fund, an opportunity fund, and a global growth fund. Now Menlo Ventures is trying to invent a new type of fund with its "inflection fund," which it describes as an early growth fund. With SoftBank Group and others making massive late-stage deals, Menlo sees an opening to bet earlier on growth-stage companies.

And now on to the news...

Today's Top Stories

Inflection funding: Menlo Ventures has closed on $500 million for a new early growth fund, with the intention of backing companies at the crucial, risky point before large-scale growth. The fund is a jump from the firm's last opportunity growth fund, which closed at $250 million in 2016. Menlo also has a $450 million early-stage fund which it closed in 2017. Menlo is looking for companies at the "inflection point," which it defined in a blog post as the "ambiguous middle ground where data, execution and risks are all open to interpretation." WSJ Pro's Heather Mack has more.

AI for genetics: With genetics research marching forward, keeping up with all the new discoveries in the field can be a challenge for geneticists. Investors are betting Emedgene has a solution. The startup has raised $6 million to expand sales of artificial intelligence-based software that automates genetic interpretation. Geneticists use this decision-support system to help ensure they don't miss important pieces of evidence in a genetic test that could help them diagnose a patient, according to co-founder and Chief Executive Einat Metzer. WSJ Pro's Brian Gormley has more.

Opening up IPOs: Any company exploring whether to go public would get greater leeway to discuss their plans privately with potential investors before announcing an initial public offering, under a proposal that securities regulators released Tuesday. In a bid to boost the number of public companies, the Securities and Exchange Commission proposed letting all companies "test the waters" before deciding whether to seek an IPO. The WSJ's Gabriel T. Rubin has the story.

Roof's Take: Social Media's Next Generation Looks to Niche Plays

By Katie Roof

Investors are looking for the next big social-media hit.

So far, the next wave of social companies hasn't reached massive scale, leaving a question of where the next Facebook or Snapchat will emerge. With few recent breakout hits, social-media funding is down, as is the number of such startups reaching a billion-dollar valuation.

Just 48 social-media businesses received $112.4 million in investment last year, according to PitchBook Data, the lowest amount of money invested in the category since 2005. The number of deals peaked at 152 in 2012.

Still, content-sharing site Pinterest Inc. is preparing for an initial public offering this year, and discussion forum Reddit Inc. just raised funding at a $3 billion valuation but both are more than a decade old.

Investors remain optimistic about chances for the next wave of social-media startups just more broadly defined.

More in Roof's Take

Exits

Palo Alto Networks agreed to acquire Demisto Inc., a provider of collaboration and automation tools to security professionals, for $560 million in cash and stock. The Cupertino, Calif.-based startup was backed by Accel and Slack Fund.

Driver-safety technology provider Newton Technologies was acquired by autonomous-vehicle startup Nauto Inc. for an undisclosed amount. Newton Technologies was backed by Pear Ventures. Palo Alto, Calif.- based Nauto raised $159 million in Series B financing in 2017 led by SoftBank Group and Greylock Partners, with participation from investors including BMW i Ventures, General Motors Ventures and Toyota AI Ventures.

Instructure Inc. agreed to purchase student-talent marketplace Portfolium Inc. for an undisclosed amount. Portfolium is backed by SJF Ventures and Vertical Venture Partners.

Publicly traded GTY Technology Holdings Inc. merged with sourcing and procurement software provider Bonfire Interactive Ltd. Terms of the deal weren't disclosed. The Canadian startup was backed by investors including Battery Ventures, Crosslink Capital and Spider Capital.

GN Audio agreed to acquire Altia Systems Inc., a provider of video communications services utilizing digital multicamera array technology. The Cupertino, Calif.-based company was backed by Intel Capital.

Kibo, a cloud commerce provider backed by private-equity firm Vista Equity Partners, acquired Certona Corp., the developer of a personalized targeting tool for B2B marketing. Terms of the deal weren't disclosed. Certona, with offices in San Diego and London, was backed by Primus Capital, ABS Ventures and Express Ventures.

New Money

Green Hills Software, a Santa Barbara, Calif.-based embedded safety and security provider, landed a $150 investment from publicly traded Cadence Design Systems Inc. through a strategic partnership. Cadence Chief Executive Lip-Bu Tan joined the company's board. Founded in 1982, Green Hills also is backed by Arcturus Capital. "https://www.ghs.com"

GoCardless Ltd., a London-based payments startup, snagged $75 million in Series E funding. Adams Street Partners, GV and Salesforce Ventures led the round, which included participation from Accel and Balderton Capital. "https://gocardless.com"

Redis Labs Inc., a Mountain View, Calif., database startup, raised $60 million in Series E funding. Francisco Partners led the round, with participation from investors including Goldman Sachs Private Capital Investing, Bain Capital Ventures, Viola Ventures and Dell Technologies Capital. David Golob, chief investment officer of Francisco Partners, will join the company's board. "https://redislabs.com"

SendBird, a San Mateo, Calif.-based mobile and web messaging service provider, collected $52 million in a Series B round led by Iconiq Capital. Existing investors, including Shasta Ventures and Y Combinator, participated in the round. Matthew Jacobson, a general partner at Iconiq, joined the board. "https://sendbird.com"

Nasuni, a Boston-based provider of cloud-scale enterprise file services, grabbed $25 million in growth-equity funding. Telstra Ventures led the round, with participation from Goldman Sachs, Sigma Prime Ventures, North Bridge Venture Partners and Flybridge Capital Partners. "https://www.nasuni.com"

CipherTrace Inc., a Menlo Park, Calif.-based blockchain security startup, raised a $15 million funding round. Aspect Ventures led the investment, which included participation from Galaxy Digital, Neotribe Ventures and WestWave Capital. Aspect's Mark Kraynak will join the company's board. "https://ciphertrace.com"

nsKnox, a corporate cybersecurity firm based in Boston and Tel Aviv, raised a $15 million Series A round led by Viola Ventures and M12, Microsoft's venture fund. "https://nsknox.net"

Loom, a work communication tool, secured nearly $11 million in Series A funding. Kleiner Perkins led the round, with Partner Ilya Fushman joining the board. "https://www.useloom.com"

Vincross, a Beijing-based consumer robotics startup, secured a $10 million Series A+ funding round. Lenovo led the investment, which included participation from GGV Capital. "https://www.vincross.com"

Teatime, a Reykjavik-based mobile-gaming startup, launched with $9 million in Series A funding from investors including Index Ventures and Atomico. "https://www.teatime.games"

Covera Health, a New York-based startup aiming to reduce medical misdiagnoses and improve quality of care, completed an $8.5 million Series A round led by Equity Group Investments. "https://www.coverahealth.com"

Pointivo, an Atlanta-based startup that captures 3-D models and measurements of as-built environments using a smartphone camera, scored $7 million in Series A funding led by BIP Capital. "http://pointivo.com"

K2 Cyber Security Inc., a San Jose, Calif.-based startup that offers cyber-attack defense, raised $6 million in seed funding from GreatPoint Ventures and Embark Ventures. "https://k2io.com"

Nuweba, a Tel Aviv-based serverless technology startup, emerged from stealth with $4.8 million in seed funding led by Magma Partners and Target Global. "https://www.nuweba.com"

NormShield, a Vienna, Va.-based provider of on-demand cyber risk scorecards for businesses, was seeded with a $3.5 million investment. Glasswing Ventures led the round with participation from Data Point Capital. Rick Grinnell and Mike Majors, managing partners at Glasswing and Data Point, respectively, joined the company's board. "https://www.normshield.com"

HiHello Inc., a Palo Alto, Calif.-based contact management app, picked up $2.5 million in seed funding led by August Capital, K9 Ventures and TenOneTen Ventures. Villi Iltchev, a partner at August, joined the board. "https://hihello.me"

Industry News

Customers are hunting for cash from bankrupt crypto exchange QuadrigaCX, after the firm's chief executive died late last year. Evidence is mounting that at least some of the money is missing. Read more.

Pinterest Inc. has stopped returning search results for terms relating to vaccinations, a drastic step aimed at curbing the spread of misinformation but one that also reflects the challenge facing social-media companies in monitoring hot-button health issues. Read more.

Fintech startup SoFi is offering brokerage services to the public. Read more.

Crypto Customers Hunt for Missing Cash

By Paul Vigna and Shane Shifflett 
887 words
20 February 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B6
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

On a rainy January day, Jason Campbell drove for three hours from his home in Quebec City to a two-story office building in the Montreal suburb of Laval. He thought he would be picking up $1,000 in cash he had stored at cryptocurrency exchange QuadrigaCX.

But the door of a suite on the building's second floor was locked and the office wasn't occupied. Mr. Campbell dialed the number he had been given. A man answered, refused to give his name, and said he had no money for Mr. Campbell.

The suite was the teller window in an unusual system Quadriga employed to allow customers to withdraw cash from the exchange rather than wire it to their banks. It all fell apart when the exchange filed for bankruptcy in Canada last month and claimed that $136 million of customer funds were trapped in an electronic vault rendered inaccessible by the death of the firm's chief executive.

Mr. Campbell made two fruitless trips to Laval. "I'm still hoping they'll give me a call back one day," he said.

The cash-pickup system is the latest unusual business practice at Quadriga to emerge since Gerald Cotten, the firm's 30-year-old co-founder and CEO, died in India late last year. An examination of public transaction records by independent researchers suggests Quadriga's missing funds may be gone, not stuck, The Wall Street Journal previously reported.

Mr. Cotten's widow, Jennifer Robertson, is now running the company. She didn't respond to a request for comment. A company bankruptcy lawyer declined to comment.

The cash option was no secret. The exchange touted it on its website and talked about it on social media. It is unclear how much cash was funneled through the exchange in this manner. Ernst & Young, the company's bankruptcy monitor, has said Quadriga kept no accounting records.

Tara Smith, another Quadriga customer, said her husband drove eight hours to the Laval office last month to collect $1,200, only to find it closed. "Our cash went to something, whether it's an orphanage or somebody's new car," she said.

On Jan. 15, Quadriga announced on its website that Mr. Cotten had died Dec. 9 from complications related to Crohn's disease while building an orphanage in India. The company filed for bankruptcy in Nova Scotia two weeks later.

In court papers, the company claimed it owed customers about C$250 million ($188.9 million), including C$180 million in cryptocurrency, spread across more than 100,000 accounts. Ms. Robertson has said the cryptocurrency is trapped in offline accounts she can't access because only Mr. Cotten had the required codes.

But evidence is mounting that at least some of the money is missing. In particular, between $10.1 million and $24.7 million worth of the cryptocurrency ether left the online accounts of Quadriga and moved through an exchange often associated with money launderers, according to a Wall Street Journal analysis of publicly available transaction data.

Quadriga claimed it employed a "cold storage" system for its customers' assets, holding digital currencies like bitcoin and ether in offline vaults guarded by "the most advanced security measures in the bitcoin industry."

Yet nearly all of the 1.4 million ether in accounts seemingly controlled by Quadriga and examined by the Journal was routed to other online wallets and exchanges. At least 268,000 ether went to ShapeShift AG, a digital-asset exchange operating from Denver. Suspected criminals have used ShapeShift to launder millions of dollars, the Journal reported last year.

"It's pretty clear there are no cold wallets holding ether," said Max Galka, the chief executive of cryptocurrency analytics firm Elementus Group.

Most cryptocurrency exchanges operating in the U.S. say they follow federal rules to combat money laundering. ShapeShift didn't do so until October 2018, when it began requiring identification. Until then, users could transact anonymously.

"The customer transactions between ShapeShift and QuadrigaCX are similar to those experienced by other exchanges and would not have appeared unusual at the time of the transactions," said Veronica McGregor, ShapeShift's chief legal officer. "Although we have yet to be contacted by law enforcement, we stand ready to help in any way that we can."

Wallets associated with Quadriga's publicly identifiable accounts sent ether to ShapeShift that was converted into bitcoin and other currencies. It was later funneled to other accounts and exchanges through multiple digital wallets.

Cryptocurrency analysis firm Bloxy also tracked Quadriga's funds to ShapeShift. Quadriga's use of ShapeShift, which charges higher fees than other exchanges, should have been a red flag, said Bloxy founder Dean Karakitsos.

Quadriga's money problems may have begun with one incident just as the crypto market was heating up. In June 2017, the exchange announced that a software upgrade inadvertently sent 67,000 ether it was holding for customers to a wallet it couldn't access. The lost ether were worth $14 million at the time of the software upgrade. By the end of 2017, they would be worth $90 million.

At the time, Quadriga said it would credit customers for the missing ether and absorb the loss.

Investors Warn Against Complacency on Fed Pause; Mester Sees Rates Slightly Higher This Year; The Wall Street Journal's central banking newsletter for Wednesday, February 20, 2019

3,430 words
20 February 2019
12:05
WSJ Pro Central Banking
RSTPROCB
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Good day. Is the Federal Reserve done raising rates, or merely on a hiatus? Stocks and bonds are rising on bets that rates won't go any higher, but some investors are concerned the Fed could upend those expectations. Money managers will get another look at the Fed's view on interest rates and the economy later Wednesday, when the central bank is scheduled to release minutes from its January meeting. And Cleveland Fed leader Loretta Mester said the central bank's next rate move was likely "a little bit later this year."

Fed's Loretta Mester Sees Interest Rates Slightly Higher This Year

The president of the Federal Reserve Bank of Cleveland said Tuesday that interest rates "https://www.wsj.com/articles/feds-loretta-mester-sees-interest-rates-slightly-higher-this-year-11550586378" slightly this year, assuming the economy grows at the healthy clip she anticipates. Stocks and bonds are rising on bets that the Federal Reserve has ended its nearly four-year campaign of interest-rate increases, worrying investors who believe the central bank could upend those expectations later this year.Ms. Mester, who is not a voting member of the rate-setting Federal Open Market Committee, said she estimates a "neutral" rate that neither stimulates nor spurs economic growth to be around 3%. The fed-funds rate is currently set in a range between 2.25% and 2.5%.

How Long Will Fed's Rate Pause Last? Minutes Could Yield Clues

The Federal Reserve releases the minutes of its Jan. 29-30 meeting on Wednesday at 2 p.m. EST, providing more detail about officials' discussions before they formally signaled a halt to interest rate rises. Last month, officials also underscored their more-flexible posture by providing important updates about their policy for shrinking their $4 trillion asset portfolio. "https://www.wsj.com/articles/how-long-will-feds-rate-pause-last-minutes-could-yield-clues-11550658600?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=2"

Investors Sound Warning About Markets' Complacency on Interest Rates

Stocks and bonds are rising on bets that the Federal Reserve has ended its nearly four-year campaign of interest-rate increases, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/investors-sound-warning-about-markets-complacency-on-interest-rates-11550588400" who believe the central bank could upend those expectations later this year. Much of the disagreement over the Fed's next rate move stems from its decision in January to remove explicit reference to future rate increases from its monetary-policy statement. Before the January meeting, the central bank had included that language in all of its statements since 2015.

Markets Warm to the Prospect of an ECB Funding Boost for Banks

Market participants are "https://www.wsj.com/articles/markets-warm-to-the-prospect-of-an-ecb-funding-boost-for-banks-11550656802" that the European Central Bank will soon try to boost the eurozone's ailing economy by rebooting its program of ultracheap long-term loans to the banking system.

Europe Again Champions Intervention to Counter China's Economic Might

European leaders are "https://www.wsj.com/articles/europe-again-champions-intervention-to-counter-chinas-economic-might-11550572200" of giving the market's invisible hand a helping hand in an effort to counter China's state-backed capitalism. Government intervention to help build corporate champions was Europe's standard for the postwar decades but faded amid privatizations and deregulation starting in the 1980s. Now, Europe's struggle to keep up with China's government coordination of business and the economic might of the U.S. has prompted more European politicians to advocate interventionism.

Asia's Most Radical Left-Wing Economic Program Faces Harsh Reality

South Korean President Moon Jae-in's flagship economic policy, "income-led growth," has led to dramatic increases in minimum wages since he took office in 2017. Those increases are "https://www.wsj.com/articles/asias-most-radical-left-wing-economic-program-faces-a-harsh-reality-11550652596?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=1" a rise in unemployment, just as a slump in global trade is already buffeting Korea.

SEC Wants to Make It Easier for Companies to Explore IPOs

By Gabriel T. Rubin

WASHINGTON Any company exploring whether to go public "https://www.wsj.com/articles/sec-wants-to-make-it-easier-for-companies-to-explore-ipos-11550608078" to discuss their plans privately with potential investors before announcing an initial public offering, under a proposal that securities regulators released Tuesday.

In a bid to boost the number of public companies, the Securities and Exchange Commission proposed letting all companies "test the waters" before deciding whether to seek an IPO. The agency had previously allowed only smaller, emerging companies to talk to investors privately.

Making it easier and more appealing for companies to go public has been a central goal of SEC Chairman Jay Clayton. The number of public companies has fallen by nearly 50% since the late 1990s. Several startups valued at above $1 billion, including Uber Technologies Inc. and Airbnb Inc., have held off on going public, though there are signs that some of those companies might make the move in 2019.

"Extending the test-the-waters reform to a broader range of issuers is designed to enhance their ability to conduct successful public securities offerings and lower their cost of capital, and ultimately to provide investors with more opportunities," Mr. Clayton said in a written statement.

Under Tuesday's proposal, firms would be able to discuss securities offerings with sophisticated investors, including institutional firms such as asset managers and other accredited investors. The conversations would allow companies to identify information that is important to investors and to gauge market interest before formally announcing a public offering.

Expanding the testing-the-waters rules is the latest in a series of efforts to boost public offerings. The move follows an action that let all companies file IPO paperwork confidentially with the SEC, which allows firms to keep their financial data secret if they decide not to go public.

The SEC also is considering changes to the shareholder proposal system, which some companies complain makes going public less appealing. The SEC is considering changing the minimum stake an investor must hold in a company to be able to submit a shareholder resolution, as well as possible modifications to how much support a proposal must receive each year in order to remain on an annual shareholder meeting proxy ballot.

The Wall Street Journal first reported that the SEC was planning changes to testing the waters last year.

Danske Bank to Shut Estonia Branch

By Samuel Rubenfeld in New York and Dominic Chopping in Stockholm

Danske Bank A/S is "https://www.wsj.com/articles/danske-bank-to-shut-estonia-branch-11550595242" in Estonia as the fallout continues from a massive money-laundering scandal that has led to investigations in several countries.

The bank said Tuesday that it was told to close down its operations in the country by Estonian regulators.

Danske Bank is under investigation in Estonia, Denmark, the U.S., U.K. and France for allegedly facilitating the laundering of about $230 billion by non-Estonians through its branch in Tallinn, Estonia, primarily by Russians, between 2007 and 2015.

Estonian regulators told Danske Bank to close the Tallinn branch within eight months and return, in full, the deposits of its customers. It has to transfer or resell all customer agreements within that time frame, or face a fine of  ¬100,000, or about $113,000, a day.

"We are sorry to be leaving Estonia against this background," said interim Danske Bank Chief Executive Jesper Nielsen, in a statement. "We understand the severity with which the Estonian [regulator] looks at this case, and we will close down our remaining activities as requested."

"We will continue our cooperation with the Estonian and other relevant authorities," Mr. Nielsen said.

Danske Bank violated antimoney-laundering rules for years by allowing high-risk clients to make suspicious transactions through the bank, and it misled Estonian authorities by providing inadequate information, the regulator said.

The bank had already closed the nonresident business line in Estonia. The scandal has led to the departure of a number of top executives. In September, Danske Bank released an investigation into the matter by a Danish law firm; its then-CEO stepped down when announcing the probe's findings.

Danske Bank said it is also closing its activities in Latvia, Lithuania and Russia independent of the Estonian order, shifting to focus on its core Nordic assets. A shared services center in Lithuania that undertakes a number of Danske administrative functions will continue operations, the bank said.

"The decision to close down our activities completely in the Baltics and in Russia is fully in keeping with this strategy," Mr. Nielsen said.

The decision won't have any bearing on the bank's 2019 guidance, it said.

Also Tuesday, the European Banking Authority, which oversees the European member-state national antimoney-laundering regulators, said it opened a formal probe into Estonian and Danish regulators over how they supervised Danske Bank's activity. The decision follows a request from the European Commission in September 2018 to use its authority to examine whether there were failures by the regulators to comply with their bloc obligations, it said.

Global Witness, an anticorruption group, welcomed the European Banking Authority's announcement, saying it's a positive step toward rebuilding the bloc's ability to fight corruption.

"The sheer scale of the money-laundering scandal at Dankse Bank in Estonia has shaken Europe to its core," said Nienke Palstra, an anticorruption campaigner at Global Witness. "We must see Danish and Estonian authorities, Danske Bank and senior bankers held to account to make sure this never happens again."

McKinsey Settles Alleged Bankruptcy Abuses

By Gretchen Morgenson and Tom Corrigan

McKinsey & Co. agreed to pay $15 million to "https://www.wsj.com/articles/mckinsey-u-s-settle-alleged-bankruptcy-abuses-11550605039?mod=hp_lista_pos2" that the large consulting firm failed to make required disclosures of potential conflicts in three chapter 11 cases it had advised on in recent years.

The settlement was struck between McKinsey and the U.S. Trustee Program, which is charged with protecting the integrity of the nation's bankruptcy system. The fine represents "one of the highest repayments made by a bankruptcy professional for alleged non-compliance with disclosure rules," according to the Justice Department.

"McKinsey failed to satisfy its obligations under bankruptcy law and demonstrated a lack of candor with the court" and U.S. Trustee said Cliff White, director of the Trustee Program, in a statement. "This settlement ensures that McKinsey is held to the same standards applicable to all professionals who participate in bankruptcy cases."

Mr. White added that if McKinsey repeats its problematic disclosure practices, his office "will seek even more far-reaching remedies."

McKinsey didn't have an immediate comment. In the past, McKinsey has maintained that its disclosures are appropriate and comply with the law.

McKinsey's undisclosed conflicts in bankruptcy cases it had worked on were the subject of a 2018 investigation by The Wall Street Journal. In seven of the 14 chapter 11 cases in which McKinsey acted as an adviser to the debtors, the Journal found, the firm had a financial interest in the outcome.

The Journal also found that McKinsey named far fewer connections in each bankruptcy case it worked on than other advisers did.

The chapter 11 process requires that advisers working on a case be disinterested parties and they are expected to disclose any potential conflicts so that other participants are aware of them.

Last month, the judge who had overseen a 2015 bankruptcy of coal-mining company Alpha Natural Resources decided to reopen that case to determine whether McKinsey's investment in a hedge fund that received Alpha's most valuable assets had tainted the bankruptcy process.

The judge said McKinsey never disclosed the investment to him even though he gave McKinsey an opportunity to disclose its connections privately in his chambers.

Worrying About Deficit Is Going Out of Style

WSJ Pro
Menlo Ventures Closes $500 Million Fund to Back Startups at  Inflection Point'; New fund is dedicated to companies at the gap between early stage financing and mega-growth rounds

By Heather Mack 
291 words
20 February 2019
12:00
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Menlo Ventures has closed $500 million for a new early growth fund, with the intention of backing companies at the crucial, risky point before large-scale growth.

The fund is a jump from the firm's last opportunity growth fund which closed at $250 million in 2016. Menlo also has a $450 million early stage fund which it closed in 2017.

Menlo is looking for companies at the "inflection point," which it defines in a blog post as the "ambiguous middle ground where data, execution and risks are all open to interpretation."

At a time when there are hundreds of seed funds, and late-stage growth funds are larger than ever, the Menlo Park, Calif.-based firm raised the Inflection Fund to back companies in the "gap" between early stage venture capital and large-scale growth investing. These companies can be existing Menlo portfolio companies or new investments.

Companies at this stage, Menlo wrote in a blog post, could have many promising attributes, such as a beloved product and revenue, but still carry significant risks. The firm said it has developed the capabilities to spot companies at this key juncture based on past investments at this stage in companies such as Uber Technologies Inc., Poshmark Inc. and PillPack Inc.

Menlo plans to make $20 million to $40 million investments across industries including mobility, artificial intelligence and cloud infrastructure. The fund will be led by partners Matt Murphy, Mark Siegel, Shawn Carolan, Venky Ganesan, Tyler Sosin and principal Steve Sloane.

Features
Morgan Stanley taps 'Calgary's best-kept secret'

ROBERT ARMSTRONG 
993 words
19 February 2019
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa
15
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Bank sets sights on gains from Solium Capital deal amid intense competition in wealth management sector

Morgan Stanley's bet on Solium Capital may not be big   the $900m acquisition, announced this week, amounts to a small slice of the $8.7bn that the bank earned in 2018   but it is bold.

It is a wager that technology sold to employers can give the bank an edge in the vicious competition for wealth management clients.

Whether or not Morgan Stanley turns out to be right, it is a triumph long in coming for Solium, its chief executive Marcos Lopez, and his team. They built Solium starting at the turn of the millennium in the oil and gas hub of Calgary, rather than Toronto or Vancouver, and as recently as two years ago the local newspaper was calling the business "Calgary's best-kept secret".

In its scrappy, early days, the company would let one client   the local craft brewer Big Rock   pay its bill in beer. Now Solium counts Heineken as a client, along with others including private tech companies such as Instacart, Uber and SpaceX, whose employees Morgan Stanley is keen to court.

Other financial institutions have paid up to acquire tech groups, but Solium is not a fast-growing consumer application in peer-to-peer payments or financial advice. Its revenues are running at just over $100m annually and it is modestly profitable, selling software for "equity administration, financial reporting and compliance".

It is the kind of business familiar to millions of workers who get part of their compensation in stock or stock options. Solium and its rivals, including leaders such as Computershare of Australia, produce statements and run websites or apps for workers to track how much their shares are worth.

Behind that, the software helps employers manage and monitor their stock-compensation plan, a complex back-office task that involves unpicking regulatory and reporting requirements across multiple jurisdictions. Solium also sells analytics tools that help companies automate share issuance, estimate compensation costs, optimise their capital structures, and value their equity.

One industry insider describes it as "administratively highly complex, capital-light, but very, very low-margin". It is also competitive. Every big US broker or wealth manager, from UBS to ETrade, plays in the space in one way or another, because acting as custodian for employee equity plans gives them a shot at marketing wealth management services to those employees. Someone cashing in stock or options, perhaps when an IPO or a sale makes them wealthy, could be offered financial advice and investments; longtime employees could be offered retirement products.

Such lead generation was the idea behind the creation of Solium. In 1999, it was founded by two Calgary financial advisers, John Kenny and Mark Van Hees, who left the business a few years later. Mr Lopez, barely out of college, joined the company's tech team and was chief technology officer before taking the CEO job in 2015.

Solium's shares went public at C$1 in 2001, and its was trading under C$2 as recently as 2012. Morgan Stanley paid C$19. Directors between them owned close to 20 per cent of the company before the Morgan Stanley acquisition; Mr Lopez, 42, holds a stake worth $30m, according to Bloomberg data.

Mr Lopez said Solium's edge came from approaching the task from the employers' perspective, rather than as a tool for wealth managers.

Equity administration "is an enterprise software problem that has been approached as a wealth management problem". Far from inhibiting success, being based in a smaller regional city was what made it possible for the group to survive and succeed, Mr Lopez said. Calgary was big enough to have plenty of corporate HQs, but small enough that personal relationships mattered.

"It was far enough away from the financial centres that people will take a chance on you," he said. "It was the perfect little Petri dish for us to grow."

Morgan Stanley said it would keep the business's headquarters in Calgary.

Solium has 1m equity plan participants versus Morgan Stanley's 1.5m, but a draw for Morgan Stanley is the difference between the groups: its clients are overwhelmingly public companies, but Solium's include tech groups where young employees stand a good chance of becoming wealthy down the road.

The relationships with Silicon Valley companies could prove lucrative, too, if Morgan Stanley can bag them as clients of its investment bank.

Jed Finn, chief operating officer of Morgan Stanley's wealth management business, said owning Solium allowed it to "offer our full suite of wealth management tools and advice to plan employees through Solium's platform". Steven Chubak, banking analyst at Wolfe Research, sees the acquisition as part of the US bank's effort to move its wealth management business deeper into the mass affluent category, as opposed to the higher end of the market where it is focused now.

"Looking at some of the players [in equity administration] such as ETrade, they have had a great deal of success converting these equity plan clients into [brokerage] clients," he said.

Goldman Sachs was making a similar grab for the mass affluent with its Ayco business, which provides financial coaching to employees through corporate partnerships, Mr Chubak said. Morgan Stanley's wealth management arm generated 40 per cent of the company's $11bn in pre-tax income last year, and has been central to chief executive James Gorman's push to reduce the cyclicality of the bank's earnings.

There was a hint of the Solium deal in Mr Gorman's remarks during the last quarterly earnings call. "We plan to further enhance our digital tools, as we expand and leverage our stock plan channel to convert more leads into new clients," he said. "We've long discussed the relative stability of [the wealth management] revenue base. Scale advantages only improve this story."

Banks  blockchain comedown

Laura Noonan

Your essential weekly fintech briefing

Ahblockchain. Once seen as the panacea for all that ails the financial services industry, expectations for the distributed ledger technology have been falling almost as fast as the price of the bitcoin cryptocurrency it underpins (down 82per cent from its high at the last count, in case anyone is still counting).

Citigroup boss Mike Corbat, who oversees one of the world s biggest banks and its $8bn a year technology budget, has been following the saga more closely than most and now believes that blockchain will not be  transformational  in the short term.

 I think in many of these things, the expectations and the pace of implementation far exceed 	. 	. 	. 	what s there,  he told the FT in a wide-ranging interview, where he also predicted the widespread replacement of call centre operatives by artificial intelligence.

Mr Corbat likened blockchain s evolution to  an old analogy; if you invent the fax machine and you ve got the only one, it s pretty lonely for a while, but eventually others [join]. What you re doing with blockchain and the distributed ledger, you need other players involved. And I think the industry [is] moving at different speeds. 

Or as the head of another investment bank puts it more bluntly:  The expectations at the beginning were f***ing ridiculous. 

That s not to say that bankers are giving up altogether on a technology that the financial services industry is collectively ploughing$1.7bn a yearinto, according to research from Greenwich Associates.

Mr Corbat said he has  high expectations  for the technology  in the intermediate to long term .

He called out trade finance  a multibillion dollar a year revenue line for Citi   as somewhere blockchain has serious potential since it s  one of those areas where it s still notebooks and pens and paper .

 Anything where you re trying to create a record of ownership   mortgage ownership, liens in lending, where you can go back and create a chain of ownership, it [blockchain]is very impactful,  Mr Corbat added. Another senior bank executive said blockchain has  great potential  over the longer term thanks to the need for  technology to ensure integrity (of transactions) in an inexpensive manner .

JPMorgan Chase has been fronting the industry s single largest blockchain initiative, the Interbank Information Network, where more than 100 banks are now putting information about certain cross-border transactions on to a mutually accessible ledger to cut down on delays when payments hit compliance hitches. 

Spain s BBVA has also been an active blockchain experimenter.It paired with two other banks in November to do the first ever syndicated loan on blockchain, andearlier in the year the Spanish bank issued the world s first corporate loan on blockchain.

Just last week, JPMorgan debuted their latest blockchain wheeze  the JPM Coin, whichcan be transferred almost instantly over blockchain between the banks  corporate clients, who can then convert them to dollars. The coin is known as a  stablecoin  since one JPM Coin will always equal one dollar.JPMorgan is starting small with a prototype involving a few clients and will roll it out more widely, if it goes well.

JPMorgan says the technology will help clients to do business across different time zones outside of traditionally  cut offs  and that the same technology could ultimately be used for other applications.

One to watch: FloodFlash

The claims process can be one of the most frustrating parts of insurance, often involving delays, suspicion and finally irritation when the ultimatepayment turns out to be less than you want or expect.

Parametric insurance could change all that,writes the FT's insurance correspondentOliver Ralph.Behind the nasty jargon is a straightforward concept   this is insurance that pays out as soon as an event happens, rather than after the scale of the loss has been calculated. It's often used for the sort of large scale insurance bought by governments. When there are strong stormsin the Caribbean, for example, island governments receive their money immediately.

UK-basedFloodFlashis one of the first companies to apply the principle to commercial insurance, selling flood coverage to small businesses. The company installs sensors on the outside walls of its customers  premises. If the sensor registers a flood, then a pre-agreed amount is paid immediately. No need to guess how much damage the water has actually caused, or to spend money on claims adjusters. Just the presence of the flood triggers the payment.

 Working out the likelihood of 50cm of water is one thing,  said co-founder Adam Rimmer.  Working out how 50cm of water turns into damage is a totally different problem and that s what creates uncertainty. 

Some insurers don t like the concept   they say that insurance payments should be based on actual losses. But Mr Rimmer sees scope to expand the concept to other countries. He says floods globally cause $50bn of damage a year, and only $9.5bn of that is covered by insurance.

FloodFlash soft launched its product in the UK earlier this year, and is targeting businesses in flood prone areas.  We re trying to protect people by rivers and coasts,  said Mr Rimmer.  If you are in a high risk area you could struggle to find [traditional] cover. 

Number of the week: $3.3bn

Amount invested in UK fintechs last year, according to Innovate Finance, up 18 per cent on 2017 despite uncertainties around Brexit.

Further fintech fascination

Regulators' advance:The Financial Stability Board (FSB)  a who's who of regulators from the powerful G20 countries  has become the latest watchdog to sound the alarm about Big Tech's potential impact on financial stability. The FSB warned last week that tech firms could  materially alter  financial services, creating stability risks as incumbents take chancesto keep up. The European Banking Authority and the Bank of England have separately raised similar concerns, butno one is stepping up to actually regulate the firms that offer financial services on the side. The FSB's policy recommendation is for continued monitoring of Big Tech's impact on the sector at large 	. 	. 	. 	

Trend watch:Spain's CaixaBank   less well-known for its fintech prowess than bigger rivals BBVA and Santander   has nonetheless become the first bank in Europe to unveil facial recognition at cashpoints. Caixa announced last week that its ATMs would now scan clients' faces instead of asking for their personal identification numbers, claiming the technology as a win for both  security and agility . Banks in Asia have been experimenting with facial identification for a few years now, including using it as a second layer of security in Macauand to detect fraudulent borrowers in China. Other forms of biometrics   particularly fingerprint ID   are also big on the fintech circuit and have been unveiled in afew marketsincluding South Africa. The technology is shiny and cool, but as we move to a cashless world, you can't help wondering if investment in cashpoint biometrics is money well spent 	. 	. 	. 	

Follow the money:Jack Ma'sAnt Financial is upping its advance on western markets, agreeing to pay$700m tobuyBritish payments group WorldFirst in what will be the Chinese firm'sbiggest overseas investment. The WorldFirst deal comes after Ant's attempt to buy US rival MoneyGram last year was shut down by USregulators on security grounds. Both sides are reportedly  confident  that this deal will get regulators' blessing, allowing Ant  to reach a greater number of customers, especially in the fast-growing area of cross border e-commerce . Already the world's most valuable young fintech firm   with a valuation of $150bn at its last fundraising round   Ant is best known for its Alibaba payments platform.

New frontiers:The UKgovernment is pumping1.4m into a new artificial intelligence-backed venture that will use voice recognition to spot insurance fraud.The project   a joint effort between Intelligent Voice, Strenuus and the University of East London   is aiming to  detect and interpret emotion and linguistics to assess the credibility of insurance claims . Insurance fraud costs the UK 3bn a year, a big prize to go forif the technology works. Overall, the UK government has put13m into 40 projectsin the insurance, accountancy and legal sectors as part of its Next Generation Challenge Fund, which promotes the use of AI in professional services.

US bank can use tech to serve customers better and more cheaply, says Mike Corbat

Citigroup chief executive Mike Corbat has suggested that  tens of thousands  of people working in the US bank s call centres are likely to be replaced by machines that can  radically change or improve  customers  experience while cutting costs.

Mr Corbat, who runs America s fourth-largest bank by assets, made the comments in an interview with the Financial Times in which he also ruled out Citi s involvement in any wave of US banking consolidation triggered by the $66bn SunTrust-BB&T merger and justified its continued presence in China.

Under pressure to bring its cost base in line with peers, Citi executives have been upfront about the impact of technology on their 209,000-strong global workforce, including last summer s warning that as many as half of the 20,000 operations staff in its investment bank could be supplanted by machines.

Mr Corbat s latest comments are the most explicit the company has been on how the $8bn a year Citi spends on technology could transform its vast consumer bank, which serves 100m customers across 19 markets.

 When you think of data, AI [artificial intelligence], raw digitisation of changing processes, we still have tens of thousands of people in call centres, and we know when we can digitise those processes we not only radically change or improve the customer experience, it costs less to provide,  he said.

He would not specify how many people Citi employed in call centre jobs or how many would be affected, but said that the  30 most common customer journeys , such as ordering replacement bank cards and asking about statements, were  pretty easy to deal with .

Other companies are already using AI in call centres   UK retailer Marks and Spencer has systems that can analyse voice commands in real time and direct queries to the right department, while Google is also developing software that uses open-ended questions to find the best answers for customers and cut waiting times.

Citi has no plans to get rid of humans in its call centres altogether though.  There ll always be the kind of thing where you ve actually got to have someone to help solve,  said Mr Corbat.  We don t want people frustrated in that. 

Mr Corbat cited the potential of Citi s technology as one of the reasons that his bank would not take part in the wave of US retail banking mergers that some analysts expect to be kicked off after BB&T and SunTrust unveiled the sector s biggest post-crisis merger a fortnight ago.

SunTrust and BB&T s merger was  predominantly driven by the need for scale around the ability to invest in and implement technology , said Mr Corbat.  We have that scale 	. 	. 	. 	We re comfortable with the markets we operate in, we re comfortable with our client base 	. 	. 	. 	That very quickly leads you back to a conversation about organic growth. 

In the era of Trumpian trade wars, critics have questioned Citi s geographic spread and its exposure to Mexico, where it owns the country s second-largest bank. Citi is also the most prominent of the US banks in China.

 China needs soy, China needs beef, China needs pork, Chinese needs import of raw materials into their supply chain,  said Mr Corbat.  Those routes may change. So as we [in the US] get tariffs and embargoes and things, those soy routes may move from the US to Brazil and Argentina   two places where we operate. We ve seen a redrawing of these trade routes, but the demand hasn t gone away. 

He added that Citi remained the best owner for Citibanamex in Mexico because  if you look at the pace of digital adoption and the expectation of consumer clients, the technology we re already using in Asia and the US is totally portable to Mexico today .

Employers seek specialist hires using MBA rankings

Jonathan Moules 
906 words
18 February 2019
04:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Recruiters are joining in debates on how business schools  performance is assessed

What is the purpose of business school rankings? The question made it on to the agenda of January s World Economic Forum meeting in Davos, Switzerland, with the publication of an in-depth report:  Business School Rankings for the 21st Century .

It came from the UN Global Compact, the world s largest global corporate sustainability initiative, and the report calls for a dramatic rethink of ranking methodologies. Significantly, the driving force for change in this report is not a school or student group but a multinational financial services group, Aviva Investors, which sponsored the research.

Aviva believes that MBA rankings  methodology should reward programmes that teach responsible capitalism, according to Steve Waygood, the company s chief responsible investment officer.  As MBA course offerings are increasingly driven by rankings, how can we ensure this race to the top between MBA programmes encourages a deeper integration of sustainable finance?  he asks.

Cambridge university research associate Ellen Quigley, who co-authored the report, says,  rankings are seen as both a shortcut [for employers] to finding good people and a driver of the types of education people want to obtain .

But finding overall agreement among employers on the best set of criteria to use to rank schools seems an impossible task. The UN Global Compact report criticises ranking lists for over-emphasising graduate salaries, penalising schools whose students go on to non-profit organisations and for not properly accounting for teaching quality.

It highlighted the Financial Times s recent introduction of sustainability teaching as a requirement for inclusion in its business school rankings as an example of a change that could be made to make the lists take on a more ethical purpose. (The Financial Times has announced that it is reviewing its methodology more widely.)

Dennis Zietsman, group deputy chairman at Servest Group, a facilities management business, says any business school ranking designed for employers would be difficult to assess because individual students vary so much even within a single institution.

Servest takes on 300 management level recruits each year, of which fewer than ten have MBAs. The London-based company, a division of Atalian Servest, employs 52,000 people across 16 countries in Europe, the US and Africa.

Mr Zietsman says that an MBA qualification would only make a difference to an applicant if it came from a school ranked among the top 15 worldwide. And the only measure on existing ranking tables that might be of use to Servest, he says, would be the percentage of international students.

Some employers look through ranking lists seeking outliers in business education, says Andrew Crisp, co-founder of the education marketing business Carrington Crisp.  Companies are often looking for people who can manage and lead new technological change,  he says.  So it can be useful if a list shows which schools are teaching subjects such as data science. 

Bain & Company, the consultancy, takes on about 400 MBA graduates each year from as many as 60 business schools. Keith Bevans, a partner who oversees Bain s MBA hiring process globally, looks to find new entrants from specialist masters and joint degree lists, such as the MBA with a combined masters in engineering sciences launched by Harvard Business School in 2017.

These lists highlight which schools provide the specific training that Bain is interested in, such as data analytics, Mr Bevans says.  We are always interested in understanding how the profiles of the schools and expectations of their students change over time, and rankings provide a measure of this,  he adds.

One of the most discussed elements of existing business school ranking lists as they stand are the salary levels graduates obtain.

While would-be business school students like to see salary data when they are choosing a course, business schools often claim that too much focus on salary detracts from the value of the teaching and other criteria.

Some employers, however, say they find rankings useful in determining salary levels for potential hires, according to Stphane Rambosson, chief executive of executive search firm Vici Advisory.

 They look at these as ballpark figures of what they can expect to pay hires,  Mr Rambosson says.  They are not going to treat the rankings like the Bible, but they are a good guide, allowing companies to tweak their offers. 

He points out that employers are interested in attracting people from schools with the strongest brands, which is what a ranking list provides.

Mark Butter, managing director of Blueprint Recruitment Solutions, says his clients are looking for particular skills from their MBA hires, so will only be interested in candidates from schools that include training in that area on their courses.

For most employers, though, an MBA from a school with a good position in a ranking list is  a nice to have  rather than the deciding factor when considering a candidate s potential, Mr Butter adds.  Their primary driver is the candidate themselves and what they can bring to the role,  he says.

Rankings still represent an enormous opportunity to ensure students are taught the skills needed to manage in the 21st century, according to Ms Quigley.

 I was told by one school that if a ranking provider announced that teaching a particular subject would count towards their place on their list, they would add that course to their curriculum instantly,  she says.

'Fairness' will be AGM buzzword for 2019

Owen Walker 
974 words
18 February 2019
Financial Times
FTFT

English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Investors expect arguments at annual meetings as some companies tackle largesse but others don't, writes Owen Walker

Executive pay has long caused conflict between shareholders and boards but this AGM season the attention will be on overgenerous pension deals.

UK investors are irate at the difference in benefits between company bosses and ordinary workers. In some cases, FTSE 100 chief executives receive retirement benefits worth more than half of their salary while other staff in the company are lucky to get a tenth of their pay as a pension contribution.

"The UK's gold-plated executive pension arrangements have to be tackled," says Iain Richards, head of responsible investments at Columbia Threadneedle, the $430bn asset manager.

"The disparity between employer contributions and those in FTSE executive schemes are unconscionable. How do you justify annual pension payments that can be 20 times or more the total gross annual earnings of the average fulltime UK employee? "There is broad agreement that investors need to tackle the issue."

A handful of FTSE 100 bosses receive a pension benefit equal to more than half their salary, including Alistair Phillips-Davies of SSE, the electric utility, George Weston of Associated British Foods and Friedrich Joussen of Tui, the travel company.

The figures are based on last year's annual results and for the financial year ending in 2017. They were compiled by the High Pay Centre and Chartered Institute of Personnel and Development.

Top of the list was Erik Engstrom, chief executive of Relx, the information and analytics company, whose pension benefit was equivalent to 66 per cent of his 1.2m salary.

The company said Mr Engstrom's pension appeared especially high because he was one of the few FTSE 100 chief executives in a defined benefit scheme   about 1,000 of the company's 30,000 staff are also members.

The Investment Association, the UK industry body, is taking a tough stance. Its view was set out in a letter sent by Andrew Ninian, director of stewardship and governance at the IA, to chairs of the remuneration committees at FTSE 350 companies. This said investors were frustrated at companies that did not align executive pay   and pension contributions   with performance.

"A stubborn minority still do not respond to shareholder concerns ... companies need to demonstrate more robustly the link between pay and company performance," Mr Ninian wrote in November's note. "If they don't, they should brace themselves for more shareholder revolts in 2019."

One reason more shareholders are objecting to lavish pension deals is a change made to the UK's corporate governance code from January 1. It says pension contribution rates for executives should be in line with those for the wider workforce.

Listed companies have to comply with the principles of the code or give reasons why they have not done so.

Companies are listening: Deloitte's annual review of FTSE 100 director pay showed that while median chief executive pension contributions were unchanged at 25 per cent in 2017-18, the upper quartile dropped from 30 to 25 per cent and the lower quartile from 20 to 16 per cent.

For companies outside the largest 30, the median pension contribution for CEOs fell from 25 to 20 per cent.

By contrast, the average FTSE 100 employer contribution to staff pensions is between 8.7 per cent and 10.5 per cent, according to Willis Towers Watson, the consultancy.

Pearson, the FTSE 100 education company, suffered a shareholder revolt against the pay award to chief executive John Fallon in 2017, two years after it sold the Financial Times.

Mr Fallon received a pension contribution equal to 40 per cent of his salary, which boosted his retirement pot by 309,000. His defined benefit scheme has since reached the maximum level for contributions so he is now in the money-purchase plan with a 26 per cent contribution rate.

While some boards are responding to shareholder criticism, others still give especially generous pensions deals to executives. They will have to be braced for showdowns at their annual meetings.

Mirza Baig, global head of governance at Aviva Investors, says shareholders will home in on egregious retirement deals. "Anything over 15 per cent is going to be fair game," he says.

"With the number of redundancies being announced and the threat of a no-deal Brexit, fairness will be one of the big themes of the AGM season. There will be less tolerance for big pay awards."

Pension entitlements should be the same across a company, according to Aviva Investors' voting policy. It acknowledges it will take time for some companies to adopt fairer arrangements but says that in the interim big differences must be disclosed and justified.

Some asset managers go further.

JPMorgan Asset Management, the $1.7tn investment business, updated its UK voting policy last month. It says executive contribution levels should mirror those of the wider workforce and that bosses should not benefit from schemes that are not open to other staff.

It adds that one-off payments into directors' pension pots and changes to their entitlement, as well as early retirement deals, should be disclosed and justified.

One argument used by shareholders to back a clampdown on largesse is that if a company has common pension rights, those who set pay policies will raise contribution rates across the board.

It is not only shareholders who seek greater fairness. "We are clear that fair pay policies are necessary to ensure good employee morale," says Caroline Escott, head of policy for investment and stewardship at the Pensions and Lifetime Savings Association, the UK industry group.

"Significant discrepancies, including those between senior executives and the rest of the workforce can be a signifier of wider problems with a workplace's culture and processes," she adds.

Do we need a global technology regulator for financial services?

Guest writer 
911 words
18 February 2019
10:38
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Why fintech doesn't work without a set of global regulatory standards.

This is a guest post by Axel P. Lehmann, UBS president of personal and corporate banking and president of UBS Switzerland, and Steffen Kern, chief economist and head of risk analysis at the European Securities and Markets Authority (Esma). Both are also members of the WEF global future council on financial and monetary systems.

Consistent international standards are vital to underpin the efficient flow of capital to the best opportunities. Cross-border financial intermediation provides the necessary connection between borrowers and savers, which supports GDP growth.

We saw this exemplified following the financial crisis, when the international community, through the G20 and the Financial Stability Board (FSB) developed an ambitious global response. A global crisis demanded a global reaction. International co-operation among regulators was central to making financial markets more resilient.

This said, the world of 2008 was very different to the one in which we are currently operating. Increasingly, inward-focused domestic policy perspectives are shaping policy agendas. Evidence of this shift in political strategy is seen in the US  doctrine of patriotism , the UK s decision to leave the European Union, and escalating trade disputes as a few examples. The belief that going it alone yields better outcomes than international co-operation continues to gain traction.

And these tendencies are deeply concerning. Our global economy is characterised by unprecedented levels of interconnectedness and these connections have benefited many citizens, investors and corporations.

The new challenge posed by technology

While certainly not a global crisis, technology is currently positioned to cause tremendous change in financial services at a global level. Distributed ledger technologies, artificial intelligence, machine learning and other innovations have the potential to change business models and value chains across the financial industry.

A recent example of new opportunities made possible through technology is the development of digital identities (E-IDs). E-IDs are particularly significant as verifiable identities are key to the next phase of the digital financial experience. The main benefit of an E-ID will be its transportability, enabling clients to access their optimal choice of services wherever and whenever they choose in a borderless online world. Important policy advances have been made in some economies, but E-IDs will only work if there are common regulatory standards and recognition of various E-ID schemes at international level.

Aligning international regulation is not a challenge isolated to E-ID technology. Rapid technological change has the potential to usher in a new phase of financial globalisation, with benefits for investors and households in both developed and developing countries. However, making effective use of technology's potential will require a new wave of global policy co-ordination.

Why technology in financial services requires co-ordinated regulation

As new technology multiplies and becomes ubiquitous in the financial sector, new risks will need to be contained and regulatory frameworks will need to be internationally co-ordinated and upgraded.

From a risk perspective, cyber security and cyber resilience are top priorities. According to the World Economic Forum Center for Cybersecurity, economic loss due to cyber crime is predicted to reach $3tn by 2020, and 74 per cent of the world s businesses can expect to be hacked in the coming year. Digital connectivity makes cyber crime a global threat that requires a standardised approach, yet, as recognised by the FSB, regulatory and supervisory approaches to cyber security are becoming increasingly fragmented, undermining the effectiveness of financial institutions' efforts to address cyber threats.

Additionally, new products, business models and players fall out of the current remit of existing regulatory and supervisory mandates of banking and capital markets authorities. This requires reflecting on the need for adaptation of existing mandates as well as on the necessity of aiming for a single regulatory approach to technology in financial services at international level.

Unlocking the benefits of new technology

Despite the regulatory challenges, the opportunities made possible with new technology are too great to ignore. Such changes range from product design to client interaction to back office operations to compliance and reporting. And they can translate into significant benefits for consumers of financial services as well as society in general.

Mobile and internet-based services are being made available independent of physical location. Only via co-operation will global resilience be ensured and the necessary level playing field emerge, enabling higher liquidity in financial products, safer transactions, fair pricing, lower compliance costs and less complexity   all to the ultimate benefit of corporate and retail consumers of financial services.

In terms of inclusion, new technologies also have great potential for supporting access to finance in less developed regions of the world, with mobile payment platforms in Kenya or India being prime examples. Yet, the challenges from multinational payment systems in terms of investor protection, information security and system integrity will be similar around the world and they will require a cohesive response.

Global policy co-operation on emerging technology is necessary to enable better finance

Opportunities will emerge from these technological changes. And they have immense potential. Globally aligned standards, data templates, protocols, conduct-of-business and anti-fraud rules are necessary preconditions for optimal development of new technological advances to support growth and, ultimately, societal wellbeing. The conclusions of the recent G20 Summit in Buenos Aires send a reassuring message, highlighting the commitment to continued regulatory and supervisory co-operation.

WSJ Tax Guide 2019: The Housing Market; By shrinking the tax benefits of homeownership, the tax law is starting to have a big impact on the housing market

275 words
15 February 2019
13:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The tax-code overhaul that partially wiped out decades-old perks designed to encourage homeownership is slowly beginning to reshape the housing market and give an extra push to those considering fleeing high-tax states.

By almost doubling the standard deductions for individual and joint tax filers, the law blunts the advantage of the mortgage-interest deduction. The overhaul also capped the deduction for state and local taxes at $10,000, a blow to homeowners in high-tax states.

["https://www.wsj.com/news/collection/wsj-tax-guide-2019-83be16c8" and "https://www.wsjplus.com/offers/tax-guide-2019".]

Taken together, the changes diminish significantly the perks of homeownership built into the tax code. ZIP Codes for areas that rely most heavily on the state and local tax deduction saw home values grow nearly a percentage point more slowly in December than ZIP Codes that rely least on the deduction, according to data from Zillow provided to The Wall Street Journal. That is a shift from November 2017, before the tax overhaul passed.

Despite the changes, U.S. home prices are still growing. But they are expected to be about 4% lower in the summer of 2019 than if there had been no change to the tax code, according to Moody's Analytics. In pricier markets in states like New Jersey, New York, Illinois and Pennsylvania, prices could be as much as 10% lower than they otherwise might have been, Moody's said.

Trump eyes bypassing Congress to privatise Fannie and Freddie

Kiran Stacey in Washington 
973 words
14 February 2019
11:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Move considered to end 10-year government control of state-backed mortgage groups

The Trump administration is considering bypassing Congress as it looks to reform the country s two largest mortgage guarantors, according to people who have been briefed on the plans, following years of failed attempts to remove them from state control.

Steven Mnuchin, the Treasury secretary, is working on a set of proposals that he hopes will end the 10-year government control of Freddie Mac and Fannie Mae, which guarantee most of the mortgages in the $10tn US housing market.

Mr Mnuchin has previously said he wanted to achieve this with support from Democrats in Congress, but with Washington as divided as it has been in recent years, officials are considering what options they have to act unilaterally.

Speaking on Thursday, Mark Calabria, the administration s nominee to run the housing regulator, said he thought the administration could recapitalise the two entities without having to secure congressional support.

But he added that more meaningful reform, such as introducing new competition into the mortgage market, would need new legislation   and therefore cross-party backing.

Mr Calabria told the Senate banking committee: I do believe that the regulator can make a number of changes that can try and get the GSEs [government-sponsored enterprises] on a stable footing for the next downturn when it inevitably comes.

 But I believe that the fundamental things that need to be changed by the system have to be done by Congress. 

One person who has been consulted on the administration s thinking said:  They want bipartisan support, but they know that is going to be difficult, especially with so many Democrats running for president. But there are plenty of things they can do without Congress giving its support. 

Freddie Mac and Fannie Mae have been in  conservatorship    which gives the government control of the two entities, but does not put their liabilities on the public balance sheet   since they were bailed out at the height of the financial crisis in 2008. Collectively their balance sheets are worth about $5tn.

In recent years, politicians on both sides have made moves to end this state of limbo. But so far these have foundered as lawmakers argue over how to restore them to the private sector while also making sure they underwrite loans for low-income households but do not pose a risk to the taxpayer.

The Trump administration has put privatisation back on the table, however, making housing reform one of its top goals for this year.

Mr Mnuchin said this month:  Protecting American taxpayers by ensuring the safety and stability of the United States housing finance system is a priority for the Treasury department. 

If Mr Mnuchin wants to succeed where others have failed, he needs either to persuade Democrats in Congress to back his plans or to find a way to avoid congressional approval altogether.

Some believe that the Trump administration can secure Democratic support if it guarantees a set amount of money for affordable housing.

Mark Zandi, chief economist at Moody s Analytics, said:  If you had the same affordability regime [as now] and on top of that provided extra money to subsidise an expansion of credit, there is a comfort level at which you get progressives on board. 

Most however believe that agreeing a package that is palatable to Democrats and Republicans is all but impossible, leaving Mr Mnuchin looking for solutions that do not require Congressional backing.

One option would be simply to recapitalise the two institutions and allow them to enter the private market as they are.

Since 2012, Fannie and Freddie have been required to return all their profits to the Treasury   a policy known as the  net worth sweep . Investors argue that ending this requirement would allow the two institutions to build up their capital buffers so they no longer present a risk to the taxpayer.

Tim Pagliara, executive director of a group of investors in Fannie and Freddie known as Investors Unite, said:  If they had capital requirements of 2.5-3 per cent, they would have enough money to survive the equivalent of a 500-year flood. 

Mr Pagliara points out that since 2008, the pair have returned $292bn to the Treasury in dividends   about 50 per cent more than they have drawn from the government since the crash.

Others, however, say new laws are required to make sure Fannie and Freddie continue to fulfil a public service by underwriting loans for poorer people and to make sure there is never a repeat of 2008.

Ed DeMarco, the president of the Housing Policy Council and former head of the FHFA, said:  Any administrative effort to remove Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac from conservatorship without legislation falls short on a fundamental goal of housing finance reform. 

One alternative option for the administration is not to remove them from conservatorship at all, but to recapitalise them while under government control and shrink their scope.

Suchan option however, is likely to attract anger from private shareholders hoping for a return, as well as from large parts of the banking industry, which does not want to compete with two fully-capitalised government-backed entities.

Given the complexities involved, many believe this administration is no more likely to succeed in privatising Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac than the host of members of Congress who have failed in the past.

Nevertheless, there remains an ideological impulse on both sides of politics to end the status quo.

Ron Haynie, who spent 20 years working for Freddie Mac and is now senior vice-president at the Independent Community Bankers of America group, said:  These two enterprises have been all but nationalised. That is something we simply don t do in this country.
