Chipmaker Nvidia falls from ranks of tech darlings

Richard Waters in San Francisco 
1,092 words
14 February 2019
04:12
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Sale of stake by SoftBank s Vision Fund comes as group hit by end of cryptocurrency boom

When SoftBank s Vision Fund took a stake of about 5 per cent in chipmaker Nvidia in 2017, it looked like vindication for one of the boldest strategies in the US semiconductor industry.

Jen-hsun Huang, Nvidia s chief executive, had long argued that the company s graphics processing units, developed to handle processes such as video gaming, would play a central role in the new era of data-intensive computing.

Last month, however, the vision ran out. SoftBank revealed that its fund had dumped its entire Nvidia stake   a holding which was worth more than $7bn at its peak last October, but which had collapsed to only $3.4bn at the end of 2018.

Among the questions left by the sale is how deep SoftBank s vision runs. The giant fund has promoted itself to both investors and the companies it backs as a new type of tech investor, willing to place bigger, long-term bets on the major disruptions caused by technology. But it held the Nvidia stake for less than two years, while also hedging its stake amid last autumn s tech stock tumble to protect itself from much of the decline.

SoftBank did not respond to a question about the length of its investment, which was unusual for the Vision Fund in being in a public company. Last month, it claimed a $2.8bn profit from the holding.

For Nvidia, the sale has thrown a spotlight on a difficult transition.

Two weeks ago, the chipmaker warned that it was likely to report revenues of only $2.2bn in the three months to the end of January, the final quarter of its fiscal year. That is 30 per cent less than Wall Street had been expecting only two months before, and represents a shocking reversal: after growing by 40 per cent in the first nine months of its fiscal year, the change implies that revenues slumped by a quarter in the final three months.

The recent hit to highly valued tech stocks   along with the company s own revenue slump   wiped 57 per cent, or $100bn, off Nvidia s stock market value in the final quarter of last year before a bounce that erased a fifth of the losses. The question now is whether the pain is over   and whether Nvidia s long-term prospects are still intact after the massive  reset  in its business.

Mr Huang is due to discuss Nvidia s prospects on a call with analysts on Thursday.

The immediate cause of Nvidia s pain can be traced to the collapse in the cryptocurrency market, where equipment based on its GPUs is used by  miners  to handle the intensive calculations required to authenticate transactions.

This dependence, which first led to a warning from Nvidia last November, has turned out to be greater than many investors expected. Some 25 per cent of the company s sales were probably tied to demand from the cryptocurrency markets, said Ambrish Srivastava, an analyst at BMO Capital Markets in San Francisco.

The end of the bitcoin boom has had other knock-on effects. While GPUs were in short supply, companies building machines for gamers submitted orders to Nvidia for far more chips than they needed, said Patrick Moorhead, an analyst at Moor Insights & Strategy in Austin.

That contributed to a spike in pricing across the GPU market last year: AMD, one of the major producers, reported that its average selling prices in its computing and graphics business had jumped by 55 per cent in the first half of last year, with much of that down to GPUs. With excess inventory likely to take another quarter or two to clear, according to Mr Moorhead, those forces have gone into reverse.

Underlying conditions in the gaming market have made the sudden slump more intense. China s limits on licensing new video games last year was seen as a major blow to demand. At the same time, Nvidia has reported slow take-up from gamers for its latest advance: chips that use so-called  ray tracing  technology, a way of simulating the effect of light hitting virtual objects that is credited with creating far more realistic images.

The question is whether this is anything more than a temporary blip. History suggests that gamers will quickly take to chips with ray tracing once developers produce more games that take advantage of it, said Mr Moorhead. Even if demand from the cryptocurrency market does not return, that would still bring renewed growth in Nvidia s main market.

The company warned last month, however, that it is facing a new headwind: weaker demand from data centre customers, who represent one of its biggest hopes for long-term growth.

Thanks to their ability to handle large amounts of data, GPUs have been added on as accelerators in machine learning systems. The AI boom has made GPUs a key component in the data centres of cloud computing companies, though a recent slowdown after a capital spending boom has brought a pause in spending. However, Mr Moorhead estimates that only around 5 per cent of data centre servers have AI accelerators, leaving a large market up for grabs.

Nvidia once had this market to itself. But there has been a race to develop new chip architectures to handle  inference    the task of applying machine learning algorithms to new data sets, the largest part of the new AI demand for silicon. Google has already developed its own specialised chips for machine learning, called TPUs, while Facebook and Amazon are both working on developing chips.

At the same time, other AI chips will soon become available. Intel, for instance, plans to launch a specialised chip for AI inferencing later this year, based on its CPUs. Start-ups like British company Graphcore represent another new source of competition.

The overall effect, said Mr Srivastava, is that Nvidia is facing far greater competition and will no longer have the AI market largely to itself. The stock price needs to fall further to reflect the new realities it is facing, he added.

But Mr Huang s assurances that his company s long-term prospects are undimmed   whatever the short-term troubles   still resonate with many.  It s a growing market, and Nvidia has room to grow,  said Mr Moorhead.  Yes, there will be more competition   but the market is going to be so much bigger.

VC Daily: Inside the (Subscription) Box | Bold Bet on Synthetic Biology's Upstarts

Good day. Johnson & Johnson's $3.4 billion cash deal to buy Auris Health Inc., the largest acquisition of a venture-backed medical-device company, could have far-reaching effects across the industry.

Auris's Monarch system is designed to help surgeons gain access to hard-to-reach nodules in the lungs while maintaining constant vision and control, according to the company, which raised more than $700 million from Lux Capital, Highland Capital Partners, Section 32, Mithril Capital and several other investors.

Peter Hbert, a Lux managing partner, said Auris's success could draw more talented engineers into medtech and spur corporations to be more ambitious with medical-device product development and acquisitions.

Added Mr. Hbert: "I wouldn't be surprised if you saw boards at large medical-device companies [reassessing] their strategy and whether they're playing it too safe, and whether they have innovation on their road map."

And now on to the news...

Today's Top Stories

Inside the box: Venture capitalists' affection for subscription-based e-commerce startups like Birchbox Inc. waned the past couple of years, but FabFitFun Inc. has emerged as a bright spot in the category.

Los Angeles-based FabFitFun, which sends subscribers a monthly box of wellness and beauty products, held a recent funding that valued the company at more than $1 billion, according to a person familiar with the matter.

The round was led by growth-stage firm Bond, one of its first since the team broke away from Kleiner Perkins last fall.

FabFitFun's latest funding was a large sum for a Series A round, but the company was founded in 2010 and has fueled growth on its own sales.

The company, which previously raised just $6.5 million in venture capital, has positive cash flow on revenue of more than $300 million, the person said. WSJ Pro's Katie Roof has the scoop.

Mega Mithril return: Auris Health's aforementioned pending sale to Johnson & Johnson for $3.4 billion is a victory for investors in the medical-robotics startup none more than Mithril Capital Management.

The growth-investment firm co-founded by Ajay Royan and Peter Thiel is Auris's largest investor, holding a stake of about 15%, according to a person familiar with the situation.

The deal is slated to return about $510 million in cash to Mithril, as well as more in potential future payouts. That would represent almost as much as Mithril's entire first fund, which closed with $540 million in 2013.

Auris raised more than $700 million in venture funding from Mithril, Lux Capital, Wellington Management and several others. WSJ Pro's Brian Gormley and Yuliya Chernova have the scoop.

Mack's Take: Labs Take Aim at Powering Synthetic-Biology Startups

By Heather Mack

It takes trial and error on a vast scale to create bioengineered versions of things like meat, leather and even cannabis.

The excitement over the promise of synthetic biology has drawn considerable investor attention in recent years. A new generation of scientists is using high-powered tools like artificial intelligence and DNA sequencing to produce microorganisms on an industrial scale.

Culture Biosciences Inc., which was unveiled this week, could emerge as another leader in the sector. The startup in South San Francisco, Calif., is backed by $5.5 million from top venture-capital names like Verily, DCVC and Bill Maris's Science 32, and it already has landed clients including agtech startup Pivot Bio Inc. and synthetic collagen maker Geltor Inc.

Culture aims to set itself apart by offering a system that combines proprietary software with custom-built "bioreactors" think of the machinery used to brew beer to automate the fermentation stage of hard-to-scale realms like plant-based meat and textiles.

"Fermentation is a key process in the scale of those products, but it's also where a lot of the bottleneck happens," said Culture co-founder and Chief Executive William Patrick "These are situations where companies are constantly tweaking the experiments, so if you have a way to automate that, it saves a lot of time and money."

Exits

Ant Financial Services Group is acquiring U.K.-based money-transfer company WorldFirst to boost its presence globally. WorldFirst was backed by investors including FTV Capital, Industry Ventures and StepStone Group. Read more.

Dynamic Map Platform, a Tokyo-based developer of high-definition maps for autonomous driving and advanced driver-assistance systems, agreed to acquire peer Ushr Inc. for an undisclosed amount. Ushr, of Detroit, was backed by GM Ventures, EnerTech Capital, Emerald Technology Ventures and Forte Ventures.

Early-stage investor Pillar has nearly completed the raising of an $85 million fund, according to a regulatory filing. The Boston-based firm counts home-equity investment platform Hometap, outdoor digital-marketing startup Soofa and 3D-printing startup Desktop Metal Inc. in its portfolio.

Mortgage Slowdown Sparks A Boom in Servicing Rights

By Ben Eisen 
964 words
14 February 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The mortgage-market slowdown is stirring up interest in a humdrum segment of American home lending: the rights to the arcane task of handling monthly payments.

Sales of mortgage-servicing rights jumped 14% in 2018 from a year earlier, according to industry research group Inside Mortgage Finance, to more than $600 billion of loans backed by Fannie Mae, Freddie Mac and Ginnie Mae. In the final three months of 2018, the servicing changed hands on loans worth $183 billion, up 27% from the previous year.

Mortgage servicers, in addition to collecting a borrower's installment and passing it on to the loan owner, take care of duties like ensuring taxes and insurance are paid. If the borrower defaults, the servicer is the first to jump into action. The company that services a mortgage used to be the same as the one that made the original loan, but in recent decades those two crucial housing-market functions have separated.

The increase in servicing transfers is the latest ripple effect from a slowdown in the housing market, which has forced lenders to slim down, consolidate or close.

Many of the sellers are independent mortgage lenders that don't have deposits to fund themselves or other lines of business that can help them withstand a downturn. Stronger players -- both banks and nonbanks -- have been picking up servicing rights from weaker lenders that need to raise cash.

The industry shakeout stems from a sharp decline in refinancing activity as rates have risen and a drop-off in new purchases.

"The name of the game when you're in lean periods of origination and you're not a bank is that you need sources of cash," said Eric Schuppenhauer, president of home mortgages at Citizens Financial Group Inc. "You're just trying to make it to the next good origination period."

Some buyers see servicing rights as a new source of revenue in the more challenging market and a chance to pitch additional services. Citizens bought Franklin American Mortgage Co. last year, more than doubling the number of households whose loans the Providence, R.I.-based bank services.

The lender's mortgage-banking fees, trending lower before the acquisition, jumped in the final half of last year. Servicing revenue made up 41% of all mortgage-banking fees last quarter, up from 21% in the year-earlier period.

New Residential Investment Corp., a real-estate investment trust managed by private-equity firm Fortress Investment Group LLC, said in its Tuesday earnings report that it snapped up servicing rights on $114 billion in loans from 15 different business partners last year.

New Residential's stock is up 20% this year, more than double the S&P 500's rise.

"We do expect more consolidation in the mortgage-origination and servicing business throughout the course of 2019, and we believe that we're perfectly situated to take advantage of that," Chief Executive Michael Nierenberg said on an earnings call Tuesday. "Mortgage bankers are going to need to raise some capital, therefore, there'll be more assets for sale."

Ditech Holding Corp., which filed for bankruptcy this week, could sell its servicing rights, analysts say. Keefe, Bruyette & Woods Inc. estimated Ditech has servicing rights on $104 billion of loans that it could sell.

Mortgage servicers came under fire during the financial crisis for the way they treated homeowners who were facing foreclosure. One firm, Ocwen Financial Corp., later settled with multiple regulators to resolve allegations of mistreating borrowers.

These days, homeowner delinquency rates are low. A number of regulators, including the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau, now oversee mortgage servicers.

Lenders don't need a borrower's consent to sell a loan or servicing right, and the terms of the loan don't change when they are transferred.

The risk for homeowners is that they end up with a servicer they don't like or trust -- and didn't sign up to work with when they took out the loan.

When Andrew Smith took out a mortgage last winter to buy a house in Queens, N.Y., he went to FM Home Loans LLC. At the closing, he found out that his servicer would be Citigroup Inc. Last fall, he found out that the servicer would now be Cenlar FSB.

Mr. Smith, who works as a camera assistant in the entertainment industry, said that he was surprised by the multiple switches.

"It was a little unnerving," he said. "It happened twice in the first year of the loan." He said he doesn't particularly like the website of his newest servicer but was reassured by a knowledgeable customer-service representative he spoke with on the phone.

Servicing rights generally become more valuable as interest rates rise because borrowers are less likely to refinance their loans and the stream of income from servicing continues for longer.

Prices on servicing rights for mortgages backed by Fannie, Freddie and Ginnie rose by 7% to 9% on average last year, though it varied widely based on the initial mortgage rate, according to data from MountainView Financial Solutions, which advises on servicing transactions and provides analytics.

Nonetheless, these assets can be volatile. Wells Fargo & Co., the country's biggest mortgage lender, reduced the value of its mortgage-servicing rights in the final three months of 2018, the company said in its earnings report.

What's more, banks may have less incentive to hold on to servicing rights because pending rules could force them to hold more capital as a result. The Mortgage Bankers Association, which lobbies on behalf of the industry, pushed back on these rules late last year and has said they are forcing banks out of the market.

Cyber Daily: Bumpy Road to NotPetya Insurance Payouts; Espionage in Iran

1,271 words
14 February 2019
12:34
WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY

Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Cyber Daily: Bumpy Road to NotPetya Insurance Payouts; Espionage in Iran

By Kim S. Nash

Good day. Is physical damage caused by cyber incidents covered by property insurance? Is data property? Who determines when a cyberattack is an act of war? And how?

In lawsuits against their insurance firms, pharmaceutical firm Merck & Co. and snackmaker Mondelez International Inc. seek to get these questions answered. The companies were denied claims for damages, expenses and losses related to the 2017 NotPetya cyberattack. The firms argue that their property policies cover physical damage, including loss of data, software and code, WSJ Pro's Catherine Stupp reports. One crucial unknown that the cases could clarify is whether NotPetya was an act of war, and what evidence is required to designate it as such.

Elsewhere, WSJ reports on the former U.S. Air Force special agent who federal prosecutors now say is a spy for Iran, in an indictment unsealed Wednesday. Monica Elfriede Witt hasn't been arrested and is believed to be in Iran.

*********

NotPetya Insurance Disputes Debate Cyber Acts of War and Whether Data is Property

By Catherine Stupp

Two lawsuits related to the 2017 NotPetya cyberattack reveal disputes about when insurance companies should pay for large-scale hacks that disrupt business operations.

Merck & Co. and Mondelez International Inc. are suing their insurers after they refused to pay claims for losses from the incident, citing exclusions in property insurance policies for acts of war or hostile acts by government agents. Industry analysts say the insurance companies carry the burden of proof to show that NotPetya was a government-sponsored act of war.

Also at issue is the more general question of whether physical damage triggered by a cybersecurity mishap is covered under traditional property insurance.

Losses globally from NotPetya will cost the insurance industry around $3.3 billion, according to research from Verisk Analytics Inc.'s risk assessment firm Property Claim Services.

Merck's expenses and lost sales related to NotPetya are about $700 million, according to its financial filings. The company is suing multiple insurance and reinsurance companies, including Zurich Insurance Group.

Mondelez is also suing Zurich Insurance, claiming $100 million in costs related to NotPetya.

Merck and Mondelez did not respond to requests for comment. A spokeswoman for Zurich Insurance declined to comment on the ongoing litigation.

The U.S. and U.K. governments in February 2018 blamed Russia for the cyberattack, but have not announced charges against individual hackers or publicly discussed details to prove their allegation.

"The attribution issue will be really, really difficult for Zurich here and the other insurers that are involved," said Judy Selby, a consultant who advises companies on cyber insurance.

Is data physical property?

Insurers dispute whether data can be considered a physical asset and therefore fall under property policy coverage, according to Scott Stransky, director of emerging risk modeling at Verisk's data analytics firm AIR Worldwide.

"On a property policy, if you want to collect business interruption, there needs to be some physical damage. In this case, the data loss was that trigger [of damage]," Mr. Stransky said.

At Mondelez, for example, NotPetya broke 1,700 servers and 24,000 laptops, according to the snack company's court filing.

Last October, Mondelez sued Zurich Insurance, asking the insurer to pay $100 million to cover property damage, distribution disruption and other costs from the cyberattack. Zurich denied Mondelez's claim, citing the policy's exclusion for "hostile or warlike action," according to the complaint. Zurich later agreed to pay the claim, Mondelez said, but then changed its position several months later, again denying the claim.

Merck filed its lawsuit last August, demanding that 15 insurance companies and seven reinsurers cover damages from the attack. In denying Merck's claim, the insurers cited a coverage exclusion for acts of war in the company's property policies. Merck contends that the policies cover destruction of computer data, code, programs and software.

The lawsuit doesn't say how much Merck seeks but notes that the company's costs and losses exceed its deductibles. Merck said it has received payments of an unspecified amount for NotPetya damages from insurers through separate privacy and network liability policies.

Limiting payouts

Some insurance companies have begun to limit coverage of cyber incidents in property and other policies, according to Jim Lynch, chief actuary at the trade group Insurance Information Institute Inc.

Insurance companies can quantify risks related to traditional property threats, such as fire or fraud, he said, but cyberattacks and their after-effects are more unpredictable.

Zurich Insurance took a step this week towards clarifying its view of which policies cover what. Cyber policies cover damages that other kinds of policies don't, Lori Bailey, Zurich Insurance, global head of cyber risk, wrote in a blog post.

"Property policies typically either exclude or offer only very limited coverage for the loss of or corruption of electronic data," she said. "Cyber policies are specifically designed to fill many of the cyber coverage gaps in other types of insurance."

**********

Quotable

"The attribution issue will be really, really difficult for Zurich here and the other insurers that are involved."

  Judy Selby, a consultant who advises companies on cyber insurance, on Zurich Insurance Group's denial of claims related to the 2017 NotPetya attack.

U.S. Accuses Former Intelligence Officer of Giving Secrets to Iran

A federal indictment says former U.S. Air Force special agent Monica Elfriede Witt disclosed information that helped the Iranian government target her former colleagues. The indictment, unsealed Wednesday, accused Ms. Witt of providing Tehran with highly classified information about American intelligence operations overseas after she defected to Iran, The Wall Street Journal reports.

The background: Ms. Witt traveled to Iran in 2013 and after telling a friend she might follow the example of NSA leaker Edward Snowden went on to disclose secret information in an attempt to undermine U.S. intelligence operations, according the indictment. She served as a counterintelligence officer and had access to top-secret information during her time in the Air Force from 1997 until 2008, the indictment said.

Four Iranians also charged: The Iranians are accused of conducting a hacking campaign for Iran's Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps in 2014 and 2015 to try to obtain access to the computer networks of Ms. Witt's onetime U.S. intelligence colleagues, the indictment said. The document didn't specify how much, if any, access they obtained.

Not arrested: All five are believed to be in Iran and aren't in U.S. custody. Ms. Witt and the other defendants couldn't immediately be reached for comment.

**********

More Cyber News

U.S. cyber official calls for paper receipts from voting machines. By the time of the 2020 election, all voting machines in the U.S. must be able to issues paper receipts to protect the process, said Christopher Krebs, director of the Cybersecurity and Infrastructure Security Agency, testifying Wednesday before the House Committee on Homeland Security. Louisiana, Georgia, South Carolina, New Jersey and Delaware use machines that don't produce paper records, Bloomberg reports. States overall need up to $1 billion to replace outdated voting equipment, said Thomas Hicks, chairman of the Elections Assistance Commission, according to The Hill.

Malta bank shuts down after hack. In an attack on Bank of Valletta, hackers created fake transactions to shift more than $14 million to banks in Britain, the Czech Republic, Hong Kong and the U.S., Reuters reports. The bank shut down its ATMs and website, as well as banking operations, while it investigates.

Nvidia Earnings: What to Watch; Chip maker tries to shake off cryptocurrency, videogame funk

By Asa Fitch 
503 words
14 February 2019
10:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Nvidia Corp. is set to report fiscal fourth-quarter earnings after the market closes Thursday. The results come a little more than two weeks after the chip maker slashed its revenue forecast, citing slowdowns on multiple business fronts. Here's what to expect:

EARNINGS FORECAST: Analysts surveyed by Refinitiv expected Nvidia to report earnings of 75 cents a share as of Wednesday. A year ago, the company reported $1.12 billion in net income.

REVENUE FORECAST: Analysts surveyed by Refinitiv expected revenue of $2.2 billion, on par with Nvidia's revised outlook. That would be down from $2.91 billion a year earlier.

What to Watch

CRYPTOCURRENCY WOES: Nvidia benefited handsomely when a boom in mining digital currencies led to surging demand for graphics-processing chips well suited to the task. When cryptocurrencies cratered in 2018, however, that "https://www.wsj.com/articles/nvidia-grapples-with-fallout-from-cryptocurrency-miners-1542395327". Analysts warned in November that inventory problems could persist, and they will be looking for signs of whether the hangover is over. Christopher Rolland, an analyst at Susquehanna Financial Group, appeared optimistic that Nvidia's plans to sell down inventory were going according to management's schedule. "We do not see deep-discounting at retailers, suggesting a controlled inventory burn," he wrote.

GAMING TRACTION: Weaker-than-expected sales of high-end graphics chips used by PC gamers were one of the reasons Nvidia revised its "https://www.wsj.com/articles/nvidia-lowers-guidance-on-weakness-in-gaming-datacenter-businesses-11548690812" to $2.2 billion in January, slashing $500 million from its earlier projection. Thursday's results and the guidance Nvidia issues for the current quarter should yield clues about how well its newest generation of graphics processors released last fall are selling. The chips include new technologies such as ray tracing, but Nvidia said in January some customers may be putting off purchases, waiting for prices to go down and for new features to be used in more games. "We don't think that the January quarter was a 'kitchen sink' quarter that will drive a V-shaped recovery, but we do see the temporary factors gradually abating," analysts at Morgan Stanley said.

CLOUD OUTLOOK: Nvidia has a strong presence in data centers, and its chips are used widely in artificial intelligence and high-performance computing. But customers are becoming more cautious as the global economic outlook cools, which the company in January said led to a number of deals not closing in the fourth quarter. Intel Corp., too, recently "https://www.wsj.com/articles/intel-projects-slower-revenue-growth-this-year-11548365723" for server chips. Moreover, with a data-center business that has grown quickly, booking eye-popping growth rates has become all the more difficult, wrote Stacy Rasgon of Bernstein Research. Headline risk is likely to keep increasing, he said. "And tactically the near-term cloud spending environment is unfavorable, driving near-term downside." Analysts foresee $671 million of revenue from the data-center business, down about 15% from the previous quarter.

Etienne Amic, once a managing director at JPMorgan, will lead the digital group

A blockchain oil platform launched late last year to modernise physical commodities trading has hired a former JPMorgan Chase energy trader as its chief executive.

Vakt, the platform backed by leading oil companies such as BP and Royal Dutch Shell, traders Mercuria and Gunvor, and banks such as ING and ABN Amro, has appointed Etienne Amic, a former managing director at Mercuria and JPMorgan, to lead the company.

Using blockchain technology, Vakt was set up in November as a way of speeding up trades and reducing costs by eliminating the paper-based trading process. The underlying platform provides security and an audit trail that cannot be altered.

Vakt s original investors and first users of the platform include energy majors BP, Equinor and Shell; independent traders Gunvor, Koch Supply & Trading, and Mercuria; and banks ABN Amro, ING and Socit Gnrale. These have since been joined by Chevron, Total and Reliance Industries.

Mr Amic is co-founder and chairman of Vortexa, a cargo and analytics company focused on global crude oil and refined products markets, and founded CommodiTech Ventures, a group funding the next generation of commodities technology.

As well as the buyers and sellers of the raw material, the trading of physical oil involves terminal or pipeline operators, acting as de facto physical clearinghouses, and service providers such as port agents and inspection companies helping the material to move along and checking its physical characteristics.

Mr Amic said  the goal of Vakt is to facilitate all these contributions seamlessly and  on platform  .

He added that to have leading oil companies and banks, many of them competitors, pulling in the same direction indicated that Vakt was  something very powerful that the industry really needs .

The Trump administration named prominent business executives to its newly formed workforce-policy advisory board Wednesday, including the leaders of Apple Inc., Home Depot Inc., International Business Machines Corp. and Visa Inc., as well as education and labor officials.

The advisory board is headed by Commerce Secretary Wilbur Ross and Ivanka Trump, President Trump's daughter and a top White House adviser, and will focus on worker training and matching workers to job opportunities, amid concerns about automation, artificial intelligence and rapidly changing workplace demands.

"We want all Americans to have the skills and opportunities to secure good paying jobs and successfully navigate technological disruptions and the rapidly changing nature of work," said Ms. Trump.

The panel is being formed amid a strong job market. "https://www.wsj.com/articles/job-openings-push-further-into-record-territory-at-end-of-2018-11549985771" in the U.S. rose in December to the highest level since 2000, and data for January showed robust hiring.

The 25-member advisory board was established by an executive order Mr. Trump signed in July. That order created the National Council for the American Worker, a group of senior administration officials focused on developing a nationwide strategy for training employees, as well as the advisory board.

At the time, the president announced pledges from companies to hire or train millions of workers over the next five years.

The Trump administration sought to partner early on with corporate leaders and developed several advisory councils but they collapsed in part over Mr. Trump's controversial response to a white-supremacist rally in Charlottesville, Va., in August 2017. Mr. Trump worked to rebuild relations, however, and corporate executives have rallied around the common cause of training workers for future job needs.

Behind one of the biggest bank deals in a decade is a recognition that BB&T Corp. and SunTrust Banks Inc., both dominant banks in the South, would be more competitive with a bigger tech budget.

The all-stock, $28.2 billion deal is expected to help the rivals close a widening digital gap that is fueling one of the biggest threats to their business: losing relevance among younger customers.

The deal, which would create the sixth-largest regional bank and a financial powerhouse in the South, underscores the urgency facing regional banks to invest heavily in client-facing and back-end technology to continue attracting consumer and corporate customers.

"What's the largest strategic risk facing the banking industry? It's technology," said Todd Baker, a senior fellow at the Richard Paul Richman Center for Business, Law and Public Policy at Columbia University. "How are they going to be more nimble and effective?"

The transaction will allow the former rivals to develop better technology together than they could on their own. They plan to reinvest $1.6 billion in projected cost savings from the merger into technology and innovation.

The companies also plan to create an innovation and technology center at the merged entity's new headquarters in Charlotte, N.C., to drive digital transformation, including the adoption of automation and an improved customer experience online.

"This is the early innings of an enormous transformation of all of financial services," Mr. Baker said.

In recent years, big banks such as JPMorgan Chase & Co. and Bank of America Corp. have invested heavily in developing consumer-facing technology such as artificial intelligence-powered chatbots and digital investing apps.

VC Daily: Unionizing Startup Workers | Betaworks Sheds Studio Focus

Good day. Unionization inside upstart media companies has rapidly become a thing.

The latest drive to form a bargaining unit has hit the newsroom at BuzzFeed News, whose parent company is backed by investors including Andreessen Horowitz, Founder Collective and SoftBank Group.

Although the union movement of news employees at BuzzFeed began before the company's recent wave of editorial layoffs, it has followed on the heels of similar organizing at digital-native places like Vox Media and Vice News. Workers also have been fighting for protections across the broader startup landscape, from minimum-wage campaigns for ride-hailing contractors to the independent "shoppers" at gig platforms like Instacart.

The message for investors is that being a disruptive startup doesn't forever insulate owners' ability to have free rein with the means of production.

And now on to the news...

Today's Top Stories

Crypto sleuthing's heavy hitters: Accel and Benchmark are making a big blockchain bet: They have invested $30 million in Chainalysis Inc., a provider of real-time compliance and anti-money-laundering software for financial institutions working with cryptocurrencies.

The Series B round was led by Accel but included an investment from Benchmark, which led the startup's Series A round last April.

Chainalysis says it aims to build confidence in the crypto industry, which is still working to prove that it can be a safe and reliable system for sending currency and completing transactions.

Despite a prolonged downturn in crypto prices, investors have shown continued interest in investing in startups that provide infrastructure and tools to improve the use of crypto currencies. WSJ Pro's Tomio Geron has the story.

Gene therapy advances: The use of healthy genes to compensate for defective ones is gaining momentum for biotech startups seeking to tackle a broader range of medical problems.

One key development was the U.S. regulatory approval in 2017 of Spark Therapeutics Inc.'s Luxturna, a gene therapy to treat an inherited disease leading to blindness.

Riding on that momentum, Neurogene Inc. has set out to show that gene therapy can treat rare neurological illnesses such as AGU, a disease causing a progressive decline in mental functioning.

New York-based Neurogene, founded just over a year ago, said it has raised a $68.5 million Series A funding, as it works to develop gene therapy for AGU and a series of rare neurological diseases. WSJ Pro's Brian Gormley has the story.

Chernova's Take: Why Betaworks Is No Longer a Venture Studio

By Yuliya Chernova

Venture studios are getting launched left and right, with the likes of Techstars getting into the game this year. But one of the originals, Betaworks, has largely abandoned that business model.

John Borthwick created Betaworks in 2008. New York-based Betaworks incubated several social-media startups including Giphy, Dots and TweetDeck. (In a nod to some of the negative consequences of social technology, the sticker on the back of Mr. Borthwick's iPhone reads, "Social media seriously harms your mental health.")

A few years ago, Betaworks was developing some eight startups at a time in-house.

The business has since evolved. Betaworks has a venture fund, an accelerator and a membership space called Betaworks Studios, not to be confused with its original startup factory. But Betaworks isn't developing new startups the way it used to.

"I don't think it makes sense to build a new studio in New York," Mr. Borthwick said, adding that the model might be better suited for cities where technology is an emerging industry.

Nowadays there are many more places for budding founders to get money and build on their own without sacrificing a big part of the equity pie and independence that startup studios can eat up. It also has become more expensive to create new startups especially in the consumer space where Betaworks specializes.

Says Mr. Borthwick: "All the beachfront real estate has been built out for the end user."

More in Chernova's Take

Exits

Monitoring software provider Datadog Inc. acquired Madumbo, an automated application testing platform. Terms of the deal weren't disclosed. New York-based Datadog is backed by investors including Iconiq Capital, Index Ventures, OpenView Venture Partners, Amplify Partners and Contour Venture Partners.

Import.io, a web data platform and web-scraping tool provider, acquired Connotate, a provider of web data extraction services for businesses, for an undisclosed amount. Los Gatos, Calif.-based Import.io is backed by investors including Imperial Innovations, Wellington Partners, Oxford Capital, OpenOcean, Delin Capital and AME Cloud Ventures.

VC Funds

PeakSpan Capital, a growth-equity firm focused on business-to-business software deals, has closed its second fund with $265 million. The firm closed its debut fund with $150 million in 2016. In a news release announcing Fund II's closing, the firm said it invested its debut offering in 12 businesses.

Sekhmet Ventures, which will invest primarily in startups with science-based products in the beauty and wellness markets, has launched. The firm counts superfoods startup Stamba in its portfolio.

New Money

Autonomous trucking company TuSimple has raised $95 million to expand its fleet of self-driving big rigs and fund joint product development with truck manufacturers and equipment makers. Read more.

More funding news...

The company also acquired recruitment marketing startup Talemetry, employee referral platform RolePoint, and Canvas, a text-based conversational recruiting provider. "https://www.jobvite.com"

Good day. Cory Johnson, the journalist-turned-chief market strategist, is out at Ripple.

Mr. Johnson, a former on-air personality at Bloomberg News and CNBC, is no longer with the blockchain-based global payments company. His position was eliminated because of "changes in market conditions," Ripple said in a statement.

Mr. Johnson, who joined Ripple last March, wasn't immediately available to comment. The value of Ripple's XRP cryptocurrency is trading this week at about one-third of its value since he arrived at the company. Now, that is indeed a change in market conditions.

And now on to the news...

Today's Top Stories

Delivery wars: Food-delivery startup DoorDash Inc. is arming itself with new funding as two of its rivals prepare for IPOs.

The venture-backed company is in the process of raising roughly $500 million at a valuation of more than $6 billion, and that might approach $7 billion, according to a person familiar with the situation.

That valuation range would be four to five times what DoorDash was valued at in a financing round less than a year ago.

Singapore's Temasek Holdings is expected to lead the round, the person said. GIC, another Singaporean state entity, is already a DoorDash investor.

DoorDash didn't respond to requests for comment. WSJ Pro's Yuliya Chernova has the scoop.

Grocery goals: SoftBank Group is investing almost $1 billion in robotic-delivery-vehicle startup Nuro Inc., a hefty injection of capital that could help accelerate the race to put driverless vehicles on the road. Nuro raised $940 million from SoftBank's tech-focused Vision Fund, the startup said. Founded in 2016, Nuro is now valued by investors at $2.7 billion. The WSJ's Tim Higgins has the story.

Energy deal: In a rare venture-capital funding for a renewable-energy startup, geothermal startup Dandelion Energy Inc. raised $16 million.

GV, funded by Alphabet Inc., co-led the Series A for the startup, which is a spinout from Alphabet's X Lab. Comcast Ventures was the other co-lead. Returning investors included New Enterprise Associates, Collaborative Fund and Zhenfund.

Led by Kathy Hannun, Dandelion Energy says it has designed a more efficient heat pump and other processes to reduce the cost of residential geothermal installations.

Homebuilder Lennar Corp. also invested. The company had explored other attempts at geothermal heating and cooling for homes but they were prohibitively expensive, said Eric Feder, managing general partner for Lennar Ventures.

Roof's Take: Spotify's Podcast Shopping Could Kick Off More Deals

By Katie Roof

Spotify Technology SA may have some competition the next time it goes shopping for podcasting-related assets.

Anchor FM Inc., a platform for making and distributing podcasts, was acquired by Spotify last week for more than $150 million, according to people familiar with the matter. Spotify also snatched up Gimlet Media Inc., the content-making platform behind hit podcasts like "The Pitch" and "Reply All."

Spotify has said it plans to spend up to $500 million on podcast deals this year.

"It's going to be a very exciting year for podcasting," said Rich Greenfield, an equity research analyst at BTIG Research. "More and more of these earlier-stage startups are going to get acquired."

He said that in addition to Spotify, Apple Inc., Alphabet Inc. and Amazon.com Inc. are candidates to make acquisitions to complement their existing music and smart-speaker businesses.

Dave Zohrob, co-founder of Chartable Inc., a podcast analytics startup, predicted that Netflix Inc. will make purchases in the space, since audio competes with video for entertainment.

Other related startups he believes could potentially get picked up include hosting platform ART19 Inc., which has received backing from DCM and Array Ventures, as well as podcast publisher Wondery Inc., which has been funded by Greycroft Partners and Lerer Hippeau.

Patrolling the high seas with fintech

Oliver Ralph 
1,584 words
11 February 2019
20:10
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Your essential weekly fintech briefing

Late last month a huge explosion, possibly caused by a fuel transfer, engulfed two cargo ships in the Kerch Straits near Russia, killing at least 11 sailors.

It's the kind of incident that marine insurers dread. ButWindward, a Tel Aviv-based tech company, says that the warning signs were there.

The company s analytics showed the tankers involved were in the top 2 per cent of the world s riskiest vessels for smuggling. Both had been involved in ship-to-ship transfer activity and had spent a suspiciously large proportion of their time outside ports. They also turned off their AIS transponders, which broadcast their location details, far more regularly than other ships.

Windward is one of a clutch of companies hoping to apply new technology to improve the performance of marine insurers.

It's a tall order. Marine insurance is a centuries-old business   Lloyd s of London was founded as a marine market   and has a reputation for being particularly resistant to change.

But it has also been a very tough business over the past few years, with profits under pressure as prices have fallen, forcing marine insurers to look to technology for answers. Chris Sandilands, partner at consultancy Oxbow, said:  It s an area where we are seeing a lot of activity. It doesn t follow that just because a part of the industry has been around a long time, there won t be any innovation. 

 The industry does not get to choose where innovation happens. Entrepreneurs do,  he added.

One of those entrepreneurs is Ami Daniel, co-founder and CEO of Windward.

Mr Daniel, a former officer in the Israeli Navy, says life in the marines and life in marine insurance both demand a  certain type of person  since the culture is  to do things bit by bit, slowly .

Nevertheless, he reports growing interest in what his company is offering. Windward creates profiles of individual ships that tell their owners and insurers how they're operated, where they go, whether they sail in deep or shallow waters and how well maintained they are. All crucial information for people deciding how much it'd cost to insure them.

He s had some big name backing. Former CIA director David Petraeus has put money in. So have John Browne and Tom Glocer, the former CEOs of BP and Thomson Reuters, along with current Salesforce CEO Marc Benioff.

More crucially, last year insurer Axa XL led a $16.5m funding round. Insurers in the industry like to move together, so an investment from Axa XL could encourage others to adopt similar technology.

Windward isn't the only tech company to have spotted an opportunity in marine insurance. UK-based Clearwater has signed a deal with DNK, a Norwegian mutual insurer that specialises in war risk for ships and covers about $224bn in assets.

Clearwater is installing specialist equipment on the 2,700 vessels covered by DNK that transmits details of their location every 7.5 minutes. If the ship enters an area where there's a high risk of war, DNK is alerted and can adjust the premium to take the higher risk into account.

Will Unwin, Clearwater s chief executive, said that his company s equipment can deliver more accurate tracking data than is available elsewhere.  DNK are accumulating data points based on true 7.5 minute reporting 	. 	. 	. 	The consistency of the data are crucial. 

The two companies have taken their partnership one stage further by setting up a joint venture called Osprey Analytics to help push the system out to other insurance companies.

There's still a long way to go before these sorts of systems are widely adopted in marine insurance. But an executive at one leading insurer says that eventually, the whole market will have to move.

 There is a lot of pressure in the marine market, and something needs to change,  he said.  Pressure is building and building. At some point something is going to shift and there could be an earthquake. 

One to watch: Pagaya

Using artificial intelligence to build portfolios of securities is a well-established practice; using AI to build the securities themselves is more of a frontier affair,writes Robert Armstrong.

Israeli fintech Pagaya, which specialises in using machine learning and big data to manage money,last week announced the issue of its first asset-backed securities (ABS) made up of consumer loans selected entirely by a computer and managed by an AI. The $100m offering was led by investment bank Cantor Fitzgerald.

The algorithm picks the loans by drawing individual borrowers' data from credit bureaus, publicly available information about past securitisations taken from the official sources such as the FDIC, as well as macroeconomic data.

Gal Krubiner, Pagaya s chief executive, says a well-designed computer model using a rich data set is  just better  at judging credit quality than a human team:  The algorithm is very good in terms of assessing the willingness of borrowers to pay. 

During the construction of the ABS, over a period of several months, the algorithms  are trying to find trends [in loan performance] rather than factors , according to Mr Krubiner. The computer is observing  the inter-connectivity between the different parameters   it is a dynamic allocation of the importance of the different factors  such as borrower, geography, credit events, and so on.

Pagaya, which now has $450m under management across its portfolio, hopes to issue as much as $2bn in consumer loan ABS in the next few years.

The loans themselves come from online lending platforms such as Lending Club, Prosper and Upgrade   and indeed, it's the appearance of those platforms in recent years that made Pagaya s business possible.  This is short duration, good quality consumer credit,  says Mr Krubiner. The average borrower FICO score is a solid 700, he says, and the average loan is for about $10,000, has an average life of a year and a half, and an annual interest rate in the mid-teens.

Number of the week: $440m

The amount SoftBank's Vision Fund has invested in British banking start-up OakNorth, making it the largest European fintech fundraising

Further fintech fascination

Stumbling blocks:Revolut, the payments unicorn best known for offering rock bottom foreign exchange rates, is being investigated by the UK's Advertising Standards Authority over a  spoof  ad campaign that drew ire from London commuters.  To the 12,750 people who ordered a single takeaway on Valentine's Day, You OK, hun?  Revolut asked on billboards across the London Underground, in a campaign reminiscent of Spotify and Netflix's controversial use of customer data to create witty ads a few years back. UK commuters weren't amused; especially when the FT's personal finance editor Claer Barrett embarrassed the payments app into admitting the figures in the Valentine's ad   and another iteration about the numbers buying Greggs vegan sausage rolls   were  just made up . In a financial services world where trust is paramount, few fintechs would want to be in Revolut's shoes right now 	. 	. 	.

Crypto chronicles:Spare a thought for the 92,000 or so crypto enthusiasts whose $150m of coin held with crypto exchange QuadrigaCX seems to have gone to the grave with Quadriga's founder. The Canadian exchange announced the death of 30-year-old Gerry Cotton last week, and said he was the only one who held the access codes to the $150m of coin Quadriga was holding on behalf of clients. Conspiracy theories abound. The Canadian regulator says it'spowerless, since it doesn't regulate Quadriga. The Canadian courts have sent in EY as a monitor to Quadriga's workout. Meanwhile, the victims, including a Californiasoftware engineer who lost his life savings, are left dangling. Still, the affair seems to have little dented the overall crypto market; bitcoin is up about 5 per cent, to around $3,600, in the six days since the debacle became public.

Trend watch:All good things come to those who wait, or at least that's the hope among those wishing to trade on Switzerland's new blockchain-enhanced exchange. The SIX exchange originally hoped to launch the SIX Digital Exchange (SDX) in the summer, but is now eyeing a date in the second half of the year, chairman Romeo Lachertold Reuters last week. The SDX hopes to used distributed ledger technology to complete transactions in  fractions of seconds , a big advancement on SIX (and other traditional exchanges), where trades can take several days to clear. Blockchain is already used in other exchanges, including theGibraltar Blockchain Exchange, but these only trade crypto-based assets   the SDX believes it's in the lead to use blockchain for an exchange that trades traditional assets.

AOB:German fintech Raisin   which helps customers maximise their deposit interest   raised $114m from a flurry of big names including Index Ventures and PayPal *** Singapore police raided the offices of German fintech Wirecard after an internal probe revealed forged documents and accounting irregularities. Here's the FT's deep dive into the scandal *** The biggest risk to quant funds is  that we lose confidence and row back , Andrew Dyson, who leads the quant unit of Prudential Financial's funds arm, told the FT. *** Around 100 fintechs have applied for licences in Lithuania, including many which are looking for a new EU home after Brexit, Lithuanian central bank official Marius Jurgilastold Reuters.

Guide to pricing of Authorised Investment Funds.

Compiled with the assistance of the IMA. The Investment Management Association, 65 Kingsway, London WC2B 6TD. +44 (0)20 7831 0898. All funds within this section, whether OEICs or unit trusts are authorised in the UK by the Financial Services Authority. The prices quoted should only be used as a guide.

OEIC: Open-Ended Investment Company. Similar to a unit trust but using a company rather than a trust structure.

Share Classes: Separate classes of share are denoted by a letter or number after the name of the fund. Different classes are issued to reflect a different currency, charging structure or type of holder. Buying price: Also called offer price. The price at which units in a unit trust are bought by investors. Includes manager's initial charge. Selling price: Also called bid price. The price at which units in a unit trust are sold by investors.

Single price: Based on a mid-market valuation of the underlying investments. The buying and selling price for shares of an OEIC and units of a single priced unit trust are the same. Exit Charges: The letter E denotes that an exit charge may be made when you sell units, contact the manager/operator for full details. Time: The time shown alongside the fund manager's/operator's name is the time of the unit trust's/OEIC's valuation point unless another time is indicated by the symbol alongside the individual unit trust/OEIC name.

The symbols are as follows:m0001 to 1100 hours;. 1101 to 1400 hours;.1401 to 1700 hours; # 1701 to midnight. Daily dealing prices are set on the basis of the valuation point, a short period of time may elapse before prices become available.

Yield: Funds comprising mainly of bonds normally quote a gross redemption yield after all charges but before tax has been deducted. Funds mainly made up of equities normally quote a yield representing the estimated annual payout net of tax for basic rate taxpayer. For further information contact the management company.

Historic pricing: The letter H denotes that the managers/operators will normally deal on the price set at the most recent valuation. The prices shown are the latest available before publication and may not be the current dealing levels because of an intervening portfolio revaluation or a switch to a forward pricing basis. The managers/operators must deal at a forward price on request, and may move to forward pricing at any time. Forward pricing: The letter F denotes that that managers/operators deal at the price to be set at the next valuation. Investors can be given no definite price in advance of the purchase or sale being carried out. The prices appearing in the newspaper are the most recent provided by the managers/operators. Scheme particulars, prospectus, key features and reports: The most recent report, scheme particulars, prospectus and key features document may be obtained free of charge from fund managers/operators.

Inside View: It's Time to Fire Your Doctor

By Andy Kessler 
864 words
11 February 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Barack Obama famously and falsely said, "If you like your doctor, you will be able to keep your doctor, period." But . . . what if you don't like your doctor?

Let's say you, like me, are one of the 20 million Americans who work for themselves -- no boss, but also no corporate-tax deduction for health insurance. The smart move is to get a high-deductible insurance plan. Now it suddenly matters what doctors charge: $500 to take your blood pressure and bang your knee with a rubber hammer, $1,200 for a blood test that uses pennies worth of chemicals to tell you your hemoglobin levels are fine. Plus four months to get an appointment, and then the doctor asks you to fax an authorization. What? It's 2019. It's time to fire your doctor.

Like roughly half of American adults, I'm borderline: hypertensive, high cholesterol, though only "pre"-diabetic. A streak of misdiagnoses has led me and many others to take doctoring into our own hands. I do an annual blood test for $199 through WellnessFX and get results on a smartphone app. A Bluetooth-connected cuff from Omron Healthcare tracks my blood pressure and even notes atrial fibrillation or irregular heartbeat. A Fitbit scale tracks my weight. Beautyrest Sleeptracker tells me my REM sleep duration. My Apple Watch charts my resting pulse and does a simple electrocardiogram. The more data, the better.

When you do get sick, you still need to see a doctor -- they have that prescription pad. But insurance companies tired of overpaying for five-minute doctor visits have begun setting up alternatives. Sutter Health runs walk-in clinics for $129 a visit. Online care is cheaper, so Anthem Blue Cross encourages customers to use LiveHealth, a videoconference platform, for $49. Aetna has a deal with Teladoc, a $4.5 billion public company, for $38 consultations. No pain meds, of course, but almost everything else.

Consumer-driven options have already changed nonvital care. After doing in-home teeth impressions, you can order orthodontic aligners for $80 a month. For acne treatment, you can upload photos of affected areas and receive medicine by mail monthly. Technology is lowering costs and improving care on all fronts.

Technology has moved so fast that several smartphone-based platforms now function like Uber for doctors: Doctor on Demand, PlushCare, Amwell and MeMD are cutting into primary care. These are gig-economy doctors who provide care on demand for, well, gig-economy workers and others without employer insurance coverage. Today I can upload my health stats and connect to my platform of choice 24/7, whereas just recently I could barely get a hold of my doctor by phone, let alone email or texts, because he couldn't get paid for that time, stuck in a 20th-century pay scheme.

Taking charge of your health care can be complicated and is not for everyone, but it's doable for most. I'd stick with services that hire doctors from top 25 medical schools. And here's a warning for hypochondriacs: Consult a real doctor and do not -- I repeat, do not -- Google your symptoms and convince yourself you have everything from diverticulitis to distomatosis.

If you're really sick you still need to see a specialist: urologist, gynecologist, gastroenterologist, otolaryngologist, pulmonologist, cardiologist. But by then you'll have blown through your high deductible, so that's what insurance is for anyway. And even the specialists will soon realize they can benefit from telemedicine to reduce in-office visits. For now, when you inevitably and repeatedly fill out the prescreening paperwork, specialists always ask for the name of your primary-care physician. I put down "Dr. Webb."

Doctors don't scale, so the real future of medicine is digital diagnosis. The best doctor sees one patient at a time, but a clever piece of code can be used by countless people. All the data that patients record is needed for smart artificial-intelligence-based diagnoses. So why not collect more? Current or emerging technologies can enable at-home cholesterol monitoring and better EKGs, easy glucose monitoring, blood tests to find cancer early before symptoms show up, Alzheimer's screening, thyroid-stimulating hormone tests, even DNA tests that demonstrate a propensity for certain diseases.

Data, data, data -- the more the better. As I write this, I have a Fantastic Voyage-like capsule near my stomach transmitting pH levels. How cool is that? As this technology progresses, more tests and more capsules will fill databases with personalized information.

The goal of these measurements isn't always to give a black-and-white diagnosis saying you have some disease or you don't, but to build data that can be analyzed over time. As more data is recorded, machine learning will allow programs to detect patterns of known disease progression, and flag them early when treatment is cheap and effective. The revolution is coming. But not from your doctor.

Inside View
Opinion
It's Time to Fire Your Doctor; Medical tech allows us to monitor health, get advice and seek care remotely and cheaply.

By Andy Kessler 
852 words
10 February 2019
20:03
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Barack Obama famously and falsely said, "If you like your doctor, you will be able to keep your doctor, period." But . . . what if you don't like your doctor?

Let's say you, like me, are one of the 20 million Americans who work for themselves no boss, but also no corporate-tax deduction for health insurance. The smart move is to get a high-deductible insurance plan. Now it suddenly matters what doctors charge: $500 to take your blood pressure and bang your knee with a rubber hammer, $1,200 for a blood test that uses pennies worth of chemicals to tell you your hemoglobin levels are fine. Plus four months to get an appointment, and then the doctor asks you to fax an authorization. What? It's 2019. It's time to fire your doctor.

Like roughly half of American adults, I'm borderline: hypertensive, high cholesterol, though only "pre"-diabetic. A streak of misdiagnoses has led me and many others to take doctoring into our own hands. I do an annual blood test for $199 through WellnessFX and get results on a smartphone app. A Bluetooth-connected cuff from Omron Healthcare tracks my blood pressure and even notes atrial fibrillation or irregular heartbeat. A Fitbit scale tracks my weight. Beautyrest Sleeptracker tells me my REM sleep duration. My Apple Watch charts my resting pulse and does a simple electrocardiogram. The more data, the better.

When you do get sick, you still need to see a doctor they have that prescription pad. But insurance companies tired of overpaying for five-minute doctor visits have begun setting up alternatives. Sutter Health runs walk-in clinics for $129 a visit. Online care is cheaper, so Anthem Blue Cross encourages customers to use LiveHealth, a videoconference platform, for $49. Aetna has a deal with Teladoc, a $4.5 billion public company, for $38 consultations. No pain meds, of course, but almost everything else.

Consumer-driven options have already changed nonvital care. After doing in-home teeth impressions, you can order orthodontic aligners for $80 a month. For acne treatment, you can upload photos of affected areas and receive medicine by mail monthly. Technology is lowering costs and improving care on all fronts.

Technology has moved so fast that several smartphone-based platforms now function like Uber for doctors: Doctor on Demand, PlushCare, Amwell and MeMD are cutting into primary care. These are gig-economy doctors who provide care on demand for, well, gig-economy workers and others without employer insurance coverage. Today I can upload my health stats and connect to my platform of choice 24/7, whereas just recently I could barely get a hold of my doctor by phone, let alone email or texts, because he couldn't get paid for that time, stuck in a 20th-century pay scheme.

Taking charge of your health care can be complicated and is not for everyone, but it's doable for most. I'd stick with services that hire doctors from top 25 medical schools. And here's a warning for hypochondriacs: Consult a real doctor and do not I repeat, do not Google your symptoms and convince yourself you have everything from diverticulitis to distomatosis.

If you're really sick you still need to see a specialist: urologist, gynecologist, gastroenterologist, otolaryngologist, pulmonologist, cardiologist. But by then you'll have blown through your high deductible, so that's what insurance is for anyway. And even the specialists will soon realize they can benefit from telemedicine to reduce in-office visits. For now, when you inevitably and repeatedly fill out the prescreening paperwork, specialists always ask for the name of your primary-care physician. I put down "Dr. Webb."

Doctors don't scale, so the real future of medicine is digital diagnosis. The best doctor sees one patient at a time, but a clever piece of code can be used by countless people. All the data that patients record is needed for smart artificial-intelligence-based diagnoses. So why not collect more? Current or emerging technologies can enable at-home cholesterol monitoring and better EKGs, easy glucose monitoring, blood tests to find cancer early before symptoms show up, Alzheimer's screening, thyroid-stimulating hormone tests, even DNA tests that demonstrate a propensity for certain diseases.

Data, data, data the more the better. As I write this, I have a Fantastic Voyage-like capsule near my stomach transmitting pH levels. How cool is that? As this technology progresses, more tests and more capsules will fill databases with personalized information.

The goal of these measurements isn't always to give a black-and-white diagnosis saying you have some disease or you don't, but to build data that can be analyzed over time. As more data is recorded, machine learning will allow programs to detect patterns of known disease progression, and flag them early when treatment is cheap and effective. The revolution is coming. But not from your doctor.
