South Korea start-ups need helping hand to break chaebol grip

BRYAN HARRIS 
864 words
1 February 2019
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa
14
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Technology. New era Tough regulation and low investment levels hold back innovative young businesses

As South Korea's economy splutters, there is a growing focus on why the country has relatively few successful tech start-ups.

The administration of president Moon Jae-in hopes the development of a start-up ecosystem in and around Seoul can create jobs and power growth as the country's long dominant conglomerates, such as Samsung and Hyundai, gradually lose competitiveness to Chinese rivals.

Officials have already pushed ahead with policies aimed at encouraging funding for new companies, and in 2018, there was a record Won3.4tn ($3.04bn) of venture capital funding for start-ups.

But for many in the tech community, much broader economic reform and deregulation efforts are needed if the plans are to succeed.

"If the start-ups could scale up and grow, they could create a number of quality jobs, which would be beneficial for young people, while improving the nation's broader economic structure as well," said Choi Sung-jin, president of the Korea Start-up Forum.

Start-up has long been a buzz word for South Korean officials, who dream of turning Seoul into an Asian Silicon Valley but have historically been unable or unwilling to wean the nation off its reliance on the big conglomerates, known as the chaebol.

With the country's manufacturing sector facing crisis, however, policymakers are being forced into action. In September, officials rolled out the first tranche of a $9bn-plus investment fund to support start-ups in a range of fields from artificial intelligence to drones.

The move was followed a month later by a proposal for sweeping capital market reforms, aimed at loosening regulations so that Seoul's start-up scene could more easily secure financing.

In South Korea, only about 2 per cent of funds raised by small businesses come from capital markets and the average investment is just Won1.7bn   about 11 per cent of the amount raised on average by US start-ups and less than 8 per cent of what Chinese companies raise, according to the Financial Services Commission.

"Most venture investment funds are led by the government," said Mr Choi. "We need to see more private investment in start-ups. We need to see successful start-ups invest in other startups and conglomerates invest in startups through mergers and acquisitions."

The economic dominance of the large conglomerates has long been a problem for emerging companies in South Korea.

Immensely wealthy and historically backed by government support, the chaebol have a tradition of establishing their own affiliates to compete and undercut start-ups instead of acquiring the new technology through M&A.

"Korean M&A has lagged behind other countries," said Park Tae-geun of the Korea Venture Business Association. "For example, the big US technology companies have acquired hundreds of promising start-ups in recent years, demonstrating how small enterprises can recover their investment through active M&A."

But Korea, Mr Park added, was a different story. "The rate of start-ups recovering their investment through M&A is less than 10 per cent. Even from a social point of view, if Koreans could see that start-ups can receive a fair price, it would be good motivation to develop their own businesses."

The broader issue facing the fledgling start-up scene is South Korea's multitude of highly specific regulatory requirements, which plague the operations of small enterprises.

Mr Choi points to the example of Neofect, a healthcare start-up whose technology allowed patients to practice rehabilitation exercises at home. While Neofect's products won plaudits in the US, the company could not operate in South Korea, where regulation requires patients to undergo rehabilitation under the supervision of a doctor, he added.

"We have many little, detailed regulations in the country, which prevent start-ups from growing and scaling up their businesses," Mr Choi said.

Lim Jung-wook, managing director of Start-up Alliance Korea, added that Korea was known to have very rigid regulations in areas such as ridesharing, healthcare and fintech, which could hamper big global companies from making investments in the local market.

At the end of last year, a regulation "sandbox" was introduced that allowed start-ups to apply for a two-year waiver from the rules to test their products in the market. A specific fintech sandbox will start taking applications from the end of this month.

The challenges facing South Korea's start-ups are borne out in the nation's limited number of unicorns   those private groups with a value of more than $1bn.

According to researchers, South Korea has only a handful of such groups, despite the country's highly educated population and advanced infrastructure.

These are led by Coupang, the ecommerce site, while others include Woowa Brothers, which operates a delivery service app, Crafton, the maker of a battle royale style computer game, and Viva Republica, which runs a money transfer service.

Mr Park said: "For start-ups to grow, the government must begin lifting all the hurdles and regulations." Additional reporting by Kang Buseong

CareerBuilder Says AI Can Cut Piles of Rsums in Half; With reams of jobs-related data collected over more than two decades, CareerBuilder is using AI to help companies find job candidates faster

By Angus Loten 
752 words
1 February 2019
11:00
WSJ Pro Artificial Intelligence
RSTPROAI
English
Copyright  2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

CareerBuilder is applying artificial intelligence to data gathered by its online employment service, an effort to speed the process of finding promising candidates among piles of rsums.

Hiring is a growing challenge for recruiters amid one of the tightest labor markets in decades, the company's chief executive told WSJ Pro AI.

"We have one of the largest data sets in the world. It was time to start doing something with it," Irina Novoselsky said.

The Chicago-based online employment and human-resources technology company last week acquired Textkernel, a Netherlands-based startup that develops AI and machine-learning tools for recruiting, sourcing, lead generation and labor-market statistics. The terms of the deal weren't disclosed.

By feeding its data into algorithms, CareerBuilder says employers can reduce the number of applications per successful hire to an average of 75, compared with an industry average of 150. It cited an internal study.

The Textkernel acquisition marked the latest move by the 25-year-old venture, owned by Apollo Global Management LLC , to beef up its technology amid rising competition from rival job boards, search engines and other online employment services.

Nearly all Fortune 500 companies now use some form of automation in the recruiting process, including robot avatars to interview job candidates, programs that scan keywords in rsums, and AI and machine-learning tools to assess potential employees.

Sites like Getting Hired or FedEx Careers have deployed Google 's Talent Solution, a machine-learning-based search platform, among other tools.

Some leave no stone unturned. DeepSense, based in San Francisco and India, uses AI to analyze a job candidate's behavior on social media everything from choice of words, to whether they ask questions or express opinions, share links or original posts as a proxy for deducing underlying personality traits. Likewise, Utah-based HireVue offers an AI algorithm that compares job seekers' tone of voice, word choices and facial expressions with top performing employees.

"The HR space is going through a seismic shift," with recruiters becoming technology buyers, Ms. Novoselsky said.

Over the past five years, CareerBuilder has hired more than 200 data scientists, engineers and machine-learning specialists, tasked with creating smart applications that more effectively match candidates with jobs, said Ms. Novoselsky. She joined the company in 2017 as chief operating officer and was named to the top post in September.

Last year, it had 200 million unique visitors and placed 6.6 million jobs globally, and is currently serving 90% of Fortune 1000 companies in 185 markets world-wide, the company said.

Its secret weapon? More than a quarter-century worth of data from online job postings, rsums, applications, wages, benefits and more, Ms. Novoselsky said.

"There's a big misnomer around AI, in that it's really just an engine," she said. "But it needs data to become a smart engine."

The spread of digital technology and online tools, which generate that data, also has made it easier for job seekers to fire off countless applications, while enabling employers to cast an ever wider net for applicants. The recruiting process has been transformed into a massive undertaking.

Ms. Novoselsky said a shortcoming of many automated HR systems is that they filter applications based on single search terms without context such as "graduate," "Java" or "multimedia" and fail to significantly narrow the field of candidates.

They also tend to trip over misleading or unrecognized terms used in rsums or cover letters, she adds. For instance, a rsum may cite a candidate's experience as a greeter at a theme park that refers to its paying guests as "clients." Most systems wouldn't know how to handle that, she said.

Textkernel, coupled with CareerBuilder 's store of candidate and jobs data, is able to search and identify strings of words and phrases to spot entire patterns of employment history, skills and experience, along with location rather than single words or terms. As such, it is able to pare down the list of potential job candidates to a more manageable amount, Ms. Novoselsky said.

CareerBuilder has rolled out the tool for some of its talent acquisition services, but expects it to be fully integrated in all of its tools within the next year, she said.

C Suite
Wells Fargo Breaks Down Internal Audit Silos to Fend Off Scandals; Changes to the 'third line of defense' are meant to address previous gaps in oversight

By Kristin Broughton 
584 words
1 February 2019
22:49
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Wells Fargo & Co.'s 103-page business-standards report, released this week to address changes the bank has made in response to a string of scandals, revealed nuanced changes to the company's system of internal checks.

The tweaks to the San Francisco company's internal auditing team a unit known as the "third line of defense" in risk parlance led to the creation of several new teams, including one that focuses exclusively on identifying inconsistent consumer practices.

The changes illustrate how the portfolio of risks facing banks is expanding, and how auditors are being organized to manage them.

Alterations within the company's auditing division, in place for months but disclosed Wednesday, are part of a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/wells-fargo-is-working-hard-to-rebuild-trust-11548886641" to improve risk management at Wells Fargo.

The lender has been fighting to improve its reputation following a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/wells-fargo-to-pay-states-about-575-million-to-settle-customer-harm-claims-11546016757" throughout the bank, including in retail banking, wealth management and foreign exchange.

The company in September 2016 paid $185 million to settle allegations that it created unauthorized customer accounts. Since then, it has disclosed that it previously overcharged auto and mortgage borrowers, among other problems, leading to billions in fines and "https://www.wsj.com/articles/wells-fargos-chief-administrative-officer-auditor-on-leaves-of-absence-1540414804" of several senior executives.

As a result, the company consolidated its retail-banking auditing team under a centralized group in an effort to break down silos and have a broader view of risk across the business, according to a company spokesman. Auditors in that group previously were assigned to niche areas, such as home lending.

The restructuring shows how the company is addressing gaps in the previous system, said Will Callender, a partner in the financial institutions division at management consulting firm A.T. Kearney. "They have organized themselves in a way that directly addresses some of the challenges they have had," he said.

Wells Fargo also created new teams to focus on issues such as board-level governance and capital management. Additionally, it created an audit services division that specializes in testing and data analytics.

"Those functions existed, but they were part of other audit functions," said Mark Folk, a company spokesman. "We broke those out to give them more attention."

Over the past two years, the bank has increased head count within its auditing division by about one-third, to 1,350 employees, Mr. Folk said. And it changed the way it manages risks, including by adding more experienced directors to its board-level risk committee.

Companies that have survived a crisis, such as banks in the aftermath of the mortgage meltdown, often revamp risk-management priorities to account for the challenges they have previously faced, experts said. The challenge facing auditing divisions is finding a way to account for future threats.

"It is not uncommon that companies are always tending to organize to fight the last war," said Richard Chambers, chief executive of the Institute of Internal Auditors, who said the changes at Wells Fargo are in line with industry standards.

It is impossible to know if the changes can stave off future scandals at the bank, said Cliff Rossi, a professor at the University of Maryland's Robert H. Smith School of Business and a former risk executive at Citigroup.

"A lot will remain to be seen as to how well they execute," he said.

Private Equity-Backed TPx to Combine With SPAC Pensare

By Jaewon Kang 
433 words
1 February 2019
11:01
WSJ Pro Private Equity

English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Private equity-backed U.S. TelePacific Holdings Corp., which operates as TPx Communications, is choosing the blank check company path to the public markets.

The company agreed to combine with special purpose acquisition company Pensare Acquisition Corp., to become publicly traded on the Nasdaq exchange with an implied enterprise value of $1.1 billion, according to a press release. The company will continue to be led by Chief Executive Dick Jalkut and Chief Financial Officer Tim Medina. Pensare Chief Executive Darrell Mays will become vice chairman of the combined company's board of directors.

TPx started in 1998 as a competitive local exchange carrier, providing telephone services to small and midsize businesses. The Los Angeles-based company has since evolved to provide managed services that monitor and deliver information-technology infrastructure for organizations. Today, about 65% of the TPx's revenue comes from managed services, said Mr. Jalkut who has led the company since 2002.

As TPx's conversion to a managed services provider progressed, the company believed it was an opportune time to go public, Mr. Jalkut said. Combining with Pensare is giving it a faster route to the public marketplace.

TPx originally received a $125 million investment from Investcorp back in 2000 and in 2017 had retained investment banks to explore a sale, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/private-equity-backed-tpx-communications-weighs-sale-1495625401". At that time, Mr. Jalkut said TPx also weighed the option to go public but ultimately decided it wasn't ready.

"It was a  show me' story more than a  tell me' story," Mr. Jalkut said, explaining that the company's strong performance in the second half of 2018 gave the team conviction that the time was finally right for a public listing. Moving forward, TPx plans to fuel growth by expanding its geographic footprint and service offerings.

TPx is projected to generate between $701 million and $711 million of revenue and between $135 million to $140 million of earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization in 2019, according to the release.

TPx's current shareholders, including Investcorp, will remain equity holders in the combined entity. Pensare will pay $248 million in cash and $95 million in stock to the equity holders of TPx upon the closing of the proposed transaction.

TPx isn't the only private-equity portfolio company that has sought to go public by merging with a SPAC this year. In January, Clarivate Analytics, a subscription-based analytics provider backed by Onex Corp. and Baring Private Equity Asia"https://www.wsj.com/articles/spac-churchill-capital-to-combine-with-clarivate-analytics-11547485990?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=1"with blank check company Churchill Capital Corp.

Fed Holds Rates Steady | Central Banks Pivot in Tandem | U.S. Economy Is Slowing | The Fed's Mysterious Pause; The Wall Street Journal's central banking newsletter for Thursday, January 31, 2019

5,317 words
31 January 2019
12:23
WSJ Pro Central Banking
RSTPROCB
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Good day. The Federal Reserve held rates steady and signaled it may continue to do so for some time, as it concluded its policy meeting Wednesday. The central bank dropped explicit references to future interest-rate increases that have been in its policy statements since it started lifting its benchmark rate in 2015. The Fed's step away from rate increases comes as economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal see the U.S. economy growing slower than the 3% rate posted through much of last year.

Now on to today's news and analysis.

Fed Leaves Rates Unchanged, Signals Possible End to String of Rate Increases

The Federal Reserve indicated Wednesday that "https://www.wsj.com/articles/fed-leaves-rates-unchanged-signals-pause-on-future-increases-11548874931" raising interest rates for now, fueling a market rally. Officials voted to hold their benchmark rate steady and delivered an about-face from their policy stance six weeks earlier. Last month, they raised their benchmark rate by a quarter percentage point to a range between 2.25% and 2.5% and signaled two more rate rises were likely this year. "The case for raising rates has weakened somewhat," Chairman Jerome Powell said at a news conference after the Fed's policy meeting. He declined, when asked, to say whether the Fed's next rate move was more likely to be an increase or a cut. "It's going to depend entirely on the data," he said.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/transcript-fed-chairman-jerome-powells-postmeeting-press-conference-11548884492"

"http://www.wsj.com/pro/centralbanking/graphics/fed-statement-tracker/?1234565662334&mod=article_inline"

"https://www.wsj.com/livecoverage/federal-reserve-january-meeting-2019?mod=article_inline&mod=hp_lead_pos1&mod=article_inline"

Analysis: The Fed's Mysterious Pause

The Federal Reserve's monetary tightening hasn't just paused; "https://www.wsj.com/articles/the-feds-mysterious-pause-11548893175?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=1", writes Greg Ip for The Wall Street Journal. That's the most logical conclusion to draw from the statement released after the central bank's meeting Wednesday and Chairman Jerome Powell's press conference. Fed statements often contain a "bias," a hint of whether the next interest rate move will be up or down. Wednesday's had none: The Fed will be "patient as it determines what future adjustments" to make to rates, it said.

Central Banks Pivot in Tandem, Postponing Stimulus Plans

A slowing global economy and low inflation has central banks around the world "https://www.wsj.com/articles/central-banks-pivot-in-tandem-postponing-stimulus-plans-to-fuel-growth-11548930601?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=1" to gradually pull back financial stimulus from markets and the banking system. The role reversal could support the economy in the months ahead and bolster markets and sectors like housing and autos.

Fed Makes Rate-Control Tool Kit Essentially Permanent

Federal Reserve Chairman Jerome Powell made it official Wednesday that the central bank will continue to use its current monetary policy rate-control tool kit for a long time to come.

"The committee made the fundamental decision today to continue indefinitely using our current operating procedure for implementing monetary policy," Mr. Powell said at the start of his press conference after the Federal Open Market Committee meeting.

When the Fed first implemented the system that puts a hard high and low end around short-term rates, it was billed as a temporary way to set monetary policy in a world where its crisis-era stimulus policies had left the banking system flooded with reserves. Those actions sidelined the Fed's precrisis method of setting short-term rates, wherein it added or subtracted reserves as necessary to influence a market-set federal-funds rate.

The current system uses a rate that it pays banks to park reserves on its books to determine the high end of the range, and a similar rate it pays to money funds to set the low end. It describes this as its fed-funds target rate range, and it's now set at between 2.25% and 2.5%.

But the implementation has been tricky. Market forces have caused the fed-funds rate to trade at the high end of the target range, so the Fed has been forced to tweak the interest on excess reserves rate paid to banks. The central bank has lowered that IOER rate relative to the top end of the fed-funds range to ensure the funds rate doesn't drift too high.

It's a complicated system, and now it's here to stay.

"Settling this central question clears the way for the FOMC to address a number of further questions regarding the remaining stages of balance-sheet normalization," Mr. Powell said.

The Fed's current system "https://www.wsj.com/articles/some-academics-warn-fed-on-risks-of-current-rate-control-tools-11545162881?mod=searchresults&page=3&pos=6". They see the rates the Fed is paying to banks and money funds as a de facto subsidy to those firms, and they worry that the higher rates go, the bigger the payout.

Others see the system as unwieldy and implemented incorrectly, citing the difficulty the Fed already has had in keeping the fed-funds rate in line. Some critics say the old system was best in part because everyone was experienced with it, it let markets do a lot of the work, and it provided the Fed and banks an insight into the health of other firms that wanted to trade reserves back and forth.

Still, the Fed has made its decision. Now the attention flows to a finalization of its balance-sheet plans.

U.S. Economy Is Slowing, Survey Says

The U.S. economy's brief flirtation with 3% growth "https://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-economy-is-slowing-survey-says-11548855001", economists say, cut short by a dimming global outlook, market tremors and sluggish business investment. Gross domestic product, or the total value of goods and services produced in the U.S., grew at a 2.6% annual rate in the fourth quarter, economists estimate in a Wall Street Journal survey conducted this week. Output will grow at a 1.8% clip in the first quarter and a 2.5% rate in the second quarter, according to the poll. That would average out to 2.3% growth for the nine-month period through this June not bad, but slower than the 3% growth notched in the year through last September.

Government Updates Timing for Data Releases Delayed by Shutdown

The U.S. Census Bureau"https://www.wsj.com/articles/government-updates-timing-for-data-releases-delayed-by-shutdown-11548863250" for some of its economic-data reports that were halted during the partial government shutdown, and it canceled other scheduled releases. Among the reports rescheduled: Data on November new-home sales will be published Thursday, and the report on November international trade will be released Feb. 6. The bureau canceled its November advance economic indicators report and preliminary steel report, both of which were scheduled for release last month.

Bank of Korea Chief: Fed More Dovish Than Expected

Bank of Korea Gov. Lee Ju-yeol expects the Fed's "more-dovish-than-expected" stance in Wednesday's statement and Jerome Powell's press conference to help stabilize South Korean financial markets. (Dow Jones Newswires)

Wells Fargo Is  Working Hard' to  Rebuild Trust'

By Emily Glazer

Wells Fargo & Co. on Wednesday "https://www.wsj.com/articles/wells-fargo-is-working-hard-to-rebuild-trust-11548886641?tesla=y" it has taken to address problems following a series of scandals that have resulted in more than $4 billion in fines and settlements since 2016 and tougher scrutiny from regulators.

The company again apologized for the problems that emerged in nearly all of its business lines, some of which resulted in improper customer charges and are still under government investigation.

"While we have made strong progress in our efforts to rebuild trust, our work to improve Wells Fargo will never be done," the bank said in a roughly 100-page report. Wells Fargo said it continues to review areas across the bank "to identify and remedy problems."

The report was in response to shareholder requests about 18 months ago. It addressed problems and resulting changes to the bank's culture, employee structure, risk management and board oversight.

Problems erupted at the San Francisco-based bank in September 2016, when it was disclosed that branch employees opened perhaps millions of fake accounts without customers' knowledge. Since then, problems have emerged in nearly every major business in the bank, from mortgage to foreign exchange to wealth management.

Wells Fargo remains under an unprecedented enforcement action from the Federal Reserve capping the bank's growth. Chief Executive Timothy Sloan said in January he expects the bank to remain under the cap through the rest of 2019, much longer than initially anticipated.

Wells Fargo said it is taking steps to improve inconsistencies in how it has handled customer complaints and refunds, which have added up to about $1 billion since the sales scandal. The Wall Street Journal previously reported problems with auto-loan customer refunds.

The bank said it would roll out a new system in the fourth quarter of 2019 to better handle customer complaints. It said it also has strengthened its platform for employees to anonymously report problems. Employees have said they have been retaliated against for reporting problems through that system over the years.

The report also detailed changes in compensation that encompass risk management, including after an executive leaves the bank.

The bank said it would better monitor and respond to regulatory warnings. Its audit group, under new leadership, will play a key role in challenging management on those actions, monitoring progress and other related matters. Wells Fargo received a regulatory knock in recent months focusing on its technology oversight, including concerns over a number of unresolved regulatory warnings.

"We acknowledge that we have not always met the expectations of some of our regulators in recent years and are working hard to address regulatory matters and rebuild trust," the report said.

Goldman Feasts at Expense of Food-Company Client, Suit Claims

By Gretchen Morgenson

Goldman Sachs Group Inc. "https://www.wsj.com/articles/goldman-feasts-at-expense-of-food-company-client-suit-claims-11548884825?tesla=y" ahead of that of a corporate client in advising on a wholesale-food company acquisition last year, a new lawsuit alleges.

The suit, filed by United Natural Foods Inc., accuses Goldman of improperly extracting more than $200 million in advising the Providence, R.I., food distributor on its $3 billion acquisition of grocery chain Supervalu Inc.

At issue is a roughly $2 billion financing loan Goldman arranged for United Natural on the deal. The company alleges that Goldman arranged the financing in a way that hurt United Natural but benefited the financial firm and its hedge-fund clients that had placed bets in the credit-default swap market against Supervalu. The company also accuses Goldman of taking advantage of the deal's provisions to extract more money from United Natural.

In a statement, a Goldman spokeswoman said the firm "believes that these claims are entirely without merit. We intend to vigorously defend ourselves against these accusations."

The suit mirrors questions raised by Congress and others after the financial crisis about Goldman's duty to clients. Goldman, which paid a $550 million penalty over a specific deal called Abacus, says it has since changed its operations and disclosures. The suit also raises fresh concerns about the potential for manipulation in the $10 trillion market for credit-default swaps, which are contracts designed to reduce the risk of losses when an entity defaults on its debt.

The suit, filed Wednesday in a New York state court, provides a glimpse of the allegedly aggressive tactics Goldman took with United Natural. When the company, which also had legal representation on the deal, objected to changes Goldman made in loan terms related to the deal, for example, it alleges that Stephan J. Feldgoise, Goldman's co-head of mergers for the Americas, warned the company that "things would get ugly."

Through the spokeswoman, Mr. Feldgoise declined to comment.

Goldman currently is embroiled in controversy over outsize fees it generated underwriting bonds issued by 1MDB, a Malaysian sovereign-wealth fund at the center of an international bribery scandal. Last month, prosecutors in Malaysia filed criminal charges against Goldman, citing securities-law violations.

Goldman has denied the 1MDB allegations and is fighting them.

Goldman's mergers-advisory unit and its bank were hired to work on the United Natural deal. The company initially agreed to pay Goldman's advisory unit $11.4 million and a Goldman bank unit $14.5 million, plus an additional $5.3 million for being lead arranger on the financing loan. If Goldman was unable to syndicate the loan, it and other lenders would have to fund the loan themselves.

Originally, the deal called for United Natural to retire Supervalu's $1.6 billion in debt. To investors who had bet against Supervalu in the credit-default swap market, this was disastrous they stood to lose big if the debt was extinguished. Some $470 million in Supervalu credit-default swaps was outstanding at the time of the deal, the suit said.

Instead of retiring the Supervalu debt, Goldman instead persuaded United Natural to add Supervalu as a co-borrower on the loan, the suit said, keeping Supervalu CDS trades alive.

Only after the deal closed, the suit contended, did United Natural learn that Goldman had hedge-fund clients holding the Supervalu CDS, which doubled in value when the deal terms added Supervalu as a co-borrower.

Some of those same hedge-fund clients also bought into the United Natural financing loan, the suit alleged.

As a result, United Natural said in the suit, those holders now have an incentive to "manufacture" a default on the United Natural debt to win their CDS bets against the company though no hedge-fund holder has done so.

Such so-called manufactured defaults are becoming more common in the CDS market, raising questions about the reliability of these instruments. Last year, the Commodity Futures Trading Commission said in a public statement that intentional defaults, created to generate profits on a CDS trade but unrelated to a company's financial health, could constitute market manipulation.

The suit against Goldman also alleges that the firm used its control over the financing to extract more money from United Natural.

A person familiar with Goldman's thinking said that structuring the financing as it did was necessary to get lenders' participation.

Under the financing provisions, Goldman could hike the loan's interest rate by 1.25 percentage points to make it more attractive to investors.

Another provision allowed Goldman to claim an additional $40.5 million from United Natural if it didn't have a full 15 days to fund the loan and was unsuccessful in raising the money.

Goldman marketed the loan against an Oct. 15 deadline, during a period of turmoil in the stock market. A few days before the deadline, the suit said, Goldman's Mr. Feldgoise told United Natural that Goldman was having difficulty attracting investors and asked for concessions.

When United Natural refused, the suit said, Mr. Feldgoise told United Natural's Chief Executive Steven Spinner that his company had forced Goldman into "full risk-mitigation mode."

Goldman then raised the interest rate on the loan by 1.5 percentage points, adding $183 million in interest costs over the life of the loan. The firm also contended there was a second marketing period on the loan, allowing it to add $40.5 million to the deal's costs, which Goldman withheld from the deal proceeds.

In the suit, United Natural alleged that Goldman "consolidated its command over all aspects of the transaction, enabling it to ensure its own profits to the detriment" of United Natural and its shareholders.

United Natural expected Goldman "to provide ethical counsel and unbiased support" of the deal, said Mr. Spinner, "not leverage their positions to pursue larger profits for themselves and other clients at our expense and ongoing damage."

New York Insurers Can Evaluate Your Social Media Use if They Can Prove Why It's Needed

By Leslie Scism

New York's top financial regulator is "https://www.wsj.com/articles/new-york-insurers-can-evaluate-your-social-media-useif-they-can-prove-why-its-needed-11548856802?tesla=y" life insurers to use data from social media and other nontraditional sources when setting premium rates, though the insurers will have to prove the information doesn't unfairly discriminate against certain customers.

New York is the first state to set specific guidance governing how life insurers use algorithms to comb through everything from homeownership records to credit scores and internet use in an effort to size up an applicant's risk. At least a couple dozen insurers are employing these automated programs to speed up the buying process, facilitate online life-insurance issuance and boost stagnant policy sales.

The regulator's goal, according to New York Financial Services Superintendent Maria T. Vullo, is to establish ground rules before the use of this information becomes more widespread.

"Because this is a rapidly evolving area in insurance underwriting, it was important for the department to create general principles now," she said in an interview.

Under guidance released earlier this month, life insurers will have to use statistical and actuarial analysis to determine that any algorithms and data are free of bias against racial minorities and other groups protected by law. Insurers can't accept an outside vendor's claim that its process is fair, the department said.

The guidance applies to companies operating in New York, but industry consultants said it could have an impact beyond the state's borders. New York has one of the largest insurance departments in the state-regulated industry. Regulators issue new guidance when they want to clarify how new developments affect existing laws.

"Everybody will pay attention," said Tom Scales, a practice leader for life insurance at Celent, an insurance-technology research and advice firm that is part of Marsh & McLennan Co s.

Trade groups said they are studying the directive. Insurers would "continue to explore ways to help more Americans benefit from financial protection," said Mary Griffin, president of the Life Insurance Council of New York. "Algorithmic underwriting can make it easier and quicker for people to get coverage in place."

New York's guidance follows an 18-month investigation in which regulators queried 160 life insurers about their practices. The department's regulators found a range of data in use: homeownership records, credit information, educational attainment, civil judgments, licensures and other public filings.

Only one insurer out of the 160 said it was using social media, retail purchases or internet activities in underwriting, according to a person familiar with the department's findings.

The department also learned that some vendors are pitching data to insurers that includes "condition or type of an applicant's electronic devices" and "how the consumer appears in a photograph," it said in material posted on its website.

"All have the potential to reflect disguised and illegal race-based underwriting," the department said of the various categories of nontraditional data. New York insurance laws have long prohibited the use of race in underwriting.

Consultants said the use of social media in underwriting decisions is limited so far. "The data exists and is being collected, but the quality and the integrity of that data is not yet demonstrated to be accurate enough to be an underwriting input at this time," said Ari Chester, a partner at McKinsey & Co.

Instead, many insurers are relying on a trove of public information. LexisNexis Risk Solutions said more than 30 insurers are using a model it developed that draws on motor-vehicle records; bankruptcy, criminal and other court documents; professional licenses; college-attendee records, property deeds and tax filings; liens and evictions and other records.

The tool doesn't use anything from social media, said Elliott Wallace, a LexisNexis Risk Solutions vice president. LexisNexis, he added, is "comfortable with the department scrutinizing and looking at our models."

Ms. Vullo's department said information that is directly related to medical conditions such as prescription-drug databases is permissible, as are motor-vehicle reports and some criminal matters. Traffic fatalities are a leading cause of death for younger people.

Other regulators are also keeping an eye on developments with life insurers. The National Association of Insurance Commissioners, a standards-setting group for state insurance departments, has a "Big Data Working Group" that is in early stages of focusing on life insurers' accelerated underwriting efforts, among other issues.

Malaysia's Securities Commission Fines Deloitte Over Breaches Tied to 1MDB

By Yantoultra Ngui and Samuel Rubenfeld

Malaysia's securities regulator "https://www.wsj.com/articles/malaysias-securities-commission-fines-deloitte-over-breaches-tied-to-1mdb-11548882601?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=1" on Deloitte PLT for four breaches related to an Islamic bond program issued by a unit of state investment fund 1Malaysia Development Bhd., or 1MDB.

The penalties mark the first time action has been taken against an auditing firm over 1MDB in Malaysia. Billions were siphoned from the fund and spread around the world on real estate, art, a superyacht and other assets.

Since Prime Minister Mahathir Mohamad came into office last May, Malaysia has reopened and accelerated probes related to the financial scandals surrounding 1MDB, which is also under investigation in several other countries, including the U.S. and Switzerland.

The penalties on Deloitte also come amid heightened scrutiny over the quality of audits by the Big Four accounting firms.

Deloitte failed to discharge its statutory obligation despite discovering irregularities when auditing the 2.4 billion-ringgit ($583.8 million) program issued by the unit, called Bandar Malaysia Sdn Bhd., the Securities Commission said. The regulator fined Deloitte 2.2 million ringgit, or about $535,000.

Representatives of Deloitte in Malaysia and the U.S. didn't immediately respond to requests for comment.

Deloitte was the statutory auditor for Bandar Malaysia and its parent, 1MDB Real Estate Sdn Bhd., now known as TRX City Sdn Bhd., for their fiscal years ending March 31, 2015 and 2016.

Bandar Malaysia was involved in the development of a transportation hub project in Kuala Lumpur for a high-speed rail link with Singapore. The project was subsequently suspended following the change in government.

The U.S. probe of 1MDB has led to criminal charges against fugitive Malaysian businessman Jho Low, the suspected mastermind of the financial frauds at 1MDB, as well as against a former banker at Goldman Sachs. Another former Goldman banker has pleaded guilty. The bank is under a U.S. investigation; it has said it was misled by 1MDB officials and will fight the charges.

Goldman's chief executive has apologized to the people of Malaysia for its role in the scandal, but the country's finance minister rejected the apology and demanded $7.5 billion in penalties to resolve criminal charges against the investment bank filed in Malaysia.

The Big Four accounting firms have come under increasing scrutiny recently over the quality of their audits, despite reporting in 2018 their strongest revenue growth since at least the financial crisis.

U.K. regulators late last year proposed sweeping changes to rules governing British audit firms.

In the U.S., audit regulators said they had found serious deficiencies in nearly half the KPMG LLP audits they scrutinized in their last two years of inspections. KPMG said audit quality "has been and continues to be a top priority across all levels" of the firm.

PricewaterhouseCoopers LLP last year was ordered to pay $625.3 million in damages for failing to catch a fraud scheme that caused one of the biggest bank failures of the financial crisis. The firm at the time said it was disappointed with the ruling and would file an appeal; litigation in the case is ongoing, according to court records.

And Ernst & Young LLP last week said it was creating an independent panel to advise it on how to improve its audits.

High-Wire Audit Looms as Regulators Scrutinize KPMG and GE

By David Benoit

Accounting giant KPMG LLP has been blessing the books of General Electric Co. for 110 years, but the audit currently under way "https://www.wsj.com/articles/kpmgs-high-wire-ge-audit-11548856801?tesla=y" for both companies.

KPMG is under fire for a string of audit failures and scandals, highlighted again Friday in a set of scathing reports by the nation's top accounting regulator, which is demanding KPMG increase its skepticism and improve the quality of work, particularly at big, complicated clients like GE.

A tough audit is the last thing GE needs right now. Investors have already been concerned about more unknowns bubbling up. The conglomerate is trying to increase cash, pare debt and return to growth. More bad news could hamper those efforts or provide evidence for investigators from the Securities and Exchange Commission and the Justice Department, which are probing GE's accounting decisions.

GE is set to release its fourth-quarter numbers on Thursday. As with most quarterly earnings, those numbers won't be officially audited. But, in one of the biggest and most lucrative audits of any company, hundreds of KPMG partners and staff are poring over GE's books in preparation for the company's annual audited results, likely released in late February.

KPMG hasn't been named in the GE investigations, but the Public Company Accounting Oversight Board, or PCAOB, released several reports Friday calling into question KPMG's work on similar issues.

The reports said PCAOB found problems in nearly half of the 103 KPMG audits it inspected from 2016 and 2017 and said improvements it ordered in prior years hadn't been made. The regulator also disclosed new details on the impact of leaks from the PCAOB about what audits would be reviewed, which helped KPMG prepare for the annual inspections.

KPMG said in a letter to PCAOB that it has taken significant steps, including management and board changes, to improve its processes.

A spokesman said it couldn't comment on GE specifically but that "We are confident that our audits and reviews were appropriately performed in accordance with applicable professional standards, and we stand behind our work."

The PCAOB reports don't name specific clients of the auditor, and it wasn't clear if the regulator had inspected or found any issues with KPMG's work related to GE, which said it doesn't believe its audit is involved.

But the reports criticize how KPMG policed its clients over the accounting questions that are central to the GE probes: How did GE decide when to book revenue and how to value assets, and why were the assumptions behind those decisions too optimistic?

GE has said it is cooperating with the probes and that it hasn't found evidence of wrongdoing. The company said it would consider new auditors for 2020.

GE has said the government investigators are looking at how it booked revenue from service contracts in its power business, which builds turbines used by electricity generators and sells longtime contracts to service them.

In the PCAOB reports, the most frequent area of trouble was KPMG's work on how clients decided to book revenue, which came up 18 times in 103 audits.

The investigators are also looking at a $22 billion write-down GE took on its 2015 acquisition of French company Alstom 's power business.

The PCAOB four times faulted KPMG for the work it did while checking clients' assumptions when assigning value to acquired assets.

In another thread of the auditing probes, GE is facing scrutiny over a $15 billion increase to its reserves for long-term care insurance policies, which cover care like nursing homes and proved more expensive than much of the industry expected. GE has said the need for more reserves was discovered following a sudden spike in claims that differed from historical trends.

In the 2017 report, the PCAOB cited KPMG for failing to adequately test an insurance client's use of historical data to estimate future insurance claims.

For KPMG, the report was the latest blow to its reputation amid many scandals, including the leak from the PCAOB about audit reviews. Three people, including a former KPMG partner, have pleaded guilty to criminal charges; two other former partners still face charges.

The regulator said that leak led to a review of 10 new KPMG audits from 2016. The inspections turned up problems in nine of those audits.

No matter what happens with the SEC and Justice Department investigations or the current audit, KPMG's bottom line is already likely to take a hit.

GE paid KPMG $142.9 million in 2017, the biggest total audit fee for any company regulated by the SEC, according to Audit Analytics. The fee was higher than in recent years due to work done on GE's efforts to break off assets. GE has been one of the 10 most lucrative audits in each of the past five years, Audit Analytics data said.

The GE audit was so important to KPMG that roughly 400 partners worked on it last year, according to GE's financial disclosures. But the importance of GE to KPMG raised questions among investors about auditor independence, and whether the accounting firm would stand up to GE's aggressive accounting maneuvers.

The pressure was clear inside KPMG, where people who worked there said a well-known phrase among executives was, "you don't want to be the partner who lost the GE account."

 Thomas Gryta contributed to this article.

Powell Now Owns the Fed's Balance-Sheet Problem

Arguably, Fed Chairman Jerome Powell's most significant statement on Wednesday came when he "https://www.wsj.com/articles/powell-now-owns-the-feds-balance-sheet-problem-11548881952?tesla=y" on the central bank's balance-sheet normalization, Spencer Jakab writes for The Wall Street Journal. "The signal was that so-called quantitative tightening would continue for now but end sooner than expected. Moreover, he also raised the possibility that the balance sheet could be  an active tool' in the future if warranted in other words, more bond purchases if markets or the economy cry out for help. Until recently, Fed officials had been insisting the balance-sheet shrinkage was on autopilot. The new stance also is a huge shift from Mr. Powell's stance as a Fed governor back in January 2013...He warned at the time...that the policies risked  driving securities above fundamental values' and that  there is every reason to expect a sharp and painful correction.' With stocks nearly entering a bear market following the December meeting, it seems he was right. Now, though, taming the Fed's monster balance sheet is Mr. Powell's problem."

Stock Exchanges to Fire Company Building Stock-Market Supercomputer; Finra to take over the Consolidated Audit Trail from Thesys Technologies

By Dave Michaels 
661 words
31 January 2019
17:41
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

WASHINGTON Stock exchanges intend to fire the contractor they hired to build a data warehouse for all U.S. stock-market activity, the latest sign of trouble for a project designed to detect trading fraud and causes behind wild swings in prices.

People familiar with the matter said the exchanges have lost confidence in Thesys Technologies LLC, a startup "https://www.wsj.com/articles/thesys-to-build-system-tracking-all-stock-and-options-orders-1484678642", known as the Consolidated Audit Trail. The Securities and Exchange Commission told the exchanges to create the database so it would have a complete picture of orders in the stock and options markets, a tool they have never had.

In November, Thesys "https://www.wsj.com/articles/stock-market-supercomputer-to-launch-with-glitches-1542236446" one year late and without the functionality that was expected.

The exchanges overseeing the contract confirmed Thursday that they are switching to a new vendor. The group said Thesys "is providing necessary services through the transition period." The exchanges are evaluating how the switch will affect coming deadlines, including one that calls for the biggest brokerage firms to report orders to the data warehouse this year.

The Financial Industry Regulatory Authority, which regulates U.S. broker-dealers, is expected to take over the project, according to the people familiar with the matter. The committee of exchanges doesn't have a signed agreement with Finra yet, the people said. A Finra spokesman declined to comment.

Thesys said the decision to end the contract was mutual. "The parties have irreconcilable differences," said Silvia Davi, a Thesys spokeswoman.

The SEC mandated in 2012 that stock and options exchanges build the audit trail. The SEC had been embarrassed by its dearth of market data following the May 2010 flash crash. It took the federal regulator over four months to piece together causes of that episode.

The SEC, which declined to comment Thursday, announced earlier this week that it hired a czar to coordinate its oversight of the project. The employee, Manisha Kimmel, is an expert in trading technology who previously led a consortium of Wall Street banks and broker-dealers that implement complex technology solutions.

SEC Chairman Jay Clayton has said several times that he was disappointed in the project's delays. Mr. Clayton said this week that Ms. Kimmel "will further enhance the commission's ability to effectively oversee" the exchanges' work on the audit trail.

New York-based Thesys is a provider of high-speed trading platforms and analytics software. It beat out two other finalists seeking to build the supercomputer. Finra was initially seen as the favorite to win the contract, but the New York Stock Exchange favored Thesys, people familiar with the matter said.

The relationship between the exchanges and Thesys has been strained, with disputes over how much money Thesys was owed for the work it has done. Work also "https://www.wsj.com/articles/trading-database-could-be-delayed-due-to-data-security-hitches-1507248613" by conflict over the technical specifications of the database.

The executives who founded Thesys previously ran a trading company, Tradeworx, that provided the SEC with a data feed capturing most exchange-based activity. The system, known as the Market Information Data Analytics System, doesn't capture orders that occur on private trading venues known as dark pools. Only about 62% of equities trading occurs on fully regulated exchanges.

Finra already operates surveillance programs that are similar to the audit trail, although their databases don't see some activity that occurs away from exchanges and regulated over-the-counter venues.

The data warehouse has also had to contend with concerns it could become a prime target for hackers. The repository will include order data that could allow sophisticated third parties to piece together an institution's trading strategy or portfolio mix. It also will include data about the individual customers behind trades, information that has typically been known only to brokers.

New Details on Amazon, Berkshire Hathaway, JPMorgan Health Venture Emerge in Court Battle; Health-care venture may build its own solutions, court filings in UnitedHealth's lawsuit against a former executive reveal

BOSTON Health giant UnitedHealth Group Inc. went to court Wednesday to try to stop a former executive from working for a company it sees as a competitor, the "https://www.wsj.com/articles/amazon-berkshire-hathaway-jpmorgan-to-partner-on-health-care-1517315659" by Amazon.com Inc., Berkshire Hathaway Inc. and JPMorgan Chase & Co.

The scope of the still-unnamed venture remains hazy beyond stated goals to improve health care and rein in costs for employees. But filings related to the case and court testimony offer some fresh clues about the "https://www.wsj.com/articles/triple-threat-amazon-berkshire-jpmorgan-rattle-health-care-firm-1517359502", including that it may build its own solutions if what it needs isn't available elsewhere.

In court, the new hire at issue said the venture is focused on serving the three founding companies and isn't competing with UnitedHealth's Optum health-services unit to sell services or products. Court filings indicate the venture is examining third-party vendors' products and combining them in ways to boost value for the founding companies.

Related

* "https://www.wsj.com/articles/amazon-berkshire-hathaway-jpmorgan-to-partner-on-health-care-1517315659" (January 2018)

Optum filed a lawsuit in Boston federal court this month seeking a temporary restraining order to keep former employee David W. Smith from working at the new venture or sharing trade secrets.

Optum alleged in its lawsuit that Mr. Smith, who spent 2 years working in Optum's corporate strategy and product units before resigning last month, violated a noncompete agreement by joining the new venture. Optum also accused Mr. Smith of improperly accessing confidential documents before departing for the new venture, which filings referred to as ABC.

Mr. Smith denied the allegations in court filings and court testimony, saying he didn't leave Optum with any company files and doesn't want or have any use for that information in his current job at ABC. Mr. Smith has asked the court to order arbitration.

During the daylong hearing, Mr. Smith described his new job, started this month, as focused on analyzing the employee patient pool among the three founding companies, which he said have significant health-care needs.

When U.S. District Judge Mark L. Wolf asked whether any innovative ideas would be scaled up and sold to the broader market, Mr. Smith said this wasn't on the near-term horizon.

"That's not the near-term goal," Mr. Smith said. He agreed when the judge asked if ABC could be a laboratory for finding new ways to deliver affordable care, but also "it could be the great solutions are already out there and it's about pulling out the right ones together."

The judge didn't rule on the restraining order request Wednesday, and the hearing is set to continue Thursday.

Jack Stoddard, the joint venture's chief operating officer, also offered some details of the new entity's operations in an affidavit filed to support Mr. Smith.

The venture is "currently using data, analytics, and expertise to combine products from third-party vendors," potentially including Optum, Mr. Stoddard said, and "if viable offerings or solutions do not exist in the market, ABC may consider building new solutions" or having vendors create them. He also said the venture isn't "profit-seeking."

The new venture is "evaluating potential health care solutions for the [founding companies'] over 1.2 million employees," said Mr. Stoddard in the filing.

Amazon, Berkshire Hathaway and JPMorgan launched their health-care venture a year ago with a high-profile announcement that drove down the share values of a number of large health-care companies, including UnitedHealth, which is also the parent of the biggest U.S. health insurer.

Limited details have emerged about the new venture's potential scope. The "https://www.wsj.com/articles/amazon-berkshire-and-jpmorgan-pick-ceo-for-new-health-care-company-1529504379" naming prominent surgeon and researcher Atul Gawande as the venture's chief executive was another clue, but Dr. Gawande and others he has hired have kept their plans under wraps.

Optum, for its part, said in its lawsuit the new venture is a potentially significant rival. The startup is immediately poised to win health-services business from JPMorgan and Berkshire Hathaway, both current Optum clients, Optum said. The venture could become a major competitor in the longer run, the UnitedHealth unit claimed, by seeking business from other companies.

A lawyer for Mr. Smith argued during the court hearing that Optum's lawsuit aimed to intimidate its own workers and a perceived business threat.

Banking & Finance: New York Insurers to Comb Social Media

By Leslie Scism 
693 words
31 January 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B10
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

New York's top financial regulator is going to allow life insurers to use data from social media and other nontraditional sources when setting premium rates, though the insurers will have to prove the information doesn't unfairly discriminate against certain customers.

New York is the first state to set specific guidance governing how life insurers use algorithms to comb through everything from homeownership records to credit scores and internet use in an effort to size up an applicant's risk. At least a couple dozen insurers are employing these automated programs to speed up the buying process, facilitate online life-insurance issuance and boost stagnant policy sales.

The regulator's goal, according to New York Financial Services Superintendent Maria T. Vullo, is to establish ground rules before the use of this information becomes more widespread.

"Because this is a rapidly evolving area in insurance underwriting, it was important for the department to create general principles now," she said in an interview.

Under guidance released this month, life insurers will have to use statistical and actuarial analysis to determine that any algorithms and data are free of bias against racial minorities and other groups protected by law. Insurers can't accept an outside vendor's claim that its process is fair, the department said.

The guidance applies to companies operating in New York, but industry consultants said it could have an impact beyond the state's borders. New York has one of the largest insurance departments in the state-regulated industry. Regulators issue new guidance when they want to clarify how new developments affect existing laws.

"Everybody will pay attention," said Tom Scales, a practice leader for life insurance at Celent, an insurance-technology research and advice firm that is part of Marsh & McLennan Cos.

Trade groups said they are studying the directive. Insurers would "continue to explore ways to help more Americans benefit from financial protection," said Mary Griffin, president of the Life Insurance Council of New York. "Algorithmic underwriting can make it easier and quicker for people to get coverage in place."

New York's guidance follows an 18-month investigation in which regulators queried 160 life insurers about their practices. The department's regulators found a range of data in use: homeownership records, credit information, educational attainment, civil judgments, licensures and other public filings.

Only one insurer out of the 160 said it was using social media, retail purchases or internet activities in underwriting, according to a person familiar with the department's findings.

The department also learned that some vendors are pitching data to insurers that includes "condition or type of an applicant's electronic devices" and "how the consumer appears in a photograph," it said in material posted on its website.

"All have the potential to reflect disguised and illegal race-based underwriting," the department said of the various categories of nontraditional data.

Consultants said the use of social media in underwriting decisions is limited so far. "The data exists and is being collected, but the quality and the integrity of that data is not yet demonstrated to be accurate enough to be an underwriting input at this time," said Ari Chester, a partner at McKinsey & Co.

Instead, many insurers are relying on a trove of public information. LexisNexis Risk Solutions said more than 30 insurers are using a model it developed that draws on motor-vehicle records; bankruptcy, criminal and other court documents; professional licenses; college-attendee records, property deeds and tax filings; liens and evictions and other records.

Ms. Vullo's department said information that is directly related to medical conditions -- such as prescription-drug databases -- is permissible, as are motor-vehicle reports and some criminal matters. Traffic fatalities are a leading cause of death for younger people.

Other regulators are also keeping an eye on developments with life insurers. The National Association of Insurance Commissioners, a standards-setting group for state insurance departments, has a "Big Data Working Group" that is in early stages of focusing on life insurers' accelerated underwriting efforts, among other issues.

Apple teams with insurer Aetna to build health app

TIM BRADSHAW ; OLIVER RALPH 
496 words
30 January 2019
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa
15
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Technology

Apple will gain access to a huge trove of health data as part of an Apple Watch tie-up with US insurer Aetna.

CVS-owned Aetna and Apple have jointly developed an app called Attain, set to launch in the coming months. Attain will use the Apple Watch to provide personalised recommendations to users, based on their health history and live data generated by the watch, which offers heart-rate monitoring and workout tracking.

To comply with US data protection rules, Apple and Aetna have entered into a "business associate agreement" that includes assurances that no personally identifiable information will be shared with the iPhone maker and that it will not be sold, revealed to employers or used for making coverage decisions by Aetna.

The health information will be collected on a voluntary basis and only used to support the Attain programme, the companies added.

The deal marks the latest incursion by Silicon Valley into the healthcare sector, while insurance companies look to technology to improve preventive care that can ultimately reduce their payouts.

Tim Cook, Apple chief executive, said this month he believed Apple's "greatest contribution to mankind" would come from its work in healthcare.

Other tech companies share that ambition. Last year, Amazon acquired online pharmacy PillPack and partnered with JPMorgan Chase and Berkshire Hathaway to create a not-forprofit healthcare company for employees. Earlier this month, Alphabet's life sciences unit Verily raised $1bn from investors including Silver Lake to expand its work in healthcare.

Using Attain will be voluntary for Aetna members but they will be provided incentives to join, including earning back the cost of the Apple Watch that is required to participate. Spurring sales of wearable devices including Apple Watch and AirPods is becoming more important for Apple at a time when iPhone revenues are slowing.

Users will be able to opt in to sharing Attain data and their broader health history with Apple, providing fuel for analytics and machine learning that the companies need to improve the service. The companies said that users' health data will be encrypted and will not be used for underwriting or calculating premiums.

"We believe that people should be able to play a more active role in managing wellbeing," said Jeff Williams, Apple's chief operating officer.

Insurance companies are hoping to use technology more broadly as a way to prevent losses from occurring.

For example, they aim to use "internet of things" technology in the home such as leak detectors that can alert homeowners at the first sign of a problem. And motor insurers have for some years installed so-called black boxes in cars to analyse how well the driver is performing.

They hope that such schemes will not only cut payouts but also lead to improved customer loyalty, which is a big problem in an industry where many people choose their insurer based only on price.

Cyber Daily: Angry Shareholders to Seek Proof of Prudent Cyber Tactics

1,369 words
29 January 2019
12:29
WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Cyber Daily: Angry Shareholders to Seek Proof of Prudent Cyber Tactics

By Kim S. Nash

Good day. Senior executives, along with members of the board, are more vulnerable to charges of shirking fiduciary responsibilities now that top leaders at the former Yahoo Inc. agreed to pay $29 million to settle claims from shareholders who accused them of such violations, reports WSJ Pro Cybersecurity's Adam Janofsky.

Shareholders, in a lawsuit filed a year ago, alleged that Yahoo leaders knew about data breaches at the company far earlier than they let on and didn't take steps to mitigate or stop harm. The monetary settlement is a first for this kind of lawsuit, Mr. Janofsky reports, and could open the way for similar lawsuits against companies after they report breaches. Top corporate leaders should be able to prove they have taken the right cybersecurity precautions at their companies and stay involved in cyber planning and response.

**********

Directors, Top Executives Vulnerable to Cyber Lawsuits after Yahoo Settlement

By Adam Janofsky

The Jan. 4 settlement of a shareholder lawsuit against Yahoo Inc. related to large data breaches makes way for lawsuits against corporate executives and directors at firms that experience significant cyberattacks, according to Nathan Owens, a partner at Newmeyer & Dillion LLP.

Former directors and officers at Yahoo earlier this month agreed to pay $29 million to a group of shareholders to settle claims that they mishandled a series of data breaches that occurred mainly in 2013 and 2014 and were disclosed in 2016 and 2017. The shareholders charged that Yahoo directors and leaders shrugged off their fiduciary duties and knew of at least some of the breaches in 2015.

Verizon Communications Inc. bought some of Yahoo for $4.48 billion in June 2017. The remainder of the company is known as Altaba Inc.

Shareholder lawsuits following data breaches at other companies have resulted in changes to corporate governance and cybersecurity policies. The Yahoo settlement is the first time such a lawsuit has brought about a monetary payment, said Mr. Owens.

"What's different here is that there's money changing hands, and in this case it was pretty sizable," he said. "It sends a message that it's no longer going to be a slap on the wrist, and that there is real exposure here." Last year, the Securities and Exchange Commission issued a $35 million penalty against Altaba related to the handling of the Yahoo breaches, saying that Yahoo knew of at least one serious intrusion in 2014.

Mr. Owens discussed with WSJ Pro Cybersecurity why companies should expect to see more such lawsuits and how directors and executives can protect themselves. Here are edited excerpts from the conversation.

Q:

How surprising is this settlement?

A:

These types of cases -- called derivative suits -- happen with some regularity. With situations like Enron, shareholders say:  You guys lied on your Securities and Exchange Commission filings, so when we were buying your stock it wasn't as valuable as you made it sound.' That's often what you see at the start of a derivative claim.

But we haven't seen a whole lot of these following a cyberattack, and we haven't seen any of those result in a cash settlement. The floodgates have opened now.

Q:

What are the lessons here for directors and executives?

A:

The biggest takeaway is that you need to protect yourself. This settlement is going to encourage lawsuits even when the leadership has done what they need to do. They need to have a good defense against claims that they didn't act prudently. So directors and officers must have a good cybersecurity protection plan in place, be aware of what's going on in the cybersecurity program, and involve themselves in that process.

Companies typically have directors and officers liability insurance, which protects against claims that they did not act prudently. But depending on how it's written, D&O policies may not provide coverage for this kind of claim. When you get into a new area like cybersecurity, you sometimes find out the hard way that you don't always have coverage. You're going to see companies ensure that their D&O policy covers this kind of situation. Fortunately for the directors and officers at Yahoo, theirs did. But if there isn't insurance, these people are going to have to write a check.

**********

U.S. Authorities Unveil Sweeping Set of Charges Against China's Huawei

The Trump administration unveiled a sweeping set of criminal charges against China's Huawei Technologies Co. in its latest salvo against the telecom giant, with authorities unsealing a pair of indictments just days before U.S.-China trade talks are set to resume, The Wall Street Journal reports.

The U.S. says: Federal prosecutors accused Huawei of violating U.S. sanctions on Iran and of stealing trade secrets from a U.S. company. The move includes charges against Huawei's chief financial officer, Meng Wanzhou, who is being held in Vancouver after Canadian authorities arrested the executive because of a U.S. extradition request.

Chinese retaliation? The U.S.'s escalating, global campaign against Huawei has drawn in allies and enraged Beijing, which has detained a series of foreign citizens in recent weeks on suspicion of endangering China's national security in what analysts view as retaliatory measures. Chinese officials have denied any links between these actions and the pressure on Huawei, and the Chinese embassy in Washington didn't provide comment on the latest charges against the company. Huawei didn't provide comment and in the past denied accusations of spying and violating laws.

Bonuses for theft. In one indictment, the U.S. accused Huawei of stealing information from T-Mobile US Inc. about a phone-testing robot, "Tappy." Huawei offered bonuses to employees who were successful in stealing confidential information from other companies, U.S. prosecutors alleged.

**********

Big Number

$1 Billion

Sum that two unidentified hacker groups likely have stolen in cryptocurrency thefts in recent years, according to Chainalysis, which makes software that tracks cryptocurrency transactions.

**********

More Cyber News

U.S. military lagging in cybersecurity development. The cyber capabilities of the U.S. military aren't improving at a pace on par with the advancement of adversaries, Bloomberg reports, citing a report it obtained from Pentagon's combat testing office. Artificial intelligence and other tools are speeding up cyberattacks and making them more precise and harder to detect. A shortage of relevant expertise affects the Pentagon's ability to assess and respond to threats, the annual report says. The Government Accountability Office said last year the Department of Defense faces challenges in securing weapons systems.

Facebook to fight interference in Europe's elections. Facebook Inc. plans new efforts to fend off interference in the European Union's parliamentary election campaign this spring, The Wall Street Journal reports.

Database of political ads: Facebook's effort is one of the largest and most complex tests of the company's response to alleged Russian interference in the U.S.'s 2016 presidential election. One step is creating a database of political ads will include details such as the budget, number of people reached and demographic data on the people it reached. The database will be searchable for up to seven years.

HIV status of 14,000 people leaked in Singapore breach. An American man leaked the personal records and medical status of more than 14,000 people diagnosed in Singapore with HIV, WSJ reports. The names, contact details, HIV status and test results of 5,400 Singaporeans and 8,800 foreigners diagnosed with the virus through 2013 were stolen and published online by the man, Mikhy K. Ferrera Brochez, who previously worked in Singapore, Health Ministry officials said Monday. The breach was discovered on Jan. 22.

Startups add cyber defenses sooner. Pressure from investors and potential corporate customers is spurring technology startups to add cybersecurity features to their products sooner in the development lifecycle than they have in the past, CyberScoop reports. Companies are asking more details questions about a startup's capabilities as worries about supply-chain attacks force more scrutiny of business partners.
