Banks and fintechs remain divided on payments despite public love-in

Laura Noonan 
1,525 words
28 January 2019
20:47
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Your essential weekly fintech briefing

Amid all the warm words about the benefits of fintechs and big banks co-operating more closely, in reality such happy harmony remains hard to achieve, as highlighted by the delays plaguing efforts to introduce a modern, real-time payments system in the US.

There was another Kumbaya moment for fintech and big banks at the World Economic Forum in Davos last week, as leading execs from PayPal and Mastercard espoused the benefits of everyone coming together to work for a greater payments system.

 The role banks play I think is really critically important,  gushed PayPal chief operating officer Bill Ready, describing the  massive amount of interest  companies like his have in partnering with banks so they can collectively  move much more quickly .

 There s a much greater objective here than saying let s split the pie that s here today,  he added, to the  absolute  agreement of his fellow panellist, Mastercard vice-chairman Ann Cairns.

Companies like Mr Ready s PayPal and Ms Cairn s Mastercard, which also owns Vocalink   which is behind the UK's real-time Faster Payments system   are growing much much faster than banks. In the US, for example, the top four banks increased revenues by an average of 3 per cent last year while the top four payments companies, led by Mastercard and PayPal, increased revenues by an average of 15 per cent.

But in the past few years, non-banks have become increasingly attuned to the virtues of their regulated competitors.

 (It s) that trust thing,  as Ms Cairns put it to delegates at the Alpine resort.  Who do you ultimately want to leave your money with? And that s the big question, regardless of how you re moving it around. 

Unlike fintechs, banks in many parts of the world offer the comfort of state-backed deposit guarantees.

From a consumer perspective, deeper co-operation between banks and fintechs would be welcome if it can speed up the pace of innovation, especially in the US where it still takes days for cheques and wire transfers to clear, payments shut down at the weekend and technologies like contactless card transactions are still in their infancy.

But the talk of common purpose has so far not been matched by action.

The Federal Reserve s payments gurus are currently reviewing an avalanche of suggestions from banks, fintechs, card companies, consumer groups and anyone else with an interest in overhauling the US s antiquated payments system.

The Fed s objective is to dramatically reduce how long it takes for workers to cash their pay cheques and for businesses to reap the rewards for their sales. The suggested fix is a real-time 24/7 payments system rather than the current one that operates only during business hours.

It is a laudable goal, and one which both banks and fintechs embrace, but the path ahead is a fraught one.

Banks, fintechs and just about everyone else agrees that universal adoption of a new faster payments infrastructure is key.  If you don t have ubiquity, you don t have a solution,  says a senior payments banker at one of America s biggest banks.

The problem?  There s a lot of confusion over who the dominant network should be,  says one fintech executive, lamenting the Fed s failure to take a  leadership role  and tell everyone in the payments ecosystem to back a single horse.

The Fed is still considering whether it should create faster payments infrastructure itself, or if a new system should be built around a private sector solution.

Craig Maurer, analyst at Autonomous, points out that The Clearing House launched the ACH faster payments network in 2017, but the system is still not used for retail payments   its market is interbank transfers, bill payments and other banking functions.

Since The Clearing House is owned by just 26 banks, Mr Maurer says  clearly the preference would be to see the Fed build something ubiquitous, that all banks have to use, and that they can influence .

Meanwhile back in Davos, on the same panel as Mr Ready and Ms Cairns, FundingCircle co-founder and CEO Samir Desai offered a contrarian argument to the folksy notion of bank and fintechs having their ebony and ivory moment.

 We re not as big as PayPal or Mastercard so a partnership between Funding Circle and a large bank is not necessarily a partnership of equals,  he said.

Funding Circle listed its shares in London last year and has a market cap north of 1bn. If even Mr Desai is wary of getting a raw deal from a big banks, where does that leave the average fintech?

One to watch: Thought Machine

The back-end plumbing powering banks  products is hardly the flashiest area of fintech, but it is one that is attracting growing amounts of interest. Lloyds Banking Group s ambitious plans for Thought Machine   a start-up it recently invested in   help show why, writes Nicholas Megaw.

Thought Machine s  Vault  core banking platform promises to be cheaper and easier to scale than the decades-old systems currently in use at most big banks, while stronger data analytics and smart contracts will let banks offer new, more tailored products.

It is one of several new companies in the space   rivals include Germany s Mambu, Ireland s Leveris and in the UK 10x Future Technologies, led by ex-Barclays chief Antony Jenkins.

Lloyds is planning to start small with the Vault platform, testing it by moving data on 500,000 customers from one of its discontinued brands, according to documents seen by the FT. But if all goes well it could be rolled out across Lloyds  business, a move the bank reckons could save it upwards of 750m a year   not a bad return from a 10m investment.

Still, if Thought Machine provides a good example of the potential opportunities in the sector, it also shows that the journey for new providers will not necessarily be plain sailing. The original company went into administration in 2017, and the group spent more than a year battling legal action from Capco, the consultancy. Capco, which was then owned by established banking technology provider FIS, had accused a former employee of stealing technology that it said was eventually used by Thought Machine. The lawsuit was settled out of court last September.

Number of the week: $20.5bn value of fintech investment in China last year according to new research from Fintech Global

Further fintech fascination

Regulators advance As bitcoin and other cryptocurrencies languish close to all-time lows, the UK s financial watchdog has decided that now is a good time to consult on how to protect consumers from the  substantial risks  of unauthorised sellers. The Financial Conduct Authority is worried that ads for crypto products are too glowing, and don t make the risks clear enough, and so the regulator plans to consult on further protections later in the year. Whether bitcoin will be worth anything at all by then remains to be seen.

Fundraising UK online wealth management pioneer Nutmeg has won another 45m from investors including Goldman Sachs and Hong Kong-based financial advisory group Convoy, paving the way for its Hong Kong launch later this year. Nutmeg has raised 116m since its 2012 launch, but has yet to turn a profit, despite growing to become the eight-largest wealth manager in the UK. This isn t the only online wealth bet Goldman is making of course   the Wall Street giant said in October that it would merge its online bank Marcus with its investment management arm, triggering speculation Goldman would launch its own robo adviser.

Trend watch Chinese peer-to-peer lenders listing in the US seem to be getting the cold shoulder from Wall Street. Bloomberg reports that big US banks including Goldman Sachs and Citigroup have walked away from US initial public offerings for Chinese peer-to-peer markets, citing concerns about the uncertain outlook for peer-to-peer lenders and the depressing impact of a slump equity markets on valuations.

Frontier fintech Digital banking is finally coming to South Africa, as online upstarts stake their claim in a market that has traditionally been characterised by its high fees, writes the FT s Joseph Cotterill. One of them   the insurer Discovery   is even planning what its chief executive describes as  the world s first behavioural bank .

AOB Non-human traders are an easy scapegoat for volatile markets, but it s not all their fault, writes David Siegel, co-chairman of quantitative investment group Two Sigma in the FT. Algorithms aren t as mysterious as they might seem, and they don t deserve a reputation as bogeymen, he says. In fact, they can be better than humans in some circumstances, he argues, as stress never clouds their performance, and they never act on a hunch.

Features
Asset managers look at a cull of weak funds

311 words
28 January 2019
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Asia
16
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

CHART TO START THE WEEK

One of the thorniest decisions facing an investment manager is whether to axe an uneconomic fund in which they have devoted time, resources and money.

Severe pressure on fund fees is forcing many managers to consider which products will stay viable. JPMorgan Asset Management said last year that it would reduce its range by about a quarter as part of a "simplify for growth" strategy.

Chris Willcox, chief executive of asset management at JPMorgan AM, predicted that other investment managers would be forced to shrink their ranges.

"High profit margins have cushioned managers from making tough decisions. Managers rarely shut funds, regardless of underperformance or obsolescence. This has led to a proliferation of mediocre products," said Mr Willcox.

Flowspring, a Denver analytics group, has developed a framework to help investment managers assess whether to shut an unprofitable fund or let it soldier on. About $5m a year in fee revenues is required for most mutual funds to remain viable, according to an industry rule of thumb.

Flowspring assessed the viability of 14,885 mutual funds globally, based on expense ratios, fee revenues, expected fee growth and the investment performance of the fund.

Flowspring recommended that no action be taken for most funds (10,084) as they were judged to be healthy. But 1,752 funds that have a question mark on profitability were tipped for merger or closure.

Backtested data found that close to half (47 per cent) of funds where Flowspring had recommended closure or merger fell below the $5m fees mark within a year.

On Flowspring's metrics, BlackRock, State Street, UBS, Invesco, Nuveen, Principal, Columbia Threadneedle, John Hancock and Natixis all have funds that could be axed.

Only Vanguard can avoid taking action due to its industry-leading low-cost base. Chris Flood

Tenfold increase in ETF sales for JPMorgan

JPMorgan's bid to join the top tier of exchange traded fundmanagers was boosted last year as the New Yorklisted groupincreased sales tenfold. The bank, which had some of the best-selling newly launched funds in 2018," has increased its efforts to regain assets lost to BlackRock and Vanguardover the past decade.

year intoETFs runbyJPMorgan Asset Management , up fromjust $1.6bn in 2017, according t

AI Deployed as Defense in Cyber Arsenal of Companies Large and Small; Businesses from community banks to financial services firms are finding ways to deploy AI to quickly spot malicious actors and staunch breaches

By Adam Janofsky 
787 words
28 January 2019
10:00
WSJ Pro Artificial Intelligence
RSTPROAI
English
Copyright  2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

The use of artificial intelligence to aid in cybersecurity efforts was once primarily the domain of larger companies with deep pockets and substantial information technology infrastructure.

But now adoption of the technology is expanding and includes smaller enterprises as well, with both using AI to quickly spot malicious actors and staunch breaches.

Kennebunk Savings Bank , with 19 branches across Maine and New Hampshire, in recent years was spending increasing amounts of time asking customers phoning the bank various security questions to verify their identity, said Kennebunk Chief Executive Bradford Paige.

"We would spend half the call verifying the customer," and often couldn't fulfill the customer's request over the phone, he said.

Since November, the bank has used AI-enabled voice biometric technology to help identify fraudsters and impersonators who attempt to access customer accounts. AI advancements benefit biometric authentication tools, which use unique body measurements to identify employees, customers and other individuals and grant them access to corporate systems and services.

The bank's voice biometric technology, developed by Nuance Communications Inc ., analyzes about two to three seconds of audio to verify a caller's identity. The technology uses deep neural networks to analyze voice patterns that aren't discernible to the human ear, such as the spacing between words and phonemes, said Brett Beranek, vice president of security at Nuance.

The Wyndham New Yorker, a 1,050-room hotel that is part of the international chain Wyndham Hotels & Resorts Inc ., also has been using AI-based cybersecurity tools for about a year and a half, said William Reid, the hotel's director of information technology.

The technology, developed by cybersecurity firm Darktrace Ltd ., has helped him identify a few notable incidents. In one, Mr. Reid was alerted an administrator at the hotel was moving an unusually large number of corporate files containing messages, calendar events and other data to personal accounts.

"It was information that should not have been going out of the network," said Mr. Reid. He said he reported the incident to human resources, but declined to say whether or how the individual was disciplined.

In a similar case, Mr. Reid said the technology predicted a salesperson at the hotel was about to leave the firm based on an unusual attempt to transfer business contacts to a personal account.

Max Heinemeyer, director of threat hunting at Darktrace , said the AI used by the hotel works by tracking real-time data from hundreds of sources, such as the time zone the user is typically in, the IP addresses and browsers they use, the applications and websites they access and the time of day these actions usually take place.

If the algorithms detect irregular behavior, such as a user accessing parts of the network they've never visited before or transferring unusually large amounts of data out of network, the system can cut off access pending a review.

"AI is good at analyzing all these subtle deviations &[and] it gets better over time," said Mr. Heinemeyer. "The more data it sees and the longer it's on, the better it gets."

Guillermo Guerra, chief information security officer at Prudential PLC, a London-based insurance and financial services firm, said AI's ability to intelligently pinpoint signs of malicious behavior can help companies focus their remediation efforts on real incidents and not false alarms.

Prudential's 350-person security team investigates about 1,000 security alerts each week, said Mr. Guerra. His team is developing and implementing AI tools to improve the accuracy of these alert systems, and help identify anomalies that are likely associated with malicious actors. Prudential isn't affiliated with the U.S. financial services firm Prudential Financial Inc .

"With some of the [AI-enabled tools] we've built, you really don't want to have an alert," he said. "Some systems should be used only in case of an emergency the moment it's used and there's not an alert, it's an issue."

Companies should not depend on AI to replace traditional safeguards, however, said Wyndham's Mr. Reid. He also deploys firewalls, antivirus and network security tools in addition to his AI cyber defenses.

Nonetheless, having this weapon in a company's arsenal can serve as an extra layer of protection, said Mr. Reid.

"Machine learning gives me extra leverage &it tightens up our network so I know how we operate on a daily and nightly basis," he said.

Good day. Washington is back at work. That doesn't mean the entire bureacracy will instantly snap to attention, but investors are hoping that regulators will at least start to catch up with the backlog of IPO filings and follow-ups.

Today's Top Stories

Stake in the kitchen: Online food-delivery services were hot when they burst onto the scene a few years back, but the craze has since led to a growing list of failures. Now, a new crop of startups are taking a swing at the rapidly evolving urban food-service industry by homing in on one layer of business the cooking facilities.

Roof's Take: Get Ready for More  Cord-Cutter' Acquisitions

By Katie Roof

Investors expect more acquisitions in the cord-cutting industry following Viacom Inc.'s $340 million purchase of streaming startup Pluto TV.

"Consolidation will have to happen," said Joanne Chen, a partner at Foundation Capital who sits on the board of Tubi Inc., a competitor to Pluto TV. Her firm also was an early investor in Netflix Inc.

Venture investment activity in the cord-cutting category peaked in 2014 and has declined since, according to PitchBook Data. Eleven such startups received funding last year, down from 37 four years earlier. The amount of money invested in the sector in each of those years was about $200 million.

These businesses provide an alternative to cable television, and consumers increasingly are ditching cable subscriptions in favor of unbundled options. Investors noticed the shift and placed their bets on the future of video content.

Video startups like Tastemade Inc. and Philo Inc. are among the most well-funded in the sector. But there have been few exits in recent years, with Roku Inc.'s 2017 initial public offering being the largest to date.

More in Roof's Take.

Exits

Medtronic PLC agreed to buy EPIX Therapeutics Inc., the maker of a catheter-based system to treat irregular heartbeats, for an undisclosed amount. EPIX, which changed its name from Advanced Cardiac Therapeutics Inc. last year, was backed by investors including Ajax Health, New Enterprise Associates and Questa Capital Management.

Artificial intelligence-based analytics company Guavus Inc. acquired SQLstream Inc., a real-time streaming analytics provider, for an undisclosed amount. SQLstream, of San Francisco, was backed by Fontinalis Partners. France's Thales SA bought Guavus in 2017.

Early-stage investor Firebolt Ventures is raising up to $50 million for a third fund, according to a regulatory filing. No commitments have been received yet. Bace Ltd. is raising a venture fund of up to $120 million, the firm disclosed in a regulatory filing. Yan Chen is listed as the director of Bace Fund LP, which is incorporated in the Cayman Islands. The fund had yet to secure any committed capital, according to the filing. Bace didn't immediately respond to requests for comment.

JPMorgan makes late push to become ETF heavyweight

Chris Flood 
598 words
27 January 2019
23:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

JPMorgan s bid to join the top tier of exchange traded fund managers was boosted last year as the New York-listed group increased sales tenfold.

The bank, which had some of the best-selling newly launched funds in 2018, has increased its efforts to regain assets lost to BlackRock and Vanguard over the past decade.

The emergence of yet another heavyweight was an unwelcome challenge for many ETF providers already ferociously battling to win investors  cash.

Investors ploughed $16.8bn last year into ETFs run by JPMorgan Asset Management, up from just $1.6bn in 2017, according to ETFGI, a London consultancy.

Last year s inflows exceeded new ETF business in 2018 for both State Street ($6.6bn) and Invesco ($6.8bn), the third and fourth largest players globally. JPMorgan was the fifth-best seller of ETFs last year, behind Vanguard, BlackRock, Nomura and Charles Schwab.

JPMorgan historically lacked a significant presence in the ETF industry, preferring to focus on active management and its alternatives arm.

This strategy had puzzled observers given that JPMorgan s $2tn asset and wealth management business provides services to an army of US financial advisers and retail investors, who have increased their appetite for ETFs since the 2007/08 financial crisis.

The ETF unit sits inside a longer-established beta strategies arm, led by global head Michael Camacho in New York.

Deborah Fuhr, co-founder of ETFGI, said:  JPMorgan has the resources and expertise to be a significant competitor in the ETF industry but it has allowed its rivals to build a lead that will require intensive efforts to recover. 

Executives at JPMorgan insist they have not left their ETF push too late.

 We expect to see substantial growth for ETFs over the next decade. We have spent a lot of time building the right teams, infrastructure and processes to compete in this space,  said Bryon Lake, head of international ETFs at JPMAM.

About $9.7bn of last year s inflows were gathered by BetaBuilders, a new range of ETFs that track equity markets in Japan, Europe, Canada and Asia. They featured prominently among the industry s best-selling launches of 2018. A further $5.3bn went into actively managed ETFs last year.

Warren Miller, founder of Flowspring, a Denver-based asset management analytics company, said JPMorgan was focusing on sophisticated, niche ETF strategies.

 It is not going for vanilla ETFs. This allows JPMorgan to retain some pricing power. Their model is different from Charles Schwab, which offers simple ETFs at bargain basement fees to compete with BlackRock and Vanguard,  he said.

JPMorgan did not disclose the breakdown of the ETF inflows that were attributed to new cash, money cannibalised from existing funds or reallocations from rival products held by clients on the bank s platform.

An analysis of regulatory filings by Flowspring indicated that about $4.2bn of the 2018 ETF inflows were reallocations from BlackRock ETFs, with a further $1.1bn shifted from State Street ETFs by current JPMorgan clients.

 There was also some modest cannibalisation from existing JPMorgan funds in 2018 but it was not meaningful,  said Mr Miller.

Even after its strong showing in 2018, JPMorgan still ranks as only the 19th-largest ETF player globally with assets of just over $21bn. BlackRock, the biggest player, controls close to $1.8tn in ETF assets.

Mr Miller said he expected JPMorgan s ETF business to maintain momentum in 2019.  Our forecast is for organic ETF asset growth, excluding new product launches, of around 24 per cent this year,  he said.

Billionaire who rebuilt Citadel from ruins of financial crisis

Ken Griffin 
846 words
26 January 2019
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa
9
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Corporate person in the news During the 2008 financial crisis, the television network CNBC parked a van outside the Chicago headquarters of Ken Griffin's hedge fund Citadel, expecting the company to fail. The producers' precaution was understandable. Citadel was losing "hundreds of millions of dollars" each week, Mr Griffin later told the Financial Times.

More than a decade on and Citadel has rebounded, becoming a $28bn behemoth, majority-owned by Mr Griffin. He also controls Citadel Securities, a market maker, which helps other investors buy and sell assets and now accounts for about a fifth of all stock trades in the US. In turn, the 50-year-old Mr Griffin has amassed a personal fortune close to $10bn, according to Forbes.

That fortune was on display as Mr Griffin agreed the purchase of a $238m New York penthouse spanning 24,000 sq ft across the top four floors of a new skyscraper being built on 220 Central Park South, which has stunning views across the leafy part of Manhattan. It marks the most expensive home purchase in the US.

It came the same week as the FT revealed that Mr Griffin had bought a 95m home near Buckingham Palace in London. His previous property purchases include one floor of the Waldorf Astoria in Chicago and he separately set the record for the most expensive home in the city when he bought a $59m four-level penthouse. He has also spent roughly $200m on land and property in Florida, where he was born.

As an undergraduate, Mr Griffin had more humble digs. It was from his Harvard dorm room that he developed a passion for trading, installing a satellite dish to receive upto-the-second price information. "That was 1987," he said at a conference in November last year. "I have to explain to my son that the internet didn't exist back then."

He said he built basic predictive analytics tools using economic theory straight from his textbooks. That process, of marrying mathematics and technology to trade, has been integral to Citadel   launched in 1990, shortly after he graduated   and Citadel Securities, which together have been at the forefront of overhauling the way markets operate.

Mr Griffin first went into contract on his latest New York purchase in 2015, shortly before finalising a heated divorce with his second wife Anne Dias, founder of the hedge fund Aragon Global Management.

The public scrutiny of their separation and lurid details exposed through a lengthy court battle stood in contrast to Mr Griffin's attempts to keep much of his personal and business dealings hidden from view.

He is described by former and current employees at Citadel as methodical, self-motivated and rooted in quantitative analysis.

Some say he is intense, but others see his intensity as part of a relentless, almost military desire for continual improvement that is instilled in the organisation.

Although he is not known for basking in the limelight, Mr Griffin does maintain a public image. It was while he was with Ms Dias that he entrenched himself in Chicago's cultural and political scene.

An avid art collector, his name adorns one section of the city's Art Institute after a $19m donation. In 2016, he paid $500m to buy two paintings   a Jackson Pollock and a Willem de Kooning   in one of the largest private art deals.

Mr Griffin's grandparents' name was given to the Gratz Center at the Fourth Presbyterian Church after an $11m donation. He is in the last of a five-year term on the University of Chicago's board of trustees, where he has previously donated $125m. According to Citadel, Mr Griffin has given more than $700m in charitable donations.

In politics, Mr Griffin has supported Democrats and Republicans and maintains that he is not ideologically committed to either party, despite once describing himself as a "Reagan Republican".

He backed Chicago resident Barack Obama for president in 2008, as well as aiding another Democrat   Rahm Emanuel   to re-election as the city's mayor. But more recently, he has tended towards Republican candidates, backing Mitt Romney and Marco Rubio in their presidential bids.

Mr Griffin also backed Bruce Rauner when he was elected as Illinois state governor in 2014, endorsing a policy of tax cuts and business freedom. Despite an injection of $22.5m from Mr Griffin leading up to his 2018 attempt at reelection, Mr Rauner was beaten by the Democrat JB Pritzker.

But the setback has not dissuaded Mr Griffin from remaining involved in politics, just as the financial crisis did not spell the end for Citadel. As for his real estate purchases, Mr Griffin is confident that his market instincts are sound.

"In the US as a whole we still have a growing population," he said at a conference in July last year. "That's going to underpin home prices in years to come." Joe Rennison

Tech
Google 's Effort to Prevent Blindness Shows AI Challenges; Company's AI can detect a condition that causes blindness in diabetes patients, but in rural India it doesn't always work

By Corinne Abrams 
1,113 words
26 January 2019
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications

Google's ARDA tool can look for signs of diabetic retinopathy. An earlier version of this article said it could look for symptoms.

### picture data found, picture dimensions are 640 by 426, depth 1
pict076.jpg

Dr. R. Kim, who runs the Aravind Eye Hospital in Madurai, Tamil Nadu, is working with Google to perfect its Automated Retinal Disease Assessment tool. PHOTO: Naveen P M for The Wall Street Journal

MADURAI, India Alphabet Inc .'s Google has developed artificial intelligence that can detect a condition that causes blindness in diabetic patients, though tests in India demonstrate the challenges of transferring such technology from the lab to the doctor's office.

The technology giant's Automated Retinal Disease Assessment tool is trained to diagnose diabetic retinopathy, a complication of diabetes, by reading images of patients' retinas. Studies show that the artificial intelligence can be as effective as a doctor at identifying signs of the condition.

Google is testing ARDA in India where at least 60 million people have diabetes and often don't know they should be screened for the eye condition. A shortage of ophthalmologists means reaching patients is difficult, and doctors say the technology could help to fill the gap.

Google is the latest tech firm to explore using AI in health care. Amazon.com Inc ., International Business Machines Corp . and UnitedHealth Group Inc . sell "https://www.wsj.com/articles/amazon-starts-selling-software-to-mine-patient-health-records-1543352136", and IBM developed AI that detects melanoma on images of skin.

By using technology similar to algorithms that recognize people or dogs in photos, ARDA detects signs of the illness lesions, burst blood vessels and patches of yellow on the retina and grades their severity.

"This is a technology that can make a really big difference," said Lily Peng, a Google product manager and trained medical doctor who helps lead the team developing ARDA at the company's California offices.

While ARDA is effective working with sample data, according to three studies including one published in the Journal of the American Medical Association , a recent visit to a hospital in India where it is being tested showed it can struggle with images taken in field clinics. Often they are of such poor quality that the Google tool stops short of producing a diagnosis an obstacle that ARDA researchers are trying to overcome.

The stakes are high. If diabetic retinopathy is caught early it can be kept at bay through monitoring and management of the diabetes, said R. Kim, an Indian ophthalmologist who runs the Aravind Eye Hospital in Madurai, Tamil Nadu, where Google is testing ARDA. More advanced stages need laser surgery that can stop progression. If it isn't treated, the condition can cause blindness.

For Google , diabetic retinopathy was an ideal case study because experts agree on its signs and symptoms. With skyrocketing rates of diabetes, India provided the right testing ground. As incomes have grown, so have citizens' waistlines. The prevalence of the condition in India increased 64% from 1990 to 2016, according to government data.

"I am not proud to say, but India is becoming the diabetic capital of the world," Dr. Kim said.

Yet the country has just 11 ophthalmologists for every million people, compared with 59 in the U.S., according to the International Council of Ophthalmologists. Training the number needed is "a race you can't win," he said.

In many cases, diabetic patients in India visit a doctor only when they have already lost some vision, Dr. Kim said. He hopes ARDA will reach more patients through doctors' offices or pharmacies, pre-empting the need for his ophthalmologists to run screening camps in rural areas.

To create ARDA, Dr. Peng's team obtained more than 1.6 million images of retinas that were each evaluated by ophthalmologists. Google used those graded images to train the algorithm. Now the team is grappling with how to make it work with low-quality images produced by the sort of equipment affordable in developing countries such as India.

On a November day in a telemedicine center at Dr. Kim's hospital, ophthalmologists wearing headsets waited for 73 local eye clinics to connect patients in rural areas via webcam.

Ophthalmologist Anusha Arunachalan got a call from a clinic an hour from Madurai. A 39-year-old diabetic homemaker was having trouble seeing. 

### picture data found, picture dimensions are 640 by 427, depth 1
pict077.jpg

Dr. Kim performs a retinal examination on a patient. PHOTO: Naveen P M for The Wall Street Journal

Six images sent by a clinic technician showed the patient's retinas covered by pools of blood. Dr. Arunachalan diagnosed proliferative diabetic retinopathy an advanced stage of the condition. Then Google gave it a try. It graded two of the six images as proliferative though one was deemed of poor quality and two others as severe. The tool was unable to grade two of the photos accurately because they were unclear.

While Dr. Arunchalan said it also was hard for her to read some of the images, "One [clear] image is enough for me. I don't need three images" for each eye to make a diagnosis, she said.

Doctors at the telemedicine center said they often look at the clear parts of faulty images to make a diagnosis, while the algorithm sometimes can't.

It isn't a unique problem, said John Quackenbush, professor of computational biology at the Harvard T.H. Chan School of Public Health . Algorithms can be trained on sample images but real-world photos can be different.

"I don't think it is insurmountable," he said, on hearing about Google 's challenges.

Google is also testing the algorithm with another partner in India and one in Thailand and is working on making ARDA work with different-quality images, Dr. Peng said. But the challenge is also ethical.

If Google allowed the algorithm to make a diagnosis from blurred images, it could miss small lesions that appear in the early stages of the condition, she said. Google must decide how bad an image can be before ARDA refuses to grade it.

"It's a trade-off. We want them to be able to use cameras that are a little harder to use but at some point it should move into something where it is ungradable," Dr. Peng said.

Nonetheless, when ARDA gets a good image, it can identify early signs of the condition that doctors may have missed, Dr. Kim said.

Whereas ARDA does it in seconds, a human can take three minutes to grade an image plus the time taken to open it on a computer, Dr. Kim said. A busy doctor, he said, could very well miss tiny red lesions in a patient's eye.

Developer of Ace and NoMad Hotels Buys Parker New York

By Keiko Morris 
681 words
25 January 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
A9A
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The developer behind trendsetting hotels in Manhattan's NoMad neighborhood has acquired the Parker New York hotel, looking to bring some of its downtown success to a midtown strip known more for opulent residences than hip lodging.

Real-estate firm GFI Capital Resources Group and Elliott Management Corp., a hedge fund and activist investor, closed Wednesday on the purchase of the 729-room hotel for about $420 million, GFI Capital said.

The joint venture purchased the hotel from the family of the late Jack Parker, a hotel and property developer.

The venture plans to spend an additional $100 million to make over the 37-year-old hotel and convert many of the rooms into private residences.

The hotel has entrances on West 56th and West 57th streets, so-called Billionaires' Row because of the ultrahigh end condominiums built along the street and around the southern end of Central Park.

The partners expect to revamp the underground retail space, but their plans are still evolving, said Allen Gross, chief executive of GFI Capital. The popular Burger Joint, located in the hotel, will remain, he said.

The hotel will stay open during the renovation, which is expected to begin in 2020. It will be relaunched as a flagship of Hyatt Hotels Corp.'s Thompson Hotel brand in 2021.

The new owners have tapped New York-based architect Thomas Juul-Hansen to create about 67 residential apartments at the top of the building. The condos will have floor-to-ceiling windows and offer hotel services and amenities, GFI Capital said.

The project won't be competing with its surrounding ultraluxury neighbors, Mr. Gross said. Most of the condos will be one-bedroom units with pricing possibly in the $2 million range, not $20 million or $30 million.

"The location is something to die for," Mr. Gross said. "It's Billionaires' Row, and this piece of real estate is right smack in the middle of it."

The Parker Hotel was part of the Le Meridien brand, originally a Paris-based hotel line that has struggled at times in the U.S. It was part of Starwood Hotels & Resorts Worldwide Inc., which was acquired by Marriott International Inc. in 2016. The Parker stopped operating under that flag about a year ago.

Bjorn Hanson, a hotel-industry consultant, described the Parker as a "quiet hotel" of less prominence and name recognition than other hotels of similar size and in similarly well-located addresses.

Hyatt can bring "a reservation system, loyalty program, group sales and international sales to a hotel that has not had those types of supports," Mr. Hanson said.

GFI Capital's affiliate developed the Ace Hotel New York and the NoMad Hotel more than a decade ago. Their open lobbies, public spaces and free Wi-Fi helped accelerate the trend of hotels evolving into popular spots for freelancers and other locals to work, much like co-working spaces.

The hotels also helped spur condo, trendy restaurants and other development in the previously shaggy NoMad neighborhood, which primarily had been populated with wholesale stores.

GFI Capital later applied a similar approach when it joined forces with Elliott Management to revamp a long-vacant 19th-century, lower Manhattan building into what is now the posh Beekman hotel.

While Elliott Management has a reputation for buying shares in companies such as eBay Inc. and pushing for change, the $34 billion hedge fund also has invested in the real-estate sector. Since 2009, the firm has made about $9 billion in direct commercial real-estate investments in the U.S., Europe and Asia in addition to investments in real-estate securities.

The team's new project comes at a time when average daily hotel rates in Manhattan have begun to rise after several years of declines, as construction continues to increase the supply. Last year, the average daily rate rose to $281.09, up 2.6% from the previous year's $274.08, according to analytics firm STR.

WSJ Pro Cybersecurity

Cyber Daily: Corporate Cybersecurity Awareness, By The Numbers

By Kim S. Nash

Good day. As the new year takes shape, let's take stock of the cybersecurity situation before us. Threats are intensifying, including from China and Russia, which U.S. intelligence officials warn continue to launch organized, methodical and deep attacks on corporate and government networks.

The Securities and Exchange Commission a year ago issued its most detailed guidance to date for how companies should disclose how they view and manage cybersecurity risks. Adherence to the SEC's, shall we say, aggressive encouragement of disclosure has been uneven. Reading the proxy statements of some firms in the S&P 500, one might surmise that cyber threats simply don't exist. They have said nothing about the risk in their filings.

Oversight by boards and senior leaders has improved in the past year or so, with directors educating themselves about cybersecurity. Some executives have also gotten schooled watching the fallout from some very large, very serious attacks. You've read about these incidents and subsequent financial and reputational damage here: Facebook Inc., Yahoo Inc., Marriott International Inc., Equifax Inc., FedEx Corp. -- we could go on.

Today, we let numbers tell the story. We've collected some of the best data out there to provide snapshots on where large companies stand as they prepare defenses against unseen attackers. Things are better than they were a year ago but there's still a long way to go. How does your company measure up?

**********

Serious Cyber Incidents

5.9

Average number of cybersecurity incidents experienced per year at a company with more than $40 billion in annual revenue, according to research firm International Data Corp.

2.2 of these incidents, on average, meet thresholds for severity for reporting to regulators, says IDC in a survey of the 201 U.S. companies.

Breach Costs

$3.9 Million

Average cost of a data breach in 2018, according to Ponemon Institute and International Business Machines Corp. That is up 6.4% compared to 2017's $3.6 million.

The average cost for each stolen record rose nearly 5%, from $141 in 2017 to $148 last year. The research examined breaches at 477 organizations globally.

Board Concern

42%

Percentage of directors who said growing cybersecurity threats will have the greatest effect on their company in 2019, after regulatory changes (49%) and economic slowdown (48%), according to the National Association of Corporate Directors.

52% of directors say they understand cyber risks enough to provide effective oversight, NACD says, based on a survey of 500 members.

Board Expertise

35

Number of companies among the 100 biggest public U.S. firms by revenue and market capitalization that identified directors with cybersecurity or data experience in 2018 financial filings, according to Shearman & Sterling LLP. That's up from 29 in 2017.

In the wider pool of the S&P 500, just 24% of companies disclosed in their latest proxy filings that they have at least one director with cybersecurity skill, says researcher MyLogIQ LLC.

Number of companies in the S&P 500 that operate a standalone board committee dedicated to cybersecurity, according to researcher MyLogIQ LLC.

CISO Power Structure

43%

Percentage of chief information security officers who report to the CEO, according to ISC(2), an association for cybersecurity professionals. That compares to 35% who report to the CIO and 14% who report to the board of directors, ISC(2) found in a survey of professionals with some cyber responsibilities at 250 companies.

14% of those surveyed said their company doesn't have a CISO.

Inside Google's Team Battling Hackers

Google's in-house counterespionage group, the Threat Analysis Group, tracks more than 200 hacker groups that pose a threat to Google and its users, analyzing hacking techniques and clues to the groups' identities to head off attacks, The Wall Street Journal reports. The 27-person team leverages access to data across widely used Google products like Gmail, with more than 1.5 billion accounts world-wide, and to a database of attack code called VirusTotal managed by another arm of Google's parent Alphabet Inc.

Tons of data: "Google probably has the most useful data set available to any private company for tracking state adversaries and intelligence services," said Alex Stamos, former chief security officer at Facebook Inc. and now an adjunct professor at Stanford University. He likens Google's efforts to those of a small intelligence agency. "You put that all together, and they are probably second only to the intelligence community" in terms of useful data, he said. The team issues about 4,000 warnings a month to Gmail users with accounts where it detects government-backed hackers trying to break in.

Iran, elections: Last summer, the team stopped an allegedly Iranian-backed disinformation campaign by pulling dozens of YouTube channels that were using fake accounts to push misleading political stories primarily about the Middle East. Disinformation, especially around elections, is a new focus.

Sovereign corporate states? Such corporate counterintelligence teams can have more impact in the private sector than in government. "These companies are sovereign authorities inside their products," said Sergio Caltagirone, a former NSA analyst hired by Microsoft Corp. in 2013. Many of his peers felt the same way, he said. "There were a lot of people who had spent close to a decade in government, and everyone was recognizing, 'Yeah, we can't really do much,' " he said.

Former FCC Chairman criticizes Trump administration over 5G security. Former Federal Communications Commission Chairman Tom Wheeler said the Trump administration failed to take appropriate cybersecurity measures to protect 5G mobile networks, in an opinion column for The New York Times. He criticized his FCC successor for removing an Obama-era cybersecurity requirement for 5G networks and withdrawing a white paper from the same period about reducing cyber threats. "The Trump administration has been told that cybersecurity is an  existential risk,'" Mr. Wheeler wrote. "The new Congress should use its oversight power to explore just why the administration has failed to protect against that risk, especially when it comes to the next generation of networks."

Sweden investigates Google for possible GDPR violation. Sweden's data protection regulator is investigating Google for a potential violation of Europe's general data protection regulation, The Register reports. Consumer groups have alleged that Google's Android design is deceptive and led users to allow tracking of their locations. The regulator asked Google to share documentation and respond to questions by Feb. 1. France's data protection regulator announced this week a $57 million fine against Google for allegedly violating GDPR.

License-plate readers connect to internet. Images from more than 150 license-plate readers are searchable on the internet, Techcrunch reports. Many cameras were easily accessible and used default passwords. The license plate readers are often operated by police departments and government agencies to track vehicle activity. Some companies such as universities also use the cameras to monitor campuses.

Verizon To Lay Off 7% of Media Group Staff; Cuts, which will slash about 800 jobs, follow a monthslong review of a unit that has failed to meet revenue targets

Verizon Communications Inc.'s beleaguered media group is laying off 7% of its staff and is focusing on fewer areas to revive its fortunes, according to people familiar with the matter. 

The moves follow a monthslong review of the business by K. Guru Gowrappan, a 38-year-old former Yahoo and Alibaba Group executive who joined Verizon last spring. He "https://www.wsj.com/articles/verizons-internet-boss-in-talks-to-leave-1536321413" former AOL executive Tim Armstrong, who departed late last year. 

Mr. Gowrappan said in an email to staff Wednesday that the business would focus on mobile and video-focused products. Yahoo-branded entertainment and news platforms are particularly important to the unit's strategy, the people said. 

Other priorities include creating more video content for its own media properties such as Yahoo Finance and those run by third parties like Roku, as well as generating more revenue from its ad platforms and a streaming video and content delivery system. That system, called Verizon Digital Media Services, works with companies like Hulu, ESPN and Walt Disney Co. 

Verizon Media Group had 11,385 employees at the end of 2018 and the planned cuts will impact staff globally, one of the people said. The unit had 12,845 employees after Verizon bought Yahoo in 2017. 

The moves are "a strategic step toward better execution of our plans for growth and innovation into the future," a spokeswoman for the unit said. 

Mr. Gowrappan's plans highlight the balance Verizon is trying to strike between creating video content that draws users and showcases its network and avoiding pricey large-scale content acquisitions. 

Hans Vestberg, Verizon's chief executive, has said the carrier is focused on its network "https://www.wsj.com/articles/verizons-5g-game-plan-1536673535". 

Mr. Gowrappan's plans are the latest attempt to breathe new life into a media business that aimed to become a powerful force in digital advertising. 

Formed after Verizon's purchases of AOL in 2015 and Yahoo two years later, the business was initially called Oath and has since been rebranded Verizon Media Group as part of a carrier-wide reorganization. 

Despite its ambitions of becoming an advertising giant, the unit has failed to meet revenue targets by selling digital ads. The carrier said in December that it is "https://www.wsj.com/articles/verizon-takes-4-6-billion-charge-related-to-digital-media-business-11544538587" related to the business and executives acknowledged that core parts of the business search and desktop usage "https://www.wsj.com/articles/verizon-adds-phone-customers-but-oath-revenue-weakens-1540297875". 

Mr. Gowrappan told staff in the email that his new plans were made after an "exhaustive review" in which "we honestly assessed where we are and outlined ambitious but achievable goals that poise us for growth." 

He and other Verizon Media Group executives in recent months have "https://www.wsj.com/articles/paywall-for-huffpost-verizon-hunt-for-web-revenue-goes-beyond-ads-1543752001" to encourage users to spend more time on its properties, the Journal has reported 

They identified Yahoo as one of the strongest brands within the business. 

Teams working on Yahoo Mail and Yahoo's home page were merged and charged with creating a more cohesive user experience. Meanwhile, staff members are working to channel more content produced by Makers, a feminist media brand, and Build, a brand that conducts live celebrity interviews, into the media group's news and entertainment platforms. 

Mr. Gowrappan has halted some efforts to sell brands within the media business, opting instead to shutter flagging brands or services. One app that will close is its WakingNews alarm. 

He viewed the process of selling photo-sharing service Flickr last year, for example, as too pricey and time-consuming, people familiar with the matter said. 

Despite the layoffs, Verizon plans to hire new engineering and content-development staff. Those new workers will focus on building new services that showcase Verizon's faster, 5G network. 

Some of the media group's artificial intelligence and machine-learning staff will work more closely with Verizon's own teams focused on those areas. The carrier is looking for ways to make its media assets showcase the benefits of its wireless network because upgrading to a 5G network sits the center of Mr. Vestberg's corporate strategy.

U2's Bono and TPG Launch Company to Measure 'Impact Investments'; Method relies on research from academics and others to assess investments

By Simon Clark 
837 words
23 January 2019
07:11
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Bono and TPG are teaming up to try to prove that investments meant to "https://www.wsj.com/articles/private-equity-firm-abraaj-raised-billions-pledging-to-do-goodthen-it-fell-apart-1539706575" do what they purport to do.

The U2 frontman and his business partners at the U.S. private-equity firm announced a new company called Y Analytics, which will use a method they developed to measure social and environmental change created by investments.

It's the latest effort in a race with dozens of participants to measure so-called impact investing, a new asset class private-equity firms are developing to try to produce social and environmental change as well as profit. Y Analytics grew out of Rise, a $2 billion investment fund TPG and Bono co-founded.

"Math is music here," Bono said in an interview. "If capitalism is to be a force for good we have to be able to measure when it's doing good and when it's doing harm," he said. "Fuzzy thinking just won't cut it. We need cold hard facts."

Skeptics say impact investing is little more than an attempt to put private-equity firms in a positive light. For years, the firms have had to fend off criticism of asset-stripping, tax avoidance, burdening companies with debt and firing workers. TPG is raising $3 billion for its second Rise fund. It manages about $103 billion in total.

"I do believe it's marketing," Anand Giridharadas, a former analyst at U.S. management consulting firm McKinsey & Co. and author of "Winners Take All, The Elite Charade of Changing the World," said in an interview. "The public hear 'TPG, impact fund, Bono' and that sounds good. It sells us on the idea that finance people are vital to help solving the problems of inequality, which obscures the way in which finance people are a big part of the cause of inequality and the social problems they are trying to solve."

"I understand the cynicism," Bono said. "There is banana skin all over the floor, I think. I am very conscious of it. We have had green-washing or purpose-washing with all manner of halos being offered up as fig leaves for companies who do bad things but then give money away and make up for it. I get that," he said.

Y Analytics measures investments with a number it calls the impact multiple of money. TPG created the method to measure investments in its Rise fund. The number is generated by tapping independent research related to each investment. For example, TPG assessed an investment in an Indian dairy based on whether it boosts incomes for the thousands of smallholder farmers who provide the milk.

The nonprofit Global Impact Investing Network identified $228 billion of such investments globally in 2017. Nick O'Donohoe, chief executive of U.K. investment firm CDC Group, told a conference in October that there are 150 ways to measure social and environmental improvement and the inability to agree on a definition for the new industry is "the elephant in the room." CDC has its own approach to screening investments.

Agreement on a reliable approach to measurement could help attract trillions of dollars of private capital to these funds, according to Bill McGlashan, the head of TPG's growth investing arm and co-founder of the Rise fund. He said the money is needed to reach the United Nations' 17 sustainable development goals by 2030. The U.N.'s goals include eliminating global poverty and improving education and health care.

"If we achieve our mission, we help catalyze a bunch of really effective, credible competitors coming into the market, because that's the scale of capital that's ultimately needed," Mr. McGlashan said in an interview.

The method uses research from academics, independent companies and institutions to assess investments. Rise's investments "https://hbr.org/2019/01/calculating-the-value-of-impact-investing"worth $26 billion 13 times the size of the $2 billion fund, Mr. McGlashan said.

Maryanne Hancock, a former McKinsey executive, will lead Y Analytics. The company has seven staff as well as advisers including Robert Zimmer, president of the University of Chicago. TPG declined to say how much it has spent on Y Analytics.

Ms. Hancock said Y Analytics will make it easier to compare investments in different sectors such as education and energy. "This is a discipline that can evolve over time into a benchmark," she said.

The attempt to make investments capable of creating social benefits was developed by executives in the $3.4 trillion global private-equity industry including Ronald Cohen, co-founder of London-based Apax Partners, who Bono described as a "hero" who "opened us up to a different way of thinking."

"Eventually we will get to a parallel impact accounting system to the financial system," Mr. Cohen said in an interview. "We are making progress all the time."

Fed's Williams Joins Chorus in Favor of Patience on Rates | Central Banks Struggle With Policy Settings | China's Worst Growth Figures in Nearly 30 Years; The Wall Street Journal's central banking newsletter for Tuesday, January 22, 2019

2,673 words
22 January 2019
12:30
WSJ Pro Central Banking
RSTPROCB
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Good day. New York Fed leader John Williams cast his lot with colleagues who favor a pause on rate rises, saying the central bank should show "prudence, patience and good judgment." Meanwhile, Fed governor Lael Brainard raised concerns that political uncertainty could end up hitting economic growth. Globally, many central banks are finding it hard to move back to monetary policy settings that were considered normal before the financial crisis. And the IMF has cut its global growth forecast for 2019, while China posted its worst annual growth figures in nearly three decades.

Now on to today's news and analysis.

Williams: Now Is Time for Fed to Show  Prudence, Patience and Good Judgment'

New York Fed President John Williams said Friday what happens with short-term interest rates and the central bank's balance-sheet drawdown "https://www.wsj.com/articles/feds-williams-interest-rate-balance-sheet-adjustments-will-depend-on-economys-performance-11547820503?tesla=y" this year by how the economy performs. "The economy is strong, the outlook is healthy, and my number one priority is using monetary policy to keep it that way," Mr. Williams said in a speech to a banker's group in New Jersey. For the Fed, "the approach we need is one of prudence, patience and good judgment. The motto of  data dependence' is more relevant than ever," the policy maker said.

Brainard Says Prolonged Political Uncertainty Could Weigh on Economic Activity

Federal governor Lael Brainard said "https://www.wsj.com/articles/feds-brainard-says-prolonged-political-uncertainty-could-weigh-on-economic-activity-11547851734?tesla=y" growing political uncertainty could weigh on consumer and business confidence, eventually crimping U.S. economic growth, in an interview that aired Friday evening. Ms. Brainard described the U.S. labor market as healthy but said political uncertainties ranging from the federal government's partial shutdown to the potential for a messy divorce between the U.K. and the European Union to trade tensions with China are negative threats to growth.

Central Banks Struggle With Policy Settings

The eurozone's economic slowdown has taken European Central Bank officials by surprise, potentially disrupting their plans to lift short-term interest rates this year. The shift underlines the difficulties central banks face getting back to growth rates and policy settings that were considered normal before the global financial crisis.

Fed's Bostic Downplays Impact of Balance-Sheet Drawdown

Federal Reserve officials may have "https://centralbanking.cmail20.com/t/d-l-nhkjttt-jlohthuq-x/" to changing the path of their balance-sheet drawdown if necessary, but so far they are showing no signs they think that's a step they will have to take.

For one thing, while financial markets have deemed the drawdown in the Fed's holdings of bonds a quantitative tightening meaning reduced Fed holdings are affecting financial conditions much like interest-rate rises Fed officials don't seem to share the market's assessment.

The Fed's balance-sheet drawdown began in late 2017. As part of the process of moving its monetary policy stance back to a more normal footing after years of crisis measures, it started allowing some Treasury and mortgage bonds in its then $4.5 trillion to mature and not be replaced. With the drawdown on a fixed schedule and essentially on autopilot, the Fed hoped changes in interest rates would be its main tool for influencing economy.

But as market volatility rose late last year, some traders and investors have said the Fed's balance-sheet shift is a big reason why the financial sector has become so unsettled. In a blog posting last week, Atlanta Fed leader Raphael Bostic took issue with that view.

He did so by looking at the returns investors demand to take on risk in bonds, the so-called term premia.

"Although estimating term premia is as much art as science, I don't think the evidence supports the argument that these premia have been materially rising as a result of our normalization process," Mr. Bostic wrote. "If the term premia channel is a critical piece of what makes balance-sheet policy work, I'm hard pressed to see much evidence of financial tightening via rising term premia in the data so far."

Getting a handle on what's going on with the balance sheet is difficult to do in the best of times, and the impact of the buying that took the Fed's balance sheet from a precrisis level of just above $800 billion to its $4.5 trillion peak is still debated. But Mr. Bostic's view that the drawdown isn't doing much to the market has supporters among private-sector analysts.

"There is little reason to think that actions emanating from the asset side of the Fed balance sheet are contributing to a meaningful tightening of financial conditions," J.P. Morgan's Michael Feroli said in a research note. And for that reason, he believes the Fed will continue to reduce its holdings, despite the market's concern over the outlook.

IMF Lowers 2019 Global Growth Forecast

The International Monetary Fund"https://www.wsj.com/articles/imf-lowers-2019-global-growth-forecast-11548075601?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=2&mod=djemCentralBanksPro&tpl=cb" for 2019 to 3.5%, down from 3.7% in October. A global recession isn't around the corner, "but the risk of a sharper decline in global growth has certainly increased," IMF Managing Director Christine Lagarde said.

Government Shutdown, Trade Tensions Weigh on U.S. Households

Fears over the global economy "https://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-consumer-sentiment-falls-11547825356?tesla=y" on American households even though demand for factory goods remains solid. An index of U.S. consumer sentiment fell to its lowest level in more than two years in January, the University of Michigan said Friday. Richard Curtin, the survey's economist, said the decline reflected worries about the government shutdown, trade tensions, volatile markets, a cooling global economy and uncertainty over what the Fed will do next. January's preliminary reading of 90.7 was the lowest since October 2016 and well below the forecast of economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal who had expected a reading of 96.4.

Shutdown Hits Industries Nationwide

By Gabriel T. Rubin

The partial government shutdown is affecting a wide range of business and financial concerns nationwide.

Shuttered government offices are stalling the approval of new loans, initial public offerings, the processing of tax documents, and the approval of new products such as prescription drugs, among other effects. While some programs are reopening on a temporary basis or providing workarounds for affected companies, most services won't return to normal until the government fully reopens and 800,000 federal workers sift through the backlog. "https://www.wsj.com/articles/shutdown-hits-industries-nationwide-11548075600?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=1&mod=djemCentralBanksPro&tpl=cb":

 "The partial closure of the Securities and Exchange Commission is delaying the ability of companies to open the IPO market. Companies that were seeking to list shares in January are delaying plans since the regulator has stopped reviewing and approving new and pending corporate registration statements. Publicly traded companies also could have to put shareholder proposals they oppose to a vote if the shutdown doesn't end before many U.S. companies hold annual meetings at the end of winter. Banking regulators remain open, operating on fees collecting from the companies they oversee.

 "Airlines expect to have sluggish revenue growth in the first quarter in part because of revenue lost from government travel cancellations. Delta Air Lines Inc. Chief Executive Ed Bastian, for instance, said the shutdown would cost his airline $25 million in lost revenue from government travel. Delta also can't start service on its new Airbus A220 airplanes until it gets approval from the Federal Aviation Administration.

 "The U.S. Food and Drug Administration has dramatically curtailed inspections of domestic facilities at food-processing companies during the shutdown, though unpaid inspectors have resumed work inspecting higher-risk products such as fresh fruits and vegetables, eggs, seafood and dairy products. Meanwhile, businesses seeking to launch new alcoholic beverage products can't get needed approvals in the tightly regulated industry. The FDA is set to run out of money in early February for evaluating new prescription drugs if the shutdown continues, potentially slowing the release of new therapies including cancer treatments.

 "At the Internal Revenue Service, the shutdown has created delays in getting some employer identification numbers, holding up some routine business deals. Many IRS auditors and collection workers are on furlough, making it difficult for tax practitioners to know if the government has received paperwork in business and individual cases. The IRS called workers back after mortgage lenders complained the government wasn't processing forms that lenders use to verify borrowers' incomes for loans.

 "Some small-business loans are also stuck in limbo. The Small Business Administration has stopped approving routine loans that the agency backs to ensure entrepreneurs have access to funds, halting their plans for expansion and repairs and forcing some owners to consider costlier sources of cash. The Agriculture Department's Farm Service Agency reopened on Thursday to help process loans and tax documents, but did so only for three days. The agency also wasn't processing new loans, just renewals of existing ones. The delay has also slowed regulations on an incentive for investing in low-income areas.

 "The government process for reviewing proposed mergers has been slowed by the shutdown, but it is still operating. Sizable mergers must be reported to the Federal Trade Commission and the Justice Department. Because the agencies don't want companies to be able to consummate problematic mergers without government scrutiny, they have concluded such matters are an essential function that can continue during the shutdown, but staff levels are limited and deadlines are slipping. The government also isn't processing streamlined approval for straightforward deals.

 "Businesses that have government contracts are feeling the strain across a variety of industries, including the building of highways and bridges. The nine major federal departments affected by the shutdown committed to spend $61 billion for contracted services in fiscal year 2018, according to public data, roughly 11% of the total $544 billion allocated for such work. Most government contractors are defense and military companies that are shielded from the shutdown. But some defense contractors are still feeling the strain.

 "The shutdown is having an effect on cryptocurrency markets. Bakkt, a bitcoin futures trading platform, cannot launch its contracts until the Commodity Futures Trading Commission weighs in on its business plan.

 "The shutdown has spread into space. Lockheed Martin warned in a regulatory filing that furloughs threaten the launch of a commercial satellite for a Saudi Arabian customer, as well as some other SpaceX launches. Lockheed needs government approval for a giant, Russian-owned cargo jet to fly satellites from California to Florida, and cautions that the timetable for some planned military launches later this year could slip.

Guide to pricing of Authorised Investment Funds.

Compiled with the assistance of the IMA. The Investment Management Association, 65 Kingsway, London WC2B 6TD. +44 (0)20 7831 0898. All funds within this section, whether OEICs or unit trusts are authorised in the UK by the Financial Services Authority. The prices quoted should only be used as a guide.

OEIC: Open-Ended Investment Company. Similar to a unit trust but using a company rather than a trust structure.

Share Classes: Separate classes of share are denoted by a letter or number after the name of the fund. Different classes are issued to reflect a different currency, charging structure or type of holder. Buying price: Also called offer price. The price at which units in a unit trust are bought by investors. Includes manager's initial charge. Selling price: Also called bid price. The price at which units in a unit trust are sold by investors.

Single price: Based on a mid-market valuation of the underlying investments. The buying and selling price for shares of an OEIC and units of a single priced unit trust are the same. Exit Charges: The letter E denotes that an exit charge may be made when you sell units, contact the manager/operator for full details. Time: The time shown alongside the fund manager's/operator's name is the time of the unit trust's/OEIC's valuation point unless another time is indicated by the symbol alongside the individual unit trust/OEIC name. The symbols are as follows: ? 0001 to 1100 hours; ? 1101 to 1400 hours; ?1401 to 1700 hours; # 1701 to midnight. Daily dealing prices are set on the basis of the valuation point, a short period of time may elapse before prices become available.

Yield: Funds comprising mainly of bonds normally quote a gross redemption yield after all charges but before tax has been deducted. Funds mainly made up of equities normally quote a yield representing the estimated annual payout net of tax for basic rate taxpayer. For further information contact the management company.

Historic pricing: The letter H denotes that the managers/operators will normally deal on the price set at the most recent valuation. The prices shown are the latest available before publication and may not be the current dealing levels because of an intervening portfolio revaluation or a switch to a forward pricing basis. The managers/operators must deal at a forward price on request, and may move to forward pricing at any time. Forward pricing: The letter F denotes that that managers/operators deal at the price to be set at the next valuation. Investors can be given no definite price in advance of the purchase or sale being carried out. The prices appearing in the newspaper are the most recent provided by the managers/operators. Scheme particulars, prospectus, key features and reports: The most recent report, scheme particulars, prospectus and key features document may be obtained free of charge from fund managers/operators.

New on Parents' To-Do List: Checking Children's Credit History; A new law allows free credit freezes to combat identity fraud targeting minors

By Yuka Hayashi 
1,115 words
18 January 2019
00:27
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

### picture data found, picture dimensions are 640 by 426, depth 1
pict089.jpg

Jill Carlon hugs her daughter, Avery, who has had to sit through multiple court hearings and learn about financial laws after becoming a victim of identity theft. PHOTO: Caitlin O'Hara for The Wall Street Journal

WASHINGTON Parents have a new item to add to their financial to-do list: check their child's credit history.

A new federal law going into effect in September will make it easier for families to combat the growing problem of "https://www.wsj.com/articles/identity-fraud-hits-record-number-of-people-1517922001" of minors, allowing them to make inquiries about credit files in their child's name and freeze a file at no cost.

Data-security experts say children are increasingly targeted by thieves who steal their Social Security numbers to "https://www.wsj.com/articles/the-new-id-theft-thousands-of-credit-applicants-who-dont-exist-1520350404", then open credit cards, take out loans or apply for public assistance. One credit-reporting company, Experian, estimates such identity theft will affect one in four children before they become an adult.

The ability to check children's credit reports is part of a broader banking law that also allowed unlimited, free credit freezes for adults, a response to "https://www.wsj.com/articles/equifax-reports-data-breach-possibly-impacting-143-million-u-s-consumers-1504819765" at Equifax Inc. last September.

Steps for Parents

* Go to "https://identitytheft.gov/Steps", scroll down and click on the Child Identity Theft section, under Special Forms of Identity Theft.

* That section has instructions on how to find out if your child has a credit report. Most young children shouldn't have credit files.

* The webpage includes contact information for credit agencies and how to freeze credit.

* Some experts recommend parents create a credit file for their children and freeze it, because the system is built on the idea that the first person to create a file is that person.

* Keep the record of freezes in a safe place, so the child or a guardian can find it when needed.

Criminals see the clean credit histories of children as particularly attractive, in part because parents rarely check on them. That often allows theft to go unnoticed for years, until the victims reach college age and start applying for credit cards and student loans. Also, a 2011 change in how the government assigns Social Security numbers to babies and new immigrants made it easier to steal numbers without being discovered, experts said.

Young children don't have credit files unless parents or guardians have opened them or someone committing fraud did so.

Last year, the Federal Trade Commission received 14,000 complaints involving identity theft targeting people 19 and younger, about 3.7% of complaints for all age groups. But experts say those numbers may understate the problem because parents are often unaware of children being targeted, and don't report it.

"We have found children are increasingly victims of identity theft," said Suzanne Barber, director of the Center for Identity at the University of Texas at Austin. "If I were going to patchwork an identity, whose would I want to use? I'd probably use the identity that is checked least often."

Al Pascual, an analyst specializing in fraud and security at Javelin Strategy & Research, said a child's personal information is more valuable on the dark web than an adult's, with a full set of personal information known as "fullz" name, date of birth, address and Social Security number "going for 10 times, 20 times more" than an adult file, which typically fetches $5 per person.

ID Analytics, an identity-fraud prevention unit of Symantec Corp., estimates 2.2% or one in every 50 of credit applications filed under newly issued Social Security numbers carried fake identities last year, up from 1.2% in 2013. Many of those numbers are believed to belong to young children.

Once a child's personal information is stolen, cleaning up the aftermath can be difficult. 

### picture data found, picture dimensions are 640 by 426, depth 1
pict090.jpg

Ms. Carlon holds hands with Avery. 'As a mother, I am supposed to be there to protect her and do everything I can,' she said. PHOTO: Caitlin O'Hara for The Wall Street Journal

Jill Carlon, a Peoria, Ariz., mother of three, said she spent seven years fixing her daughter's credit file after receiving a call in 2011 about a new J.C. Penney Co. credit card opened using her then 7-year-old daughter's Social Security number. More accounts followed, including at Dillards Inc., Macy's Inc. and Fry's Electronics Inc. Then collection notices came from a local hotel.

"As a mother, I am supposed to be there to protect her and do everything I can," said Ms. Carlon, a 46-year-old nurse. "It was out of my control."

Ms. Carlon tried to close the accounts, but was turned away because the name on the accounts didn't match her daughter's, Avery.

She called banks, credit-reporting companies, the police and lawyers, then turned to local city council members and U.S. senators for help. Ms. Carlon said the effort required stressful trips with her daughter to court and Social Security offices.

The Carlons changed their daughter's first and middle names in an attempt to get her a new Social Security number. The issue was finally resolved earlier this year when a new Social Security number was issued to Avery just before she started high school.

Once the provision in the new law goes into effect on Sept. 21, parents should contact the three main credit-reporting companies Experian, Equifax and TransUnion to check on their children's credit files, experts say. Most young children shouldn't have credit files. If that is confirmed, parents should either check back with the companies on a regular basis, or create a new file and freeze it, the option some experts recommend. 

### picture data found, picture dimensions are 640 by 426, depth 1
pict091.jpg

An old Social Security card issued to Avery Carlon. She had to change her identification number and legal name after falling victim to identity theft. PHOTO: Caitlin O'Hara for The Wall Street Journal

Ken Meiser, chief compliance officer at ID Analytics, recommends parents create a credit file for their children, because the credit-reporting system is built on the idea the first person to claim an identity is that person.

"It makes sense for a parent to make sure that they have created that file and protected it," Mr. Meiser said.

The FTC has a new page on its "https://identitytheft.gov/" website with a detailed guide on how to freeze credit files for adults and children. The page includes contact information for the credit-reporting companies and how to freeze credit.
