Features
Shut out of the boom

Javier Espinoza 
2,266 words
17 January 2019
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa
7
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Since its return to economic growth, Spain has seen a steady rise in private equity investments. But some fear these deals will kill jobs in a country where unemployment is the second highest in the EU. By Javier Espinoza

L ike his father before him, Josep Bayod Cueto worked at one of Spain's oldest companies for decades. It never occurred to the 59-year-old that he might lose his job at the sparkling winemaker Codornu even as the company prepared to sell the familyowned business to a US buyout fund. So when his redundancy notice arrived just over a year ago "it felt like a divorce", says Mr Bayod Cueto, sitting in a caf in the town of Sant Sadurn d'Anoia in Catalonia where Codornu has had its headquarters for almost 500 years. "I fear I won't be the last."

His position was one of 100   a sixth of the total workforce   axed in 2017 as Codornu began negotiating a  ¬390m sale, prompted by falling sales and shareholder disquiet, to the US-based Carlyle Group. Although the job losses predate completion of the Carlyle deal it has fuelled fears of further cuts in the town, whose 13,000 inhabitants rely on cava production to make a living.

The fallout from the job losses has also cast an uncomfortable light on the rising role of private equity in Spanish dealmaking. In the first nine months of 2018, foreign private equity funds invested a record  ¬4.35bn in Spain   according to an analysis by the Spanish Venture Capital and Private Equity Association (ASCRI).

In some quarters that investment is prompting anxiety over the expansion of what critics view as a ruthless form of capitalism.

According to ASCRI, the number of international funds   some of them with billions of dollars under management   operating in Spain has grown, from 157 in 2016 to 184 in 2017. Permira, BC Partners, Cinven and KKR have all struck deals in Spain recently against a backdrop of economic recovery.

"Spain is an attractive place to invest," says Lionel Assant, European head of private equity for the US buyout group Blackstone, which recently took control of the gaming group Cirsa. "It has grown above most other eurozone countries for the last few years on the back of the structural reforms the country undertook and we expect that healthy rate of growth to continue."

Last year, Luxembourg-based CVC, one of Europe's largest buyout funds, bought a 20 per cent stake in Gas Natural Fenosa from Spanish energy company Repsol in a  ¬3.82bn deal. The company was already planning to cut 1,400 jobs globally as part of a wider restructuring to save  ¬538m. But since the deal was struck the company, rebranded Naturgy, has axed 800 posts through voluntary redundancies, more than the 300 planned for 2018. Naturgy is now minority owned by a Spanish holding company, Criteria Caixa, which owns 24 per cent. Global Infrastructure Partners, a New York-based private equity fund, owns another 20 per cent.

It is not just at Naturgy that there are concerns. Across Spain, workers fear the impact these groups might have on their jobs. Private equity groups tend to make money by buying companies, improving their efficiency through job cuts and acquiring smaller businesses to merge with them with the view to selling the company for a profit two or three years down the line.

"These cuts are a way of generating more value for shareholders," says Toi Prieto, general secretary of the 117,000-member Unin Sindical Obrera and a longtime employee at Naturgy. "But we are slowly losing our culture. Following the voluntary redundancies the loyalty to the company has been diluted."

Acute scepticism Spain is not the first country to display ambivalence to private equity funds. Industry observers have likened the hostile reaction in Spain to that in Germany more than a decade ago when the chairman of the then ruling Social Democrat party likened financial investors to "a plague of locusts" descending only to strip firms of assets and shred jobs to make a quick buck.

Such scepticism is acute in Spain, a country battered by the financial crisis. At the height of the recession, in 2013, Spain had one of the worst unemployment rates in the EU, with nearly half of all young people without a job. The deep downturn was mainly a result of the crash of the local property market and disarray in the financial system.

The country is now enjoying some of the healthiest growth rates in the EU. Unemployment is expected to fall to 13.3 per cent by next year   still high at almost double the EU average, but down from 16 per cent in 2017 and close to the rate in 2008 before the crisis hit.

Labour reforms dating back to 2012 and an overhaul of the banking system have acted as catalysts for dealmaking, according to multiple private equity executives in the country.

"For many years Spain was a place to avoid for investors because we didn't enjoy a recovery as others in Europe did," says Miguel Zurita, chairman of ASCRI. "At some point there was even talk of Spain leaving the euro and unemployment figures were quite alarming. It was almost impossible to justify an investment in [the country]."

While there are domestic private equity players, it is overseas funds that are making inroads. It is now "an attractive destination", says Mr Zurita, who is also a managing partner at Altamar Capital Partners.

The relaxation of labour laws, making it easier to hire and fire, has been particularly attractive to buyout funds, adds Mr Zurita. "As an investor you prefer to invest where you have the ability to adjust to the market and labour is a key component." He stresses that buyout groups do not, however, "buy with the idea of cutting jobs" but they do want "to have the option".

Iaki Echave, former head of Spain for Blackstone, believes increased financing by local and global banks has been critical to foreign investors.

"For years banks were either unable or unwilling to lend. The reform of the financial system in Spain has been more extensive and deeper than anywhere else in the continent," he says. It means more loans are being made available to fund transactions. S&P Global Markets Intelligence says  ¬4.36bn was lent in 2017, up from just  ¬700m two years earlier, according to data from LCD.

Cash piles A growing economy   gross domestic product increased by 3 per cent in 2017   is not the only factor driving private equity investment. Concerns over Brexit have diverted attention to businesses in Spain, while groups including CVC, Carlyle and Apollo have also raised their largest ever funds.

Armed with that cash, private equity is thriving across Europe and in the US, where the largest transactions since the financial crisis took place last year. Private equity groups have in recent times influenced the shape of some of the largest businesses in the world   from Unilever to Thomson Reuters   deploying billions in loans and employing millions of people around the globe.

But this frenetic activity is triggering a backlash in Spain, with some characterising buyout groups as little more than rapacious, greedy financiers looking to ride the recent economic boom without thinking about the long-term future of these companies and their employees.

This image of the finance sector in Spain was cast during the country's financial crisis when distressed investors   mainly hedge funds   bet against the battered Spanish economy. Some of these made quick profits by buying companies low and selling assets high after the collapse of the property sector.

"We are not in distress any more but their actions created some noise," says Mr Echave. "Those investors have moved elsewhere but private equity is about the long term."

Negative headlines about private equity deals outside the country have dogged the sector in Spain, says Ludovic Phalippou, professor of finance at Oxford university's Sad Business School and author of Private Equity Laid Bare. "There is a sense of emergency that private equity funds bring with them. People know this and it worries them at the start of an investment."

He highlights a recent Washington Post article that accused Carlyle, the purchaser of Codornu, of putting financial considerations ahead of patient care at a nursing home it took over in Pennsylvania. Carlyle has said the quality of care at HCR ManorCare was not compromised by financial considerations. "Open any newspaper and you will find evidence of a bad image for private equity," says Prof Phalippou. "It is the purest version of capitalism on steroids and their ultimate goal is to make money. They don't care about many other things and that's where the tension with the rest of society comes in."

Rising opposition Mr Zurita points to a study commissioned by ASCRI showing that private equity-owned businesses in Spain added 7.6 per cent more jobs annually three years after acquisition. This compares with a 2.3 per cent rise in jobs for those that did not receive private equity backing, according to the report by Spain's Universidad Complutense de Madrid, based on 186 companies.

A survey conducted by Consumer Science &Analytics, a polling group, shows public opinion towards private equity funds in Spain is roughly split down the middle. The reaction to the influx of foreign buyers in parts of the Spanish media has been more partisan, with some employing nationalist or protectionist language.

Gonzalo Baratech, a newspaper columnist for Crnica Global in Barcelona, described the Codornu sale as being "positive to the shareholders" but "calamitous for the country because we are losing control of a company from these lands, one of the oldest in Spain and Europe".

The political debate in Spain has not reached the high pitch it hit in Germany or even in the UK when buyout funds were under public scrutiny over the little tax they paid. But on a local level Rafa Berlanga, a politician in Sant Sadurn, sums up the fears of many in his community: "Private equity groups are like a virus, they suck in as much juice and then leave the carcass behind."

ASCRI's Mr Zurita insists that private equity funds add value to society. "It is not only good for us to have a healthy private equity industry but it is good for the Spanish economy as well."

At the time of the purchase, Carlyle sought to reassure the Codornu workers by highlighting its record of buying family-owned businesses. Alex Wagenberg, managing director at the US buyout group, said: "We hope to build on this successful trajectory by supporting the company with growing its global footprint, both organically and through acquisitions."

But in Sant Sadurn workers are wary. Codornu's head of marketing and other executives lost their jobs at the end of last year. Some expect Carlyle to identify further cuts in administrative roles. "There will be some fat to trim," says a longtime Codornu employee.

Antonio Cruces, a longtime worker at the company and a prominent union leader, sums up the view of many in his town: "These funds move like the lion in the savannah: they prey on the weak."

Deals Tourism and finance seen as attractive sectors Target Cirsa Gaming Corporation Buyer Blackstone Price  ¬2bn Year 2018 Spain's largest casino operator, pictured, also manages thousands of slot machines and many bingo halls. Long regarded as a good barometer of Spanish consumer confidence, its revenues took a big hit at the height of the financial crisis but have since recovered.

Blackstone, which bought 100 per cent of Cirsa, fought off some of the largest names in private equity, including Apollo Global Management, to secure the deal.

Target Allfunds Bank Buyer Hellman & Friedman and Singapore's GIC Price  ¬1.8bn Year 2017 As in other recent large deals, competition for Allfunds Bank was subject to an intense bidding battle with a string of foreign buyers vying to buy an asset that was once part controlled by Spanish banking group Santander.

Among the bidders were the Chinese investor group Legend, Advent International and Permira. Hellman & Friedman and the Singapore sovereign wealth fund GIC won out against a backdrop of opposition to Chinese ownership.

Target Hotelbeds Buyer Cinven and Canada Pension Plan Investment Board Price  ¬1.2bn Year 2016 This deal was a bet on growing tourism demand in Spain. Individual tourist spending has been increasing every year since 2012 and, according to multiple analysts, it is likely to grow further thanks partly to troubles in holiday destinations in the Middle East and Africa.

The acquisition of Hotelbeds, which sells rooms to travel agents and tour operators, is an example of how private equity buyers have spotted this opportunity. Swedish fund EQT was reportedly also interested in the business.

Boom time In the first nine months of 2018, foreign private equity funds invested a record  ¬4.35bn in Spain Green shoots The country's economic recovery and post-crisis reforms have attracted the buyout industry Cash rich Having raised some of their biggest ever funds, private equity firms are thriving in Europe and the US

WSJ Pro
Brands Seek Deeper Insights From AI; Marketers want to understand what their machines are seeing

By Nat Ives 
826 words
17 January 2019
22:35
WSJ Pro Artificial Intelligence
RSTPROAI
English
Copyright  2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Marketers increasingly are using AI tools to try to show the right people the right messages at the right time. For many, the next step is to learn something deeper from the results.

"I can answer specifically why a particular decision was made," said Steve Nowlan, senior vice president of decision sciences at Conversant LLC, which uses machine learning to help brands recognize and engage consumers. Machine learning is the subset of AI in which systems are trained to "learn" complex tasks without being fed every rule.

Conversant can explain, for example, that an algorithm decided to serve a particular impression to a certain consumer at a given time because it was most likely generate a purchase, Mr. Nowlan said. "That's an absolutely true statement," he said, "but it's also a really uninformative statement for the marketer because they're looking for something that can guide their strategy."

Conversant and a client, the vitamins and supplements marketer Swanson Health Products Inc., had to figure out what was going on during a campaign when they realized that Swanson's revenue was starting to decline even though the number of its customers was increasing.

It turned out that Swanson was winning new buyers among a desirable, younger demographic but was getting them through online shopping platforms that didn't encourage brand loyalty as much as Swanson's own home page. Conversant and Swanson responded by creating campaigns designed to find such new customers and steer them toward the company site.

AI "isn't just,  turn it on and let it run,'" said Corey Bergstrom, chief marketing and merchandising officer at Swanson.

Human Vision

Advance Auto Parts Inc. uses machine learning in part to predict whether customers are likely to keep spending or drop off. "Our model predicted really well who was going to reduce spending," said Yogi Jashnani, chief marketing officer at the company. "But we needed to build intuition into that."

In some ways, marketing AI is nothing but making significantly better predictions about what people will do, Mr. Jashnani said. "Better prediction is not as valuable an additive if you don't combine that with judgment."

Advance Auto Parts built a lot of its AI-based predictive spending models in-house, according to Mr. Jashnani. "It also forced me to be a better customer of data scientists and a better student of AI," he said. "CMOs need to have that skill set going forward."

The good news is that it isn't that hard to understand the reasoning behind the machines, or at least not yet, said Kristin Lemkau, chief marketing officer at JPMorgan Chase & Co. "Right now, you're just making your existing campaign management more efficient &through an attribute-based campaign process that you can understand," she said.

Gaining useful strategic insights already takes effort, others argued.

"Tools don't provide insights," said Lauren Radcliffe, head of media and acquisition strategy at E*Trade Financial Corp. "They provide incredible platforms for us to do quantitative testing so that we can analyze the data and understand what's happening. The tool will never tell you the why."

Sometimes not knowing exactly why is fine, Ms. Radcliffe added. Brands can set AI loose to chase relatively straightforward goals like finding qualified prospects to open an account at certain levels, she said. "All they're doing is finding people in the universe who are most likely to convert on the goal they're giving them. The brilliance of AI and big data is I don't know why anyone would even try to understand it."

In other instances, it's important to interrogate an unexpected or disappointing result. "That's where the people come in," Ms. Radcliffe said. "I would never trust a machine to analyze that level of data ever."

AI "certainly might say,  Change tactics here or change audiences or pricing or some kind of element,' but it doesn't necessarily say why,'" said Hannah Mirza, former global head of media partnerships at MediaCom, a media planning and buying agency within WPP PLC's GroupM.

As AI becomes more complex, moreover, it will be more important to bring the right kind of talent into marketing to help understand it. "In my experience, it's the biggest gap in terms of mathematicians and data analysts coming into the sector in the volume we need them," Ms. Mirza said.

Pro Tips

* Have the right talent in-house and at your vendors. Without experts who understand AI, gleaning insights will be next to impossible.

* Don't be overly reliant on an AI-vendor-driven decisions. Marketers often have the most telling picture of their own customers and businesses.

* Don't dive deep into every AI result. Interrogating the "black box" isn't always worth the effort.

Egis Capital Partners and ABS Capital Partners have acquired a majority interest in ClearObject, an Internet of Things integration provider for data and asset intensive industries.

Fishers, Ind.-based ClearObject partners with software vendors to build platforms and integrate services such as machine learning modeling, big data analytics, as well as track and trace asset management. Its partners include International Business Machines Corp. and Alphabet Inc.'s Google Cloud Platform, according to the company's website.

ClearObject was founded in 2010. Its products serve industries that include energy, automotive, agriculture, food services and manufacturing.

Indianapolis, Ind.-based ClearObject, which was formerly known as CloudOne Inc., has received several rounds of venture funding over the years. In December 2014, the company "https://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFVW00120141205eac5nbpok?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=2" in a Series D round led by Plymouth Growth Partners (formerly Plymouth Ventures) and with participation from existing investors that included Elevate Ventures and HKS Capital Advisors. In February of that same year the company "https://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFVW00020140210ea2aogdza?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=3" led by Bootstrap Venture Fund. In 2016 the company sold $4.2 million in equity, according to a report by WSJ Pro Venture Capital.

Egis Capital Partners led and sourced the deal, with ABS co-leading the new funding, according to the release. Needham & Co. LLC provided financial counsel to ClearObject.

Summit, N.J.-based Egis makes control buyout and growth investments in technology-related businesses in the security and protection industry. The firm typically invests in companies with enterprise values of between $30 million and $200 million and that require equity investments of $10 million to $80 million of equity, according to the firm's website.

ABS Capital, which has offices in Baltimore and San Francisco, makes investments in later-stage growth companies. Most recently its portfolio company Inttra Inc., a transportation software company for ocean shippers and carriers, was acquired by E2open LLC, an Insight Venture Partners-backed company.

Features
Why Fink's heir is not yet apparent

ROBIN WIGGLESWORTH 
1,636 words
16 January 2019
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Main
17
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

BlackRock boasts a 'deep bench' of executives from which to choose a successor if and when founder retires

Last week, Larry Fink promoted Mark Wiedman, the head of BlackRock's sprawling index investing business, to a new enlarged role that thrusts him to the top of the list of executives likely to take up the reins of the $6.4tn asset management group if its founder decides to step down.

However, Mr Fink likes to brag about BlackRock's "deep bench" of senior executives and in his memo announcing Mr Wiedman's promotion he hinted at a broader reshuffle later this year. Insiders say that no one senior is expected to leave, but new frontrunners could still emerge as roles are rejigged.

While there are no hints that Black-Rock's founder plans to step down, about half a dozen people are broadly considered by observers to be in the running to succeed Mr Fink when he does retire: three Marks, two Robs and one Rich.

Mark Wiedman

Until this week, Mr Wiedman was head of BlackRock's $4.1tn index investing group, which includes the asset manager's crown jewel   iShares, the rapidly growing exchange-traded fund business acquired from Barclays following the financial crisis.

Mr Wiedman is a gregarious Long Island native who joined BlackRock in 2004 after stints at McKinsey and as an adviser to the US Treasury. His first job was at an advisory group that counselled BlackRock clients such as central banks on market issues, but he rose to head corporate strategy and in 2011 was put in charge of the iShares business and tasked with integrating it fully into BlackRock.

When he took over iShares, it had about $600bn in assets. Today, it manages $1.8tn, and accounted for almost a third of BlackRock's revenue in the third quarter. Mr Wiedman is now overseeing all international operations, corporate strategy and marketing. "The future is all about creating new markets," Mr Wiedman said in a 2012 interview with the FT. "The idea that giant insurance companies that are accustomed to buying individual bonds or derivatives are now beginning to use ETFs as core parts of their portfolios, that is totally new and we are starting to see it globally."

Mark McCombe

Mr McCombe, the San Francisco-based head of Americas at BlackRock, is a more recent arrival, having been poached from HSBC in 2011. At HSBC, he ran the asset management arm and ultimately its Hong Kong operations before jumping ship for BlackRock.

About 40 per cent of BlackRock's client assets are managed out of San Francisco, where Barclays Global Investors was based before Mr Fink swooped for the group in 2009. The location allows Mr McCombe to be BlackRock's main liaison when it comes to California's big pension funds as well as the growing Asian customer base, a priority for the investment group in the coming years.

His ties to Asia and Hong Kong especially are extensive, and in 2006 he was awarded an OBE in the Queen's New Year honours list.

As a point person for BlackRock's US pension business, he has focused on retirement as a live wire subject. "We want to take a lead in addressing what we believe is one of the most significant and important societal issues we face today," he said at BlackRock's investor day last year.

Mark Wiseman

Another relatively new arrival at Black-Rock, Mr Wiseman was chief executive at the Canada Pension Plan Investment Board   one of the biggest and most sophisticated pension schemes in the world   before Mr Fink brought him to BlackRock in 2016 (where his wife Marcia Moffat already worked). The hockey-loving Canadian chairs BlackRock Alternative Investors, where he is working on building up its private equity and debt investing business. But his main job is to run the asset manager's $366bn global active equities business.

This unit is a profitable but long under-performing corner of the investment group. Mr Wiseman is an advocate of integrating more advanced analytics, systematic investing techniques, big data and technology broadly into the traditional stockpicking process. He is trying to use BlackRock's Systematic Active Equities, its San Francisco-based quant arm, to modernise the rest of its stockpicking operations.

"If you're a traditional portfolio manager it's a challenging time because there's so much change happening around you," Mr Wiseman told the FT last year. "If you're unwilling to evolve you're likely going to get eaten alive."

Rob Goldstein

Mr Goldstein is the youngest member of BlackRock's global executive committee, but the only one to have spent his entire professional career there.

He joined BlackRock in 1994 as a 20-year-old analyst in its portfolio analytics group and has been instrumental in building up Aladdin, the famed analytics and risk management platform that underpins all of the group's operations and has been sold to other asset managers around the world.

Short for Asset Liability and Debt and Derivative Investment Network, Aladdin is now used by 25,000 other investment managers that manage more than $18tn. It is the centrepiece of Black-Rock's technology services sales, which were a bright spot in the third quarter.

Mr Goldstein is now chief operating officer, in charge of the BlackRock Solutions unit that houses Aladdin, and oversees all technology at the investment group. Last year, he championed a new "BlackRock Lab for Artificial Intelligence" in Palo Alto, California, on the view that machine learning can enhance its operations across the board.

"AI may be in a big hype cycle but there's no doubt the technology is real and changing the world already," Mr Goldstein said in an interview with the FT at the time. "This is about increasing the efficiency of what we do across the board. Every part of our business is already being augmented by these new capabilities."

Rob Kapito

Mr Kapito is one of the co-founders of BlackRock, its president since 2007 and Mr Fink's right-hand man. He is in charge of all day-to-day operations of its major units. Before becoming president, he was in charge of all of BlackRock's portfolio management across equities, bonds and alternative investments.

As a co-founder and top executive who worked with Mr Fink at First Boston (later Credit Suisse First Boston) as a mortgage trader in the 1980s, Mr Kapito was long considered the favourite to take over as BlackRock's chief executive if the top man had snagged the Treasury Secretary job he has long wanted.

But observers expect that Mr Kapito's age   he is just five years younger than BlackRock's chief executive and turns 62 on February 8   means that he could now lose out to one of the younger guns.

Mr Kapito has warned that many financial services companies were too complacent about the threat of disruptive technology and new entrants. "Apple was not in the music industry, Google was not in the mobile phone industry and Amazon was not in the groceries business   until they were," he said at Davos last year. "Tech companies are going to enter the financial services market in a very, very aggressive way."

Rich Kushel

Mr Kushel is not as well-known as some of the other members of "Larry's bench". But insiders say the BlackRock veteran   he joined the group in 1991   is well-regarded and among the top half-dozen candidates to succeed Mr Fink.

After a series of senior positions, Mr Kushel took charge of BlackRock's global fixed income investing in February 2018, in addition to leading multi-asset strategies. BlackRock's bond group manages almost a third of its overall assets and accounted for about $2bn of revenues in 2017, or roughly one-sixth of all that year's income.

At BlackRock's investor day in 2018, Mr Kushel argued that the typical investor approach to portfolio construction had not evolved much since the theories put forward by Nobel laureate Harry Markowitz in the 1950s   and was thus a big opportunity for a big, broad financial supermarket like BlackRock. "We believe that BlackRock could and should do better and set the objective to raise the bar, update current practice and apply a more modern and rigorous process to build better portfolios for our clients," he said. "I believe that portfolio construction is one of the fastestgrowing opportunities in the industry and an area where we can really differentiate BlackRock."

Bombshell: Top dealmaker chooses money over dream job

Welcome to Due Diligence, the FT s daily deals briefing

FT premium subscribers can click here to receive Due Diligence every day by email.

September s news that UBS s uber dealmaker Andrea Orcel was jumping ship to lead Santander sent shockwaves through the European banking world. But there were even bigger ricochets on Tuesday when it emerged that the Italian would in fact not be joining the Spanish bank.

The passionate banker, who told the FT back in 2015 that he d quite like to be chief executiveof a bank some day, originally hoped to start his new role as early as January after stepping down as head of UBS s investment bank in September.

Then came a delay. Forced to serve his full six months gardening leave at UBS, Orcel couldn t start until around April   followed by Tuesday s bombshell that Santander and Orcel had failed to reach a deal on giving him  ¬50m of Santander stock to make up for what he was forfeiting at UBS.

The news was greeted with disbelief by finance folks on both sides of the Atlantic. How could one of Europe s most eminent bankers have got the biggest deal of his own career so wrong, agreeing to go to Santander without a watertight agreement on pay?

And, from Santander s side, how could it so publicly name a new CEO without doing the basic maths   and negotiation   on what it would take to get him to sign on the dotted line and show up for work in Madrid?

To be more exact, according to people informed about the matter, Orcel did have a letter of guarantee from Ana Botn, Santander s executive chair,and she tried her hardest to get his pay deal approved.But the board simply overruled her.

Santander had hoped UBS wouldn t claw back all of the compensation, which would have left the Spanish bank with a smaller bill to pay. But UBS wouldn t budge, Santander s board balked at the 10-digit sum, and the marriage was off.

Which leaves us with a lot of speculation over Orcel s next steps.

His departure from UBS is well and truly final   he s already been replaced there with two co-heads of the investment bank, and the Swiss lender is reportedly courting former Orcel rival Christian Meissner for a senior leadership role.

Is it time for the Orcel & Co boutique? Will he try his hand at running a bank in his native Italyor give Wall Street another shot? Or have a few months of life s more leisurely pursuits given him a taste for actual retirement?

There s a side issue too:can UBS keep Santander as one of its biggest financial institutions group clients after playing hardball and depriving the Spanish bank of its would-be CEO? Let the pitching begin 	. 	. 	.

Gold miners join the deal frenzy

Activity is finally stirring in mining. Resources bankers have emerged from their bunkers and are gearing up for a year of deals in the gold mining sector, following a multiyear drought.

The action was kicked off by Barrick Gold s John Thornton, a Goldman Sachsveteran, who paid $6bn to acquire Randgold and its (big game hunting)South African boss Mark Bristow last year. The union, struck in September, led the two sides toproclaimthey had created the world s largest gold company.

Not so fast. This week US gold miner Newmont Mining, which was founded in 1916, said it would acquire struggling Canadian miner Goldcorp for $12.5bn including debt   creating an even bigger gold company. However, the market reaction has been poor and investors are grumbling.

Bankers predict that every gold company will consider similar moves in a sector that s in dire need of consolidation. Gold miners have barely made investors any money over the past five years, despite a rising gold price. Instead they ve spent a lot on executive compensation and in ill-timed acquisitions. Investors will now be pressing companies to act.

There are a handful of mid-tier gold companies with depressed share prices thatcould be next, bankers say. These include Canada s Kinross Gold and Yamana Gold. Shares in Kinross have fallen by 28 per cent over the past year while Yamana is off by 27 per cent.

South African gold miner AngloGold Ashanti could be one company looking for assets. AngloGold s new chief executive is Kelvin Dushnisky, an ambitious executive who was previously at Barrick. He s keen to build the company s international operations and list in London, according to people familiar with his thinking.

Shares in AngloGold have risen by 24 per cent over the past year in Johannesburg, a rare bright spot in the sector.

Watch this space.

John Malone s complex empire is tripping up credit default swaps traders. Again.

Regular DD readers will be well versed in recent shenanigans in credit derivatives, after we took you behind the scenes of the  manufactured default  furore last year, which saw Blackstone face off against Goldman Sachs in a bitter spat over a money spinning trick many thought was tantamount to manipulation.

But this time, it s not devious schemes that are causing headaches for banks. Instead it s a consequence of collective incompetence: read Robert Smith s story about how Dutch telecoms groupVodafoneZiggo s CDS contracts might become worthless to get up to speed.

The basic problem is this: CDS traders didn t notice that the company   which is jointly owned by Vodafone and John Malone s Liberty Global   moved its bonds to a new entity in March, meaning the CDS references a now non-existent special purpose vehicle. Even worse: it might now be too late to fix it.

The thing is, the most famous example of this phenomenon happening in the past was also with Liberty Global: avery similar glitchhappened with Malone s German cable company Unitymedia back in 2012.

The uncharitable view is that CDS traders are paid vast sums of money to track the companies they buy and sell derivatives on, and they shouldn t miss a major change for the best part of the year.

The more sympathetic view is that Malone s shifting web of companies is so difficult to track that even people with vast sums of money on the line make mistakes.

If you want a flavour of this complex dealmaking, check out Lex on his latest ploy atLiberty Latin America.

 Thanks Jamie 

Spare a thought for Mike Gaito and Michele Colocci, JPMorgan Chase s co-heads of healthcare investment banking, who had to listen to their chief executive Jamie Dimon publicly hypinga competitor on Tuesday.

Asked about the bank s latest quarterly earnings by Jefferies analyst Ken Usdin, Dimon launched into an ode to some of Gaito and Colocci s rivals.

 I just want to give a shout out to Jefferies,  Dimon said. You guys did a hell of a good job in healthcare last year. 

Kind words, given that JPMorgan was top of the healthcare investment banking leagues for both US and global fees last year, while Jefferies was fourth in both league tables, according to Dealogic.

Jefferies has been on the rise though   in 2017 it was seventh in both the global and US healthcare leagues.

 Thanks Jamie,  Jefferies healthcare banker Michael Brinkman tweeted.

Apax Partners has bought a significant minority stake in artificial-intelligence technology company Fractal Analytics for $200 million.

A person familiar with the deal said the transaction valued Fractal at $500 million.

The London private-equity firm injected fresh capital in Fractal Analytics, and bought stakes from investors including U.S. firm TA Associates and Khazanah Nasional Bhd., a Malaysian government investment fund, according to a statement released by Fractal Analytics.

Fractal Analytics' sales totaled $100 million in 2018, the person familiar with the matter said.

The Indian company sells products including Qure, a health-care imaging tool that is used for interpreting X-rays, and Cuddle, a system for detecting anomalies in business data. The company said Apax's investment will help fund its growth projects.

Its clients have included Google Inc. and Visa Inc., the statement said. Founded in 2000 in India and now based in New York, the company employs more than 1,200 people in 16 offices world-wide, it said.

TA acquired a minority stake in Fractal Analytics for $25 million in 2013, Private Equity News previously reported.

AI is expanding fast. About half of 2,135 managers surveyed by McKinsey & Co. for a report published in November said they already deployed an AI-based system, and nearly three-quarters said they expect AI investments to increase.

Apax made the investment from the $9 billion global buyout fund it closed in 2016, a second person familiar with the matter said.

The Property Report
Blackstone Property Fund to Set Record

By Peter Grant 
531 words
16 January 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B6
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Blackstone Group LP is about to finish raising the largest-ever real-estate fund, which at $20 billion will amplify the investment firm's influence over prices paid for major office towers, shopping centers and hotels around the world.

The $20 billion that Blackstone has raised from U.S. and overseas pension funds, foreign governments and wealthy individuals is more than twice as large as any fund ever raised by a competitor, according to data firm Preqin.

The fund is expected to close in the first quarter of 2019 for all but some of its smaller individual investors, according to people familiar with the matter.

The record size reflects both the New York firm's history of double-digit returns and a herd effect, where pension-fund managers and other big investors tend to favor the same brand names with established track records.

Blackstone's actual buying power with the new fund is closer to $60 billion. That is because its real-estate funds typically uses $2 of debt for every dollar of equity, say people familiar with firm's strategy. That $60 billion is more than the total value of all the commercial property purchased in New York, Chicago and San Francisco in the first 11 months of 2018, according to Real Capital Analytics.

Blackstone's so-called "opportunistic" real-estate funds, which take higher risks to go after higher returns, have delivered annual average net returns of 16% putting them in the top quadrant compared with their peers over the past 27 years, according to filings by the firm.

It may be more difficult for Blackstone to repeat its past heady performance with its new fund, many property analysts say.

After a decadelong bull market, there is growing concern that property prices might fall, especially if interest rates rise. Real estate is interest-rate sensitive, partly because buyers typically use a lot of leverage.

Not all Blackstone's investments have been big hits. In 2015, for one, it purchased office buildings and other properties in Brazil, hoping to take advantage of low prices resulting from political and economic upheaval in that country. The Brazil property market has yet to rebound.

Still, Blackstone's fundraising success comes as peers struggled to raise funds. Last year, real-estate funds world-wide raised only $118 billion, the smallest amount since 2013, according to Preqin.

Even before its latest fund, Blackstone had the record for raising the four largest funds, according to Preqin. Its previous real-estate fund, at $15.8 billion, currently holds the record.

Giant firms like Blackstone and Brookfield Property Group typically have an easier time raising money than smaller firms. Investment officers working for pension funds, endowments and other institutional investors prefer firms with marquee names and long track records because they have less explaining to do if things go wrong.

Blackstone had $119.9 billion in real-estate assets under management at the end of the third quarter of 2018, more than five times the $23 billion it managed when Blackstone did its initial public offering in 2007.

Despite Mark Wiedman s promotion, observers say other executives remain in the running

Last week, Larry Fink promoted Mark Wiedman, the head of BlackRock s sprawling index investing business, to a new enlarged role that thrusts him to the top of the list of executives likely to take up the reins of the $6.4tn asset management group if its founder decides to step down.

However, Mr Fink likes to brag about BlackRock s  deep bench  of senior executives, and in his memo announcing Mr Wiedman s promotion he hinted at a broader reshuffle later this year. Insiders say that no one senior is expected to leave, but new frontrunners could still emerge as roles are rejigged.

While there are no hints of the BlackRock founder planning to step down, about a half-dozen people are broadly considered by internal and external observers to be in the running to succeed Mr Fink when he does retire: three Marks, two Robs, and one Rich.

Mark Wiedman

Until this week, Mr Wiedman was head of BlackRock s $4.1tn index investing group, which includes asset manager s crown jewel   iShares, the rapidly growing exchange-traded fund business acquired from Barclays in the wake of the financial crisis.

Mr Wiedman is a gregarious Long Island native, who joined BlackRock in 2004 after stints at McKinsey and as an adviser to the US Treasury. His first job was at an advisory group that counselled BlackRock clients such as central banks on market issues, but rose to head corporate strategy and in 2011 was put in charge of the iShares business and tasked with integrating it fully into BlackRock.

When he took over iShares, it had about $600bn in assets. Today, it manages $1.8tn, and accounted for almost a third of BlackRock s revenue in the third quarter. Mr Wiedman is now overseeing all international operations, corporate strategy and marketing.

 The future is all about creating new markets,  Mr Wiedman said in a 2012 interview with the FT.  The idea that giant insurance companies that are accustomed to buying individual bonds or derivatives are now beginning to use ETFs as core parts of their portfolios, that is totally new and we are starting to see it globally.

Rob Kapito

Mr Kapito is one of the co-founders of BlackRock, its president since 2007 and Larry Fink s right-hand man. The second-highest paid person at the asset manager is in charge of all day-to-day operations of its major units. Rich Kushel

After a series of senior positions, Mr Kushel took charge of BlackRock s global fixed income investing in February 2018, in addition to his job leading multi-asset strategies. BlackRock s bond group manages almost a third of its overall assets, and accounted for about $2bn of revenues in 2017, or roughly one-sixth of all that year s income.

At BlackRock s investor day in 2018, Mr Kushel argued that the typical investor approach to portfolio construction had not evolved much since the theories first put forward by Nobel laureate Harry Markowitz in the 1950s   and was thus a big opportunity for a big, broad financial supermarket like BlackRock.

 We believe that BlackRock could and should do better and set the objective to raise the bar, update current practice and apply a more modern and rigorous process to build better portfolios for our clients,  he said.  I believe that portfolio construction is one of the fastest-growing opportunities in the industry and an area where we can really differentiate BlackRock.

WSJ Pro
Partners Group: Competition, Speedy Auctions Will Pose Challenge This Year; A more selective investing strategy saw the Swiss firm screen 1,000 fewer assets last year, even as it invested more money

By William Louch 
540 words
15 January 2019
14:17
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

More competition, higher prices and speedier auctions will make life difficult for buyout firms this year, according to David Layton, co-chief executive officer of Partners Group Holding AG.

"The level of competition we see is significant. If I look back over the last number of years, we've seen asset prices increase pretty significantly," Mr. Layton said on a conference call after the Swiss alternative investment firm reported its updated assets under management.

Meanwhile, changes in how companies present their accounts is making it harder to assess their earnings, Mr. Layton said an issue that is compounded by faster, more competitive sale processes.

"The percentage of marketed earnings that are attributable to add backs or [earnings before interest, tax, depreciation and amortization] adjustments have increased," Mr. Layton said. "Sale process timelines have also contracted meaningfully. We've seen them as short as 60 days recently," he added.

Add-backs are "https://www.wsj.com/articles/cummings-take-add-backs-fill-deals-with-hot-air-1538170508?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=2" such as difficult-to-predict estimates of the cost savings that would result from a merger.

Partners is taking a more selective approach to deal-making in an effort to mitigate the effects of the current investment climate. The firm has narrowed its focus to certain sectors that it believes to be resilient, such as big data, health-and-wellness and software.

The firm said it screened more than 2,800 potential direct investments last year across asset classes, more than 1,000 fewer than in 2017. From that reduced pool, the firm made 77 investments, meaning it rejected around 97% of the assets it looked at, down fractionally from 98% in 2017.

Despite increasing competition and the drop in the number of direct investments, the firm deployed a record total of nearly $20 billion across its strategies a sign of its "https://www.wsj.com/articles/partners-group-makes-a-splash-with-deal-spree-1530617401?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=1". Among the notable investments Partners completed last year was the  ¬4.6 billion acquisition of German smart metering business Techem GmbH by a consortium it led.

Partners, like private-equity firms in general, has benefited from the rising tide of asset prices in recent years. Mr. Layton said the firm will have to work harder to grow the businesses it backs in the years ahead.

"In an environment like this where I think we can all expect to get less and less from the market itself, we have to be focused on building value asset by asset," he said.

The firm said growing its industry value-creation team is a key priority for this year, part of a wider hiring spree that is expected to add 300 more executives to its books in the next two years.

Partners said its assets under management rose to  ¬72.8 billion as of Dec. 31, an increase of  ¬10.9 billion year-over-year.

On the fundraising front, Partners said it expects client demand of between  ¬13 billion and  ¬16 billion, putting it on course to least match the  ¬13 billion raised last year.

WSJ Pro
Former Executives of Engie Seek  ¬300 Million for Clean Energy Fund

By William Louch 
503 words
15 January 2019
12:00
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

The three co-founders of TiLT Capital Partners have more than altruism on their mind as they pitch a debut impact fund focused on clean energy: Their share of the firm's profits is directly tied to the social and environmental impact of its investments.

The Paris firm is seeking as much as  ¬300 million ($344.3 million) to invest in U.S. and European businesses that stand to benefit from a shift toward cleaner, more environmentally-friendly energy sources, according to documents sent to investors that were reviewed by WSJ Pro. The fund will focus on areas that include energy-efficient technologies, renewable energy generation and storage, data analytics as well as electric vehicles and related technologies.

The fledgling firm was launched by Nicolas Piau, Nathanael Krivine and Nicolas Lepareur, former deal makers of French utility company Engie SA. TiLT Capital plans to take minority stakes in companies that it could hold for as long as 10 years, a somewhat unconventional strategy. Typically, private-equity firms seek controlling stakes in their assets and sell them within a five- to seven-year period.

Despite strong investor appetite for the wider private-equity asset class, first-time funds had a difficult time on the fundraising trail in Europe last year. Signs of an overheating market prompted some investors to concentrate their relationships with a smaller group of experienced managers.

Last year saw the total volume of cash raised by first-time buyout and growth funds fall for the second consecutive year, with 23 funds closing on  ¬4.4 billion in 2018, according to data provider Preqin Ltd. That is down from the  ¬4.8 billion raised by 21 funds in 2017.

TiLT Capital's founders collectively have more than 40 years of experience working in senior positions at Engie, including Mr. Piau's role as global head of mergers and acquisitions at the company. During their time at Engie, the trio helped manage 40 greenfield developments worth about  ¬5 billion and led 37 acquisitions, according to the documents.

Alongside its commitment to investing in environmentally friendly energy technology, the firm is tying its founders' compensation to whether its investments have a positive social impact. The carried interest the slice of the profits buyout executives take from successful investments will be tied to the environmental, social and governance performance of its investments as well as financial performance, according to the documents.

Impact funds, which seek both strong financial returns as well as some social or environmental benefits, have become more popular with investors in recent years. Some of the world's largest investment firms have launched impact funds or are in the midst of pitching them. Swiss private-equity firm Partners Group, for example, is seeking to raise $1 billion for a new impact fund, while TPG Capital is seeking $3.5 billion for its second TPG Rise Fund.

Guide to pricing of Authorised Investment Funds.

Compiled with the assistance of the IMA. The Investment Management Association, 65 Kingsway, London WC2B 6TD. +44 (0)20 7831 0898. All funds within this section, whether OEICs or unit trusts are authorised in the UK by the Financial Services Authority. The prices quoted should only be used as a guide.

OEIC: Open-Ended Investment Company. Similar to a unit trust but using a company rather than a trust structure.

Share Classes: Separate classes of share are denoted by a letter or number after the name of the fund. Different classes are issued to reflect a different currency, charging structure or type of holder. Buying price: Also called offer price. The price at which units in a unit trust are bought by investors. Includes manager's initial charge. Selling price: Also called bid price. The price at which units in a unit trust are sold by investors.

Single price: Based on a mid-market valuation of the underlying investments. The buying and selling price for shares of an OEIC and units of a single priced unit trust are the same. Exit Charges: The letter E denotes that an exit charge may be made when you sell units, contact the manager/operator for full details. Time: The time shown alongside the fund manager's/operator's name is the time of the unit trust's/OEIC's valuation point unless another time is indicated by the symbol alongside the individual unit trust/OEIC name. The symbols are as follows:m0001 to 1100 hours;. 1101 to 1400 hours;.1401 to 1700 hours; # 1701 to midnight. Daily dealing prices are set on the basis of the valuation point, a short period of time may elapse before prices become available.

Yield: Funds comprising mainly of bonds normally quote a gross redemption yield after all charges but before tax has been deducted. Funds mainly made up of equities normally quote a yield representing the estimated annual payout net of tax for basic rate taxpayer. For further information contact the management company.

Historic pricing: The letter H denotes that the managers/operators will normally deal on the price set at the most recent valuation. The prices shown are the latest available before publication and may not be the current dealing levels because of an intervening portfolio revaluation or a switch to a forward pricing basis. The managers/operators must deal at a forward price on request, and may move to forward pricing at any time. Forward pricing: The letter F denotes that that managers/operators deal at the price to be set at the next valuation. Investors can be given no definite price in advance of the purchase or sale being carried out. The prices appearing in the newspaper are the most recent provided by the managers/operators. Scheme particulars, prospectus, key features and reports: The most recent report, scheme particulars, prospectus and key features document may be obtained free of charge from fund managers/operators.

WSJ Pro
SPAC Churchill Capital to Combine With Clarivate Analytics 

By Colin Kellaher 
218 words
14 January 2019
17:13
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Blank-check company Churchill Capital Corp. on Monday said it agreed to merge with Clarivate Analytics, a subscription-based analytics provider carved out of Thomson Reuters Corp. in 2016, with private equity-backed Clarivate becoming a publicly traded company.

Churchill said Clarivate's owners will hold roughly 73.8% of the combined company upon completion of the transaction, which implies an initial enterprise value of about $4.2 billion.

Private-equity firms Onex Corp. and Baring Private Equity Asia bought Clarivate in 2016 from Thomson Reuters for $3.55 billion. Churchill said Onex, Baring and Clarivate management are retaining 100% of their equity, with Onex the majority owner.

Churchill, a special purpose acquisition company formed by former Citigroup Inc. investment-banking chief Michael Klein and former IHS Markit Ltd. Chairman and Chief Executive Jerre Stead, raised $690 million in an initial public offering in September.

Mr. Stead will serve as executive chairman of the combined company, while Clarivate's existing management team, led by Chief Executive Jay Nadler and Chief Financial Officer Richard Hanks, will continue to lead the business, Churchill said.

The company said it expects to complete the transaction in the second quarter.
